The Names al-Wahid and al-Ahad: Two Interpretations
The author introduces the two names and gives two interpretations of al-Wahid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two divine names al-Wahid and al-Ahad and the two possible interpretations of al-Wahid.

Core Idea
Al-Wahid and al-Ahad both point to God's oneness, but al-Wahid has two distinct meanings.

The author begins by presenting the ninth and tenth names from the divine names: al-Wahid (the One) and al-Ahad (the Unique). These names are supported by Quranic verses. He then clarifies that al-Wahid can be understood in two ways: negating multiplicity in essence, or negating a contrary and rival. This dual meaning sets the stage for deeper definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the Quranic verses and definitions come through.
2
Identify the two names and their verses
Notice that the author cites a different verse for each name: 'Your God is One God' for al-Wahid, and 'Say: He is God, the Unique' for al-Ahad.
3
Understand the two interpretations of al-Wahid
The passage states that al-Wahid can mean negating multiplicity in essence or negating a contrary and rival. Reflect on how these differ.
The first interpretation leads to definitions of absolute oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم التاسع والعاشر : الواحد : والأحد . قال تعالى : ( وإلهكم إله واحد ) وقال : (قل هو الله أحد) واعلم أن الواحد قد يراد به نفي الكثرة في ذاته ، وقد يراد به نفي الضد والند . أما الواحد بالتفسير الأول فقد ذكروا في تعريفه وجوهاً : الأول : إنه شيء لا ينقسم . وإنما قلنا : شيء. احترازاً عن المعدوم . وإنما قلنا : لا ينقسم . احترازاً عن قولنا : رجل واحد ، إنسان وثوب واحد فإنه يقبل القسمة . أما الواحد الحقيقي فإنه لا يقبل القسمة بوجه من الوجوه .
The ninth and tenth names: al-Wahid (the One) and al-Ahad (the Unique). He, exalted, says: 'And your God is One God' and says: 'Say: He is God, the Unique.' And know that al-Wahid may be intended to negate multiplicity in His essence, and may be intended to negate the contrary and the rival. As for al-Wahid according to the first interpretation, they have mentioned in its definition aspects: The first: that it is a thing that does not divide. And we only say 'thing' as a safeguard against non-existence. And we only say 'does not divide' as a safeguard against our saying 'one man', 'one human', 'one garment', for these admit division. As for the true one (al-wahid al-haqiqi), it does not admit division in any way.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage introduces the two divine names al-Wahid and al-Ahad with Quranic verses. It then clarifies that al-Wahid can be interpreted in two ways: negating multiplicity in essence, or negating a contrary and rival. Focusing on the first interpretation, the author presents three definitions. The first definition: al-Wahid is a thing that does not divide. He explains that the term 'thing' excludes non-existence, and 'does not divide' excludes ordinary 'one' objects like a man or garment, which are divisible. True oneness (al-wahid al-haqiqi) is absolutely indivisible, applicable only to God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Wahid only means 'one' in the numerical sense, like counting, without deeper theological nuance.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Wahid has at least two distinct theological meanings, both pointing to God's transcendence, not numerical counting.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Quranic verse is cited for the name al-Ahad?
A) 'And your God is One God'
B) 'Say: He is God, the Unique'
C) 'There is no god but Allah'
D) 'Allah is the Self-Sufficient'

Key Takeaway
Al-Wahid and al-Ahad are two distinct divine names that affirm God's oneness, with al-Wahid encompassing two theological interpretations: negation of multiplicity and negation of rivals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God's oneness is just a number—that He is 'one' rather than 'unique'? How does the distinction between al-Wahid and al-Ahad challenge your everyday understanding of God? This week, when you recite 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad', pause and reflect: are you affirming His absolute uniqueness, or just counting?
?
The two interpretations of al-Wahid—negating multiplicity and negating rivals—address both internal division and external competition. In your own life, do you treat God as if He is 'divided' (giving different parts of your heart to different loyalties) or as if He has 'rivals' (fearing people more than Him)? Which of these is your greater struggle today? Commit to one concrete action this week that acknowledges God as al-Wahid in that specific area.
?
You have just learned that al-Wahid can mean negating the contrary and the rival. Consider: is there any person, status, or possession you treat as a 'contrary' to God—something you obey even when it contradicts His command? Write down one such idol, and decide today how you will demote it from 'rival' to 'servant'.

The First Definition of al-Wahid: Absolute Indivisibility
The author gives the first definition of al-Wahid and explains its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first definition of al-Wahid as 'a thing that does not divide' and why it applies only to Allah.

Core Idea
True oneness is absolute indivisibility, which cannot be said of any created thing.

After introducing the two interpretations of al-Wahid, the author focuses on the first one—negation of multiplicity—and presents the first of three definitions. This definition states that al-Wahid is 'a thing that does not divide.' He carefully explains each word in the definition to avoid misunderstandings: 'thing' excludes non-existence, and 'does not divide' excludes ordinary objects that are one in name but divisible in reality. The true one (al-wahid al-haqiqi) is absolutely indivisible in every way.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its safeguards
Read the Arabic: 'إنه شيء لا ينقسم' followed by the explanations of 'شيء' and 'لا ينقسم'. Notice how each part is justified.
2
Understand the reason for 'thing'
The word 'thing' is used to exclude non-existence (al-ma'dum). Only existing entities can be called 'one' in this sense.
3
Understand the reason for 'does not divide'
The phrase 'does not divide' excludes ordinary objects like one man or one garment, which are divisible into parts. These are not truly one in the sense of absolute oneness.
4
Recognize the conclusion about true oneness
The passage concludes that the true one (al-wahid al-haqiqi) does not admit division in any way—this applies only to Allah.

Source Text

Key Takeaway
The first definition of al-Wahid establishes that Allah is absolutely indivisible, unlike any created 'one' that can be split into parts.

The Second Definition of the One: Impossible to Modify
The author presents the second definition of al-Wahid based on impossibility of modification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of al-Wahid (the One) that emphasizes the impossibility of adding or removing parts from God's essence, and why this distinguishes God from all created 'ones'.

Core Idea
God's oneness means His essence cannot have anything added or removed.

Some scholars offered a second definition of the true one (al-wahid al-haqiqi): that which cannot undergo 'placing' (wad') or 'removing' (raf'), i.e., modification by composition or decomposition. This definition guards against thinking that God's oneness is like the oneness of a human being, who can lose a limb and still be called 'one'. The passage concretely distinguishes the divine from the contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the author's wording. Then read the English translation.
Notice the contrast between 'one human' and 'the Truth' (al-Haqq).
2
Understand the terms 'al-wad'' and 'al-raf''
These terms refer to placing something into an essence (adding a part) or removing something from it (taking a part away).
The passage illustrates with the example of a human losing a hand.
3
Contrast the human example with the divine
A human is called 'one' but can lose a hand and still be one human. God's oneness is such that no part can be removed or added.
This shows God's essence is absolutely simple and immutable.
4
Reflect on why this matters
This definition safeguards the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih) by ruling out any composition in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : قال بعضهم : الواحد الحقيقي هو الذي لا يصح فيه الوضع والرفع بخلاف قولك : إنسان واحد : لأنك تقول : إنسان بلا يد . فإنه يصح رفع شيء منه والحق - سبحانه - أحدي الذات
The second: Some of them said: The true one (al-wahid al-haqiqi) is that in which placing (al-wad') and removing (al-raf') are not valid, unlike your saying 'one human,' because you say 'a human without a hand,' for it is valid to remove something from him. And al-Haqq (the Truth, God) is unique in essence (ahadi al-dhat).
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the second definition of al-Wahid from some scholars: the true one is that which cannot undergo placement or removal (wad' and raf'), meaning parts cannot be added or taken away. This contrasts with created 'one' objects like a human, from whom a hand can be removed. God is 'one of essence' (ahadi al-dhat), implying His essence is not composed of parts that could be altered.

Key Terms
الوضع
الرفع
أحدي الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's oneness is similar to the oneness of a human being, which can be modified.
Correction: God's essence is absolutely indivisible and immutable; nothing can be added or removed from it, unlike created 'ones' such as humans.

Checkpoint
What does 'al-wad' wa al-raf'' (placing and removing) refer to in the second definition?
A) Syntactic concepts in grammar.
B) The ability to add or remove parts from an essence.
C) Physical raising and lowering.
D) Theories of predestination.

Key Takeaway
God's essence is ahadi al-dhat—unique and indivisible—so it cannot be changed or broken down.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you truly grasp that God's essence cannot be added to or subtracted from—or do you treat His oneness as a mere number? How would your worship change if you lived in constant awareness that nothing can be joined to Him and nothing can be taken away from Him?
?
Consider a moment you tried to 'add' something to your faith—like an extra ritual or a personal rule—or 'remove' a difficult obligation. How does this definition of God's oneness challenge your tendency to modify your relationship with Him based on convenience?
?
If God is 'ahadi al-dhat', completely indivisible, then your entire being—every thought, action, and intention—is either wholly submitted to Him or not. Is there any part of your life you are keeping separate, thinking it doesn't affect your oneness with God? What would it cost you to bring that part fully under His exclusive authority?

Third Definition of al-Wahid and the Atom Objection
The author gives the third definition, then addresses an objection regarding the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third definition of 'the one' as 'that which is not a number,' the objection that this would imply deficiency in God, and the beginning of the response that distinguishes God's oneness from the atom's relative smallness.

Core Idea
The one is not a number; the atom is a true one, but God's oneness is incomparable.

In this passage, the author presents a mathematical definition of 'the one' as that which is not a number, identifying the indivisible atom as an instance of such a one. However, an objection arises: this definition would make God the smallest being, which is impossible. The author begins his response by explaining that the atom's smallness is relative to isolation, hinting at God's absolute transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the passage once through to get a sense of the argument. Note the mathematical definition of 'al-Wahid' as 'not a number' and the explanation involving extremes.
The definition uses arithmetic: the smallest number (2) has two extremes (1 and 3), while the one has only one extreme.
2
Understand the mathematical reasoning
The author shows that two is a number because half the sum of its extremes (1 and 3) equals 2. The one has only one extreme, so it cannot be a number. Grasp this logic before moving to the objection.
This definition of number is specific to this context; it is not the only way to define number.
3
Identify the objection
The objection states that this definition implies that God would be the smallest of the small, like the indivisible atom, which is a deficiency impossible for God. Recognize why this is a problem for the definition.
The objection assumes that if God is 'one' in this sense, His oneness would be like the atom's—minimal and lacking.
4
Follow the beginning of the answer
The answer begins by explaining that the atom is only described as small when isolated; when it connects with others, it becomes great. The answer is cut off, but the direction is clear: God is not subject to such combination or isolation.
The text ends mid-sentence, so we only have the start of the response. Reviewers may need to complete the thought.
5
Reflect on the incompleteness
Consider how the author would likely complete the argument: since God cannot be combined or isolated, His oneness is not relative like the atom's. This preserves God's transcendence.
The key point is that God's oneness is absolute, not subject to increase or decrease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قال بعضهم : الواحد ما لا يكون عدداً والعدد ما كان نصف مجموع حاشيته وأقل العدد اثنان ، وله حاشيتان ، الواحد والثلاثة ومجموعهما أربعة ونصفهما إثنان ، فعلمنا أن الاثنين عدد ، وأما الواحد فليس له إلا حاشية واحدة ، فلم يكن عدداً . واعلم أن الجوهر الفرد عند من يقول به : واحد حقيقي بهذا التفسير. فإن قيل : الواحد بهذا التفسير مشعر بأنه أقل القليل كما في حق الجوهر الفرد، وذلك نقص ، وهو على الله محال . قلنا : كون الجوهر الفرد موصوفاً بالصغر والقلة إنما كان من حيث أنه يصح عليه أن يماسه غيره ويتصل به غيره ، فيعظم ويكبر . فإذا انفرد قيل : إنه صغير وحقير ، وإذا ماسه واتصل به غيره . قيل : أنه كبير وكثير ، فلما كان وصف
The third: Some of them said: Al-Wahid (the one) is that which is not a number, and a number is that which is half of the sum of its two extremes. And the smallest number is two, which has two extremes, one and three, and their sum is four, and half of it is two. So we know that two is a number. As for the one, it has only one extreme, so it is not a number. And know that the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), according to those who affirm it, is a true one (wahid haqiqi) according to this interpretation. If it is said: The one according to this interpretation implies that it is the smallest of the small, as in the case of the indivisible atom, and that is a deficiency, and it is impossible for God. We say: The fact that the indivisible atom is described as small and little is only because it is possible for another to touch it and connect with it, so it becomes great and large. When it is isolated, it is said to be small and insignificant, and when another touches and connects with it, it is said to be great and much. Since the description
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage gives the third definition of al-Wahid: it is not a number, defined mathematically as having only one extreme, unlike the number two which has two extremes. The indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is a true one in this sense. An objection arises: this would imply God is the smallest being, which is impossible. The answer explains that the atom is only considered small when isolated, but when combined with others it becomes large; God is not subject to such changes—He is not an atom that can be combined. The uniqueness of God is absolute, not relative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that defining God as 'one' in the same sense as the indivisible atom would imply that God is small and deficient.
Correction: The author shows that the atom's smallness is relative to its isolation; God is not subject to such physical relations, so His oneness is not a deficiency.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that the third definition makes God like the smallest indivisible atom?
A) By denying that the atom is truly one.
B) By explaining that the atom's smallness is relative to isolation, and God is not subject to combination.
C) By stating that God is also small in a metaphorical sense.
D) By rejecting the third definition.

Key Takeaway
The third definition of al-Wahid as 'not a number' is mathematically precise, but the objection shows that God's oneness is not like the atom's relative smallness; God transcends such comparisons.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously reduced God's oneness to a mere mathematical or conceptual unity, making Him fit into your mental categories like any other 'one'? What does this passage reveal about the danger of such comparisons?
?
When you reflect on God's attribute of 'One,' do you really believe He is incomparable to any created unity, including the smallest physical objects? Examine your heart: Are there remnants of thinking of God as 'small' or limited by your understanding?
?
The atom is described as small only in isolation. Today, commit to remembering that God's oneness is absolute and not dependent on any relation or contrast. Will you deliberately affirm His transcendence every time you recite 'Allahu Ahad'?

The Deniers' Objection: Multiplicity and Polytheism
Presents the claim of nufat al-sifat that affirming attributes contradicts tawhid and leads to polytheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key arguments used by the deniers of attributes to claim that affirming attributes leads to polytheism.

Core Idea
Affirming many attributes with the essence appears to create many gods — but appearances are not proofs.

This passage presents the case of those who deny that Allah has eternal attributes. They raise two objections: first, that multiple attributes alongside the essence would mean many things, not one; second, that affirming eight attributes leads to belief in nine gods, which is even worse than the Christian trinity. As you read, remember that this is the objection — the author will refute it later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two arguments
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that the passage presents two separate claims: one about multiplicity and one about the analogy to the Trinity.
2
Understand the logic of each argument
For the first argument, ask: why does multiplicity contradict tawhid? For the second, why does the analogy to the Trinity make it seem worse?
Remember that these are presented as claims, not refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن نفاة الصفات زعموا أن من أثبت الصفات لله ـ تعالى ـ فإنـه لا يمكنه أن يقول بوحدانية الله تعالى ، وذلك لأنا إذا حكمنا بقيام الصفات الكثيرة بذات الله - تعالى - كان مجموع الذات والصفات أشياء كثيرة لا شيئاً واحداً ، ويصح أيضاً فيه معنى الوضع والرفع مثل أن يقال : قادر وليس بعالم إلى أن يثبت بالدليل بطلان ذلك وزعم بعضهم : أن من أثبت لله تعالى صفات ثمانية فقد قال بتاسع تسعة ثم إن الله تعالى قال : ( لقد كفر الذين قالوا : إن الله ثالث ثلاثة )) ولما كان القائل بالثلاثة كافراً ، كان القائل بالتسعة ـ وهو قائل بالثلاثة ثلاث مرات - أدخل في الكفر
Know that the deniers of attributes claimed that whoever affirms attributes for Allah - exalted is He - cannot affirm the oneness of Allah, because if we judge that multiple attributes subsist in the essence of Allah, then the totality of the essence and attributes would be many things, not one thing, and also the meaning of setting and raising is valid in it, such as it being said: powerful but not knowledgeable, until it is proven by evidence that this is false. And some of them claimed that whoever affirms for Allah eight attributes has said nine gods, and then Allah said: 'They have disbelieved who say that Allah is the third of three.' Since the one who said three is a disbeliever, then the one who said nine - and he is the one who said three three times - is more deeply in disbelief.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the nufat al-sifat (deniers of attributes) against the Ash'ari view that Allah has eternal attributes. First, they argue that affirming multiple attributes alongside the essence results in multiplicity, contradicting absolute oneness. They claim that if the essence and attributes together form multiple things, then it would be possible to say 'powerful but not knowledgeable' until that is disproven. Second, they analogize: if affirming three persons (as Christians do) is polytheism, then affirming eight attributes plus the essence (nine) is even worse. The author presents these claims without immediate refutation, indicating that they will be disproven later.

Key Terms
نفاة الصفات
الصفات
وحدانية
ذات
تاسع تسعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some readers may think that the author of the text agrees with the deniers' arguments.
Correction: The passage is presenting the objection of a rival group (nufat al-sifat) to be refuted later. The author does not endorse these claims.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument of the nufat al-sifat against those who affirm attributes?
A) Attributes are not eternal.
B) Affirming multiple attributes with the essence leads to multiplicity.
C) Attributes are identical to the essence.
D) Attributes are created.

Key Takeaway
The deniers of attributes argue that affirming multiple attributes leads to multiplicity (many things) and that believing in eight attributes plus the essence is akin to polytheism, even worse than the Trinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The deniers claim that affirming attributes leads to thinking of many gods. When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing, All-Powerful,' do you ever struggle to keep these attributes unified in your mind? What do you do to remind yourself that His attributes are not separate beings?
?
In a discussion with someone who accuses Muslims of believing in many attributes, do you have the evidence from the Quran and reason to respond? If not, what step will you take this week to prepare?
?
This lesson presents an objection without immediate refutation. How patient are you when you hear a strong argument against your faith? Will you seek knowledge to answer it, or will you let it plant doubt?

5 min
The Second Meaning of 'One' and the Deniers' Objection
Defines the second interpretation of divine oneness and presents the denial of attributes based on pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation of divine oneness and how the deniers of attributes misapply it.

Core Idea
Allah's oneness means nothing shares His necessary existence, knowledge, or power.

After establishing that Allah is one in essence and attributes, the author now presents a deeper meaning: nothing in existence equals Allah in His essential attributes. This leads to the objection of those who deny divine attributes, claiming that affirming eternal attributes contradicts oneness. In this lesson, we examine both the correct definition and the flawed reasoning of the deniers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its structure. Then read the translation.
2
Understand the three unique aspects
Notice that the passage lists three ways nothing equals Allah: necessary existence, knowledge, and power. These are essential attributes.
This is the second interpretation of 'one'.
3
Analyze the deniers' claim
The deniers twist the meaning: they say oneness means uniqueness in pre-eternity, and then accuse attribute-affirmers of having multiple eternal entities.
Contrast their claim with the correct meaning.
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
Ask yourself: Why is the deniers' objection invalid? Because they redefine oneness in a way that excludes attributes, while the correct definition includes them.
This guards against a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الواحد بالتفسير الثاني ، فهو أنه ليس في الوجود موجود يساويه ، في وجوب الوجود بالذات، ولا موجود يساويه في العلم بجميع المعلومات، التي لا نهاية لها ، ولا موجود يساويه في القدرة على جميع الممكنات . وزعم نفاة الصفات : أنه تعالى واحد بمعنى أنه ليس في الوجود موجود يساويه في القدم والأزلية ، بخلاف مثبقي الصفات فإنهم أثبتوا موجودات قديمة أزلية .
As for 'one' in the second interpretation, it is that there is no existent in existence equal to Him in the necessity of existence by essence, nor an existent equal to Him in knowledge of all infinite known things, nor an existent equal to Him in power over all possible things. And the deniers of attributes claimed that He is one in the sense that there is no existent in existence equal to Him in pre-eternity and beginninglessness, in contrast to the affirmers of attributes, for they affirmed pre-eternal, beginningless existents.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage defines the true meaning of divine oneness: nothing shares Allah's necessary existence, omniscience, and omnipotence. Then it presents the objection of the deniers of attributes (nufat al-sifat), who claim that if attributes are eternal, then there would be multiple eternal entities, violating the meaning of oneness as uniqueness in pre-eternity. The passage thus contrasts the correct understanding of oneness with the false claim of the deniers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the second interpretation of oneness is exclusive to the deniers or that it means uniqueness only in pre-eternity.
Correction: The correct interpretation is that nothing shares Allah's necessary existence, omniscience, and omnipotence. The deniers' claim that oneness means only uniqueness in pre-eternity is a misapplication that leads to denying attributes.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second meaning of divine oneness is that nothing shares Allah's necessary existence, all-encompassing knowledge, and absolute power; the deniers' reduction to mere pre-eternity is a distortion.

?

?

?
Do you functionally reduce Allah's oneness to mere belief that He is eternal, while living as if your own power controls your outcomes? What would change if you truly internalized that nothing equals Him in power over all possible things?

The Threefold Oneness of Allah
Sanusi classifies divine oneness into three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine oneness is classified into oneness of essence, attributes, and actions, and the meaning of each.

Core Idea
Allah's oneness means no composition, no equal, and no partner in creation.

Sanusi presents a foundational classification of tawhid: oneness in essence, attributes, and actions. Each type addresses a possible misconception about multiplicity in God. This passage lays out the three categories with clear definitions, establishing that Allah is absolutely unique in every respect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the threefold structure signalled by 'أما أنه واحد في ذاته...' and 'وأما أنه واحد في صفاته...' and 'وأما إنه واحد في أفعاله...'.
2
Identify the three categories
Identify the three types of oneness: essence, attributes, actions. For each, write down the key point Sanusi makes.
Essence: no composition. Attributes: no equal. Actions: sole initiator.
3
Understand the explanations
For each category, understand what is being negated. Oneness of essence negates composition (physical and logical). Oneness of attributes negates any equal in existence, knowledge, power, and independence. Oneness of actions negates any other ultimate cause.
4
Reflect on importance
Ask yourself: why is it necessary to distinguish these three aspects? How would failing to affirm any one of them lead to shirk?
Each protects a different dimension of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الأول : اعلم أنه تعالى واحد في ذاته ، وواحد في صفاته ، وواحد في أفعاله، أما أنه واحد في ذاته فلان ذاته منزهة عن جهات التركيبات ، لا من التركيبات المقدارية الحسية كما في الجسم ، ولا من التركيبات العقلية كما في النوع المركب من الجنس والفصل ، وأما أنه واحد في صفاته فهو أنه ليس في الوجود موجود آخر يساويه في الوجود بالذات ، وفي العلم بكل المعلومات ، وفي القدرة على كل الممكنات ، وفي الغني عن كل ما سواه . وأما إنه واحد في أفعاله فهو أنه ليس في الوجود موجود يكون مبدئاً لجميع الممكنات إما بغير واسطة ، وإما بواسطة إلا هو .
The first branch: Know that He is one in His essence, one in His attributes, and one in His actions. As for His being one in His essence, it is because His essence is free from all aspects of composition, neither from quantitative sensible composition as with a body, nor from intellectual composition as with a species composed of genus and differentia. As for His being one in His attributes, it is that there is no other existent in existence equal to Him in existence by essence, in knowledge of all known things, in power over all possible things, and in independence from everything other than Him. As for His being one in His actions, it is that there is no existent in existence that is the initiator of all possible things, either directly or indirectly, except Him.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage classifies divine oneness into three categories. First, oneness of essence: Allah's essence is simple, free from both physical composition (like a body) and logical composition (like genus and species). Second, oneness of attributes: no being shares His necessary existence, omniscience, omnipotence, and self-sufficiency. Third, oneness of actions: He alone is the ultimate cause of all possible things, whether directly or through intermediaries. This triple distinction refutes any notion of multiplicity in the divine.

Key Terms

واحد في الصفات
واحد في الأفعال
التراكيب المقدارية
التراكيب العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'one in attributes' means Allah's attributes are identical to creation or that He shares attributes with others.
Correction: The passage clarifies it means uniqueness: no one equals Him in existence, knowledge, power, and independence.

Checkpoint
What does 'one in essence' mean according to this passage?
A) His essence has parts.
B) His essence is simple with no composition.
C) His essence is like other essences.
D) His essence is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Allah is one in essence (simple), one in attributes (unique), and one in actions (sole creator).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Allah's essence has no parts. In your worship, do you ever ‘compartmentalize’ your devotion—giving some parts of your life to Him and holding back others? What single area of your life have you not yet submitted to His complete oneness? What step will you take this week to surrender it?

God's Oneness in His Actions
Sanusi defines God's oneness in actions as exclusive origination of all possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's oneness in His actions means He alone is the origin of all possible things, directly or indirectly.

Core Idea
God alone originates all that exists.

In this passage, the author explains a key aspect of tawheed: that God is unique in His creative action. He states that no other being is the origin of all possible things, whether directly or through intermediaries. This clarifies that all causation ultimately traces back to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the brevity and precision of the definition.
The passage is a single sentence, so every phrase matters.
2
Identify the key terms
Spot the terms 'مبدئ' (origin) and 'ممكنات' (possible things). Understand how they define the scope of God's exclusive action.
'مبدئ' is not just 'creator' but 'source/originator'.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the exclusion of intermediaries is important. Does this deny secondary causation? How does it guard against shirk?
Intermediaries like angels or prophets do not act independently.

Source Text

وأما إنه واحد في أفعاله فهو أنه ليس في الوجود موجود يكون مبدئاً لجميع الممكنات إما بغير واسطة وإما بواسطة إلا هو.
As for His being one in His actions, it is that there is no existent in existence that is the origin of all possible things, either without an intermediary or with an intermediary, except Him.

This affirms that God alone is the creator and sustainer of everything, and no other entity shares in this role.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Three Stations of Travellers in Surah al-Ikhlas
Explains the spiritual significances of the three divine names in 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three divine names in 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' correspond to three spiritual stations of travellers towards Allah.

Core Idea
The words of Surah al-Ikhlas are tailored to three spiritual levels.

This passage from al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliyah explores the deeper spiritual meanings of the verse Qul Huwa Allāhu Aḥad. It reveals that each of the three divine names—Huwa, Allāh, and Aḥad—addresses a different type of traveller, from the highest gnostic who sees only Allah to the weakest who may entertain polytheism. This lesson will unpack these three stations and their significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three stations
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the passage explicitly names three stations: the near ones (muqarrabūn), the people of the right (aṣḥāb al-yamīn), and the people of the left (aṣḥāb al-shimāl).
The passage uses the word 'maqām' for each station.
2
Understand the first station: muqarrabūn
The highest station is for those who see only God's existence. They perceive the contingency of all else and thus need no distinguishing descriptor—'Huwa' alone suffices. Focus on why the word 'Huwa' is an absolute reference for them.
Their insight is based on the principle that possible things without a cause are pure non-existence.
3
Understand the second station: aṣḥāb al-yamīn
These travellers see other existents alongside God, so they need a distinguishing word. Hence 'Allāh' is added to 'Huwa'. Notice how the passage explains the need for a descriptor due to multiplicity.
Their station is lower because they still perceive multiplicity.
4
Understand the third station: aṣḥāb al-shimāl
The weakest station is for those who may entertain the possibility of multiple gods. For them, the full phrase 'Huwa Allahu Aḥad' is needed to explicitly negate shirk and affirm tawḥīd. Observe how each station's need determines the divine words used.
This shows the Quran's perfect tailoring to different spiritual capacities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثالث : إن قوله تعالى : (قل هو الله أحد ) فيه ألفاظ ثلاثة من أسماء الله. وكل واحد منها إشارة إلى مقام من مقامات السائرين إلى الله . فالمقام الأول مقام المقربين وهو أعلى المقامات . وهؤلاء هم الذين نظروا إلى ماهيات الأشياء وحقائقها من حيث هي هي ، فلا جرم ما رأوا موجوداً سوى الله ، لأن الحق واجب الوجود لذاته ، وما سواه ممكن والممكن إذا أخذ مع عدم السبب كان عدماً محضاً . فهؤلاء ما رأوا موجوداً سوى الله - سبحانه - وقوله « هو » إشارة مطلقة ، والإشارة المطلقة تتعين بشرط أن يكون المشار إليه واحداً فقط . فلا جرم كان قولنا : هو » إشارة إلى الحق - سبحانه - ولم يفتقروا مع هذه اللفظة إلى ذكر صفة لذلك المشار إليه ، لأن الافتقار إلى المميز إنما يحصل حيث حصل هناك موجودات وقد بينا أن هؤلاء لم يشاهدوا بعيون عقولهم إلا موجوداً واحداً ، فأدى افتقارهم إلى ذكر المميز ، فلهذا السبب كانت لفظة ( هو ) كافية في حصول العرفان التام لهؤلاء المقربين . وأما المقام الثاني - فهو دون المقام الأول - وهو مقام أصحاب اليمين ، وذلك لأنهم شاهدوا مع الحق موجوداً فحصلت في عقولهم كثرة في الموجودات ، فكلمة ( هو ) غير كافية في حق هؤلاء ، بل احتاجوا إلى صفة لكلمة « هو » باعتبارها يمتاز الحق عن غيره ، فقرن بلفظة « هو » لفظة ( الله )، فقيل مع هؤلاء : ( هو الله ) والمقام الثالث : وهو مقام أصحاب الشمال ، وهو أضعف المقامات ، وهم الذين يجوزون أن يكون الإله أكثر من واحد فقيل لأجلهم : ( هو الله أحد ) إبطالاً للشرك وتصريحاً بالتوحيد .
The third branch: Verily the saying of the Exalted: 'Say: He is Allah, the One (Aḥad)' contains three words from the names of Allah. Each one of them is an indication to a station (maqām) among the stations of the travellers to Allah. So the first station is the station of the near ones (al-muqarrabīn), and it is the highest of stations. These are those who looked at the quiddities of things and their realities in themselves, so inevitably they did not see any existent besides Allah, because the Truth is necessary of existence by itself, and other than Him is possible, and the possible when taken with the absence of the cause is pure non-existence. So these did not see any existent besides Allah - exalted - and His saying 'Huwa' is an absolute reference, and the absolute reference is determined by the condition that the referent is only one. So inevitably our saying 'Huwa' is a reference to the Truth - exalted - and they did not need with this word to mention a description of that referent, because the need for a distinguisher only occurs where there are existents, and we have clarified that these [people] did not behold with the eyes of their intellects except one existent, so they did not need to mention a distinguisher. For this reason the word 'Huwa' was sufficient for attaining complete gnosis for these near ones. As for the second station - it is below the first station - and it is the station of the people of the right (aṣḥāb al-yamīn). That is because they saw an existent with the Truth, so multiplicity of existents occurred in their minds, so the word 'Huwa' is not sufficient for these, rather they needed a description for the word 'Huwa' by which the Truth is distinguished from other than Him. So the word 'Allah' was coupled with the word 'Huwa', and it was said to these: 'Huwa Allah'. And the third station is the station of the people of the left (aṣḥāb al-shimāl), and it is the weakest of stations, and they are those who permit of there being more than one god, so it was said for their sake: 'Huwa Allahu Aḥad' to nullify associationism and to declare tawḥīd clearly.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is the third branch that draws spiritual lessons from the three divine names in Surah al-Ikhlāṣ. It divides the travellers to Allah into three stations (maqāmāt). The highest station is that of the muqarrabūn (the near ones), who see only God's existence because they contemplate the quiddities of things and realize that contingent things are pure non-existence without a cause. For them, the word 'Huwa' (He) alone suffices as a reference to God without any descriptive adjective. The second station is that of aṣḥāb al-yamīn (the people of the right), who perceive other existents alongside God, so they need a descriptive word like 'Allāh' to distinguish the referent. Thus they say 'Huwa Allah'. The third station is that of aṣḥāb al-shimāl (the people of the left), who are weak and may entertain polytheism, so they are explicitly told 'Huwa Allahu Aḥad' to negate shirk and affirm tawḥīd.

Key Terms
مقام
مقربون
أصحاب اليمين
أصحاب الشمال
إشارة مطلقة
عرفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The words of the verse 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' are arbitrarily chosen and have no particular relevance to the spiritual state of the listener.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that each divine name addresses a specific level of spiritual realization: 'Huwa' for those who see only Allah, 'Allāh' as a distinguisher for those who see multiplicity, and 'Aḥad' to negate polytheism for the weakest. The words are precisely tailored.

Checkpoint

C) The station of the near ones (muqarrabūn)
D) All stations

Key Takeaway
The divine names in Surah al-Ikhlas are not arbitrary but correspond precisely to the spiritual levels of the believers, guiding each according to their understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you recite 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad', which word resonates most with your soul? Does that reveal something about the level of your spiritual vision?
?
If you find yourself in the second or third station, what steps can you take today to purify your gaze so that you see only one Existent? What attachments are still making you see multiplicity?

Classification of Divine Attributes: Relative and Negative
Author classifies divine attributes into relative and negative and links them to the names 'Allah' and 'Ahad'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine attributes are classified into relative and negative categories, and how the names 'Allah' and 'Ahad' comprehensively indicate each category.

Core Idea
The name 'Allah' affirms all perfections; 'Ahad' negates all imperfections.

This passage from the author's discussion on divine attributes presents a foundational classification. He divides attributes into two types: relative (affirmative) and negative (transcendental). He then shows that the name 'Allah' encompasses all relative attributes, while 'Ahad' encompasses all negative attributes, providing a proof for why 'Ahad' implies absolute oneness and negation of multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the author's structure come through.
Notice the two categories and the proof for 'Ahad'.
2
Identify the two attribute categories
Find the definitions and examples given for relative and negative attributes.
Relative: knowing, powerful. Negative: not a body, not a substance.
3
Understand why 'Allah' indicates relative
The author explains that deserving worship requires complete power and wisdom, so 'Allah' implies all relative attributes.
4
Analyze the proof for 'Ahad'
Study the argument: if two necessary beings existed, they would be composite, contradicting oneness. This shows 'Ahad' negates all multiplicity.
This proof is a key rational argument in tawhid.
5
Reflect on the theological import
Consider how this classification shapes our understanding of God's names and attributes.
The names are not arbitrary; they have deep meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا بحث آخر على ما تقدم، أن صفات الله تعالى إما أن تكون إضافية ، أو سلبية أما الإضافية فكقولنا عالم ، قادر ، خالق ، وأما السلبية فكقولنا : ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر . والمخلوقات تدل على النوع الأول من الصفات أولاً ، وعلى النوع الثاني ثانياً . فقولنا : « الله ، يدل على جميع الصفات الإضافية المعتبرة في الإلمية. وقولنا و أحد ) . يدل على جميع السلبية . وذلك لأن قولنا : « الله ، معناه : أنه الذي يستحق العبادة ، واستحقاق العبادة لا يكون إلا لمن يكون مستقلاً بالإيجاد والإبداع وذلك لا يحصل ، إلا لمن كان موصوفاً بالقدرة التامة والحكمة التامة . وأما الأحدية دالة على كونه منزهاً عن التركيبات الحسية والعقلية . وهذا يدل على أنه ليس ( بجسم ولا متحيز ) لأن كل متحيز فهو منقسم ، وإذا لم يكن متحيزاً في شيء من الأحياز والجهات ، وجب أن لا يكون حالا في شيء ، ولا محلاً لشيء . وأيضاً : إذا كان أحداً ، لم يكن له مثل ولا ضد ولاند ، لأنه لو حصل في الوجود موجودان ، كل واحد منهما واجب لذاته لكانا متشاركين في الوجود ومختلفين في التعين ، وما به المشاركة غير ما بنه المخالفة ، فيكون كل واحد منهما مركباً في ذاته من هذين القيدين ، فيثبت أن كونه تعالى أحداً ، يدل على جميع الصفات السلبية
And here is another discussion following what has preceded: that the attributes of Allah, exalted is He, are either relative (idafiyya) or negative (salbiyya). As for the relative, like our saying 'knowing', 'powerful', 'creator'. As for the negative, like our saying 'not a body' and 'not a substance'. The created things indicate the first type of attributes primarily, and the second type secondarily. So our saying 'Allah' indicates all the relative attributes considered in divinity. And our saying 'Ahad' indicates all the negative attributes. That is because our saying 'Allah' means that He is the One who deserves worship, and deserving worship is only for one who is independent in origination and creation, and that is not attained except for one described with complete power and complete wisdom. As for the oneness (al-ahadiyya), it indicates that He is transcendent above sensory and intellectual compositions. This indicates that He is not a body nor spatially extended, because everything spatially extended is divisible, and if He is not spatially extended in any of the spaces and directions, it is necessary that He is not indwelling in anything, nor a locus for anything. Also, if He is One, He has no equal, no opposite, and no rival, because if there existed two existents, each necessary in itself, they would share in existence and differ in individuation, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so each would be composite in itself from these two considerations, thus it is established that His being, exalted is He, 'Ahad' indicates all the negative attributes.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes into two types: relative (idafiyya) and negative (salbiyya). The author states that the name 'Allah' indicates all relative attributes, because 'Allah' means 'the One deserving worship', which requires complete power and wisdom (affirmative attributes). The name 'Ahad' indicates all negative attributes, because oneness entails transcendence above composition, both sensory (like having parts) and intellectual (like having attributes distinct from essence). The author then elaborates: being 'Ahad' means not a body, not spatially extended (since extension implies divisibility), not indwelling in anything, not a locus, and having no equal, opposite, or rival. A proof is given: if two necessary beings existed, they would share in existence and differ in individuation, making each composite—which contradicts absolute oneness. Thus, the name 'Ahad' comprehensively negates all imperfections and multiplicity.

Key Terms
صفات إضافية
صفات سلبية
أحد
الله
أحدية
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Ahad' only negates polytheism (multiple gods).
Correction: The author shows 'Ahad' negates all forms of composition, spatial extension, and multiplicity, including any internal parts or external equals.

Checkpoint
What do the names 'Allah' and 'Ahad' primarily indicate according to this passage?
A) 'Allah' indicates negative attributes, 'Ahad' indicates relative attributes
B) 'Allah' indicates relative attributes, 'Ahad' indicates negative attributes
C) Both indicate only relative attributes
D) Both indicate only negative attributes

Key Takeaway
The names 'Allah' and 'Ahad' comprehensively cover all divine attributes: 'Allah' affirms perfections, 'Ahad' negates all imperfections.

?

Classification of Divine Attributes: Additional and Negative
Classification of attributes into إضافية and سلبية with linkage to names الله and أحد.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between additional and negative attributes and how the names 'Allah' and 'Ahad' respectively indicate them.

Core Idea
God's names 'Allah' and 'Ahad' summarize all positive and negative attributes.

In this passage, the author introduces a fundamental classification of divine attributes into two categories: additional (those affirming perfection) and negative (those negating imperfection). He then shows how the two most important divine names, 'Allah' and 'Ahad', encapsulate these categories. This lays the groundwork for understanding how we speak about God in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's classification and arguments come through.
Notice the two main categories right at the beginning.
2
Identify the two types of attributes
The passage defines additional attributes (like knowing, powerful) and negative attributes (like not a body). Notice how created things indicate these types.
Created things primarily indicate additional attributes.
3
Understand the link to divine names
The author connects 'Allah' to all additional attributes and 'Ahad' to all negative attributes. Follow the reasoning for each connection.
'Allah' means the one worthy of worship, requiring perfect power and wisdom.
4
Examine the arguments for negative attributes
The passage provides detailed arguments under 'Ahad': not a body, not occupying space, no like/opposite/rival, and the impossibility of two necessary existents.
These arguments reinforce the scope of negative attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات إضافية، أو سلبية. أما الإضافية فكقولنا: عالم، قادر، خالق(١)، وأما السلبية فكقولنا: ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر. والمخلوقات تدل على النوع الأول من الصفات أولاً، وعلى النوع الثاني ثانياً. فقولنا: «الله» يدل على جميع الصفات الإضافية المعتبرة في الإلمية. وقولنا: «أحد» يدل على جميع السلبية. وذلك لأن قولنا: «الله» معناه: أنه الذي يستحق العبادة، واستحقاق العبادة لا يكون إلا لمن يكون مستقلاً بالإيجاد والإبداع وذلك لا يحصل إلا لمن كان موصوفاً بالقدرة التامة والحكمة التامة(٢). وأما الأحدية دالة على كونه منزهاً عن التركيبات الحسية والعقلية. وهذا يدل على أنه ليس بجسم ولا متحيز(٣) لأن كل متحيز فهو منقسم، وإذا لم يكن متحيزاً في شيء من الأحياز والجهات، وجب أن لا يكون حالا في شيء، ولا محلاً لشيء. وأيضاً: إذا كان أحداً، لم يكن له مثل ولا ضد ولا ند، لأنه لو حصل في الوجود موجودان، كل واحد منهما واجب لذاته لكانا متشاركين(٤) في الوجود ومختلفين في التعين، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة، فيكون كل واحد منهما مركباً في ذاته من هذين القيدين، فيثبت أن كونه تعالى أحداً يدل على جميع الصفات السلبية.
The attributes are either additional or negative. As for the additional, such as our saying: knowing, powerful, creator. As for the negative, such as our saying: not a body, not a substance. And created things indicate the first type of attributes primarily, and the second type secondarily. So our saying 'Allah' indicates all the additional attributes considered in divinity. And our saying 'Ahad' indicates all the negative attributes. That is because our saying 'Allah' means: He who is worthy of worship, and worthiness of worship is only for one who is independent in bringing into existence and originating, and that is only attained by one who is described with perfect power and perfect wisdom. As for oneness (al-ahadiyya), it indicates His being transcendent above sensory and intellectual compositions. And this indicates that He is not a body nor an occupant of space, because every occupant of space is divisible. And if He is not an occupant of any spaces or directions, it necessarily follows that He is not indwelling in anything, nor a locus for anything. Also, if He is One, He has no like, no opposite, and no rival, because if there existed two existents, each necessary by itself, they would share in existence and differ in individuation, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so each of them would be composite in its essence from these two aspects. Thus it is established that His being, exalted, One indicates all the negative attributes.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines two types of divine attributes: additional (إضافية) and negative (سلبية). It then explains how the names 'Allah' and 'Ahad' indicate each type. The author begins by classifying attributes into those that affirm a positive quality (like knowing, powerful) and those that negate imperfections (like not a body). Created things point primarily to the additional attributes and secondarily to the negative. Then the argument connects the name 'Allah' to all additional attributes because it means 'the one deserving worship,' which requires perfect power and wisdom. The name 'Ahad' indicates all negative attributes because oneness implies transcendence above composition, both physical and intellectual. The author then elaborates: being neither a body nor an occupant of space, having no like, opposite, or rival, and refuting the possibility of two necessary existents, which would entail composition. The conclusion reaffirms that 'Ahad' encompasses all negative attributes.

The Meaning of As-Samad and Its Relation to As-Sayyid
Explaining that 'as-samad' means absolute independence and rejecting corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true meaning of the divine name 'as-Samad' and why it cannot imply corporeality.

Core Idea
As-Samad means absolute independence, not a solid body.

This lesson explains one of the most misunderstood divine names. The Qur'an describes Allah as 'as-Samad', but some have mistakenly taken it to mean a solid body. The author clarifies that as-Samad actually means complete independence from all composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: first linking Samad to Sayyid, then correcting a wrong interpretation.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find where the author gives the correct meaning (based on Sayyid) and where he mentions the incorrect interpretation (solid body).
3
Understand the refutation
Why does the author reject the solid-body interpretation? Because it implies composition and corporeality, which are impossible for Allah.
This connects to tanzih (transcendence).
4
Grasp the correct theological implication
The correct meaning is that Allah is free from all forms of composition. This affirms His absolute independence and uniqueness.

Source Text

كان هو السيد في الحقيقة ، فلهذا قال بعده : ( الله الصمد ) وأيضاً : قيل الصمد هو الجسم المصمت الذي لا جوف له ، وذلك في حق الله محال . فوجب أن يحمل ذلك في حقه تعالى على براءته عن جميع جهات التركيبات

Then it presents an incorrect interpretation—that al-Samad means a solid body without a cavity—and immediately rejects it as impossible for God. The correct interpretation, the author says, is that al-Samad means God's freedom from all forms of composition (tarkibat). This shows that the attribute must be understood in a transcendent sense.

The Proof That Ahad Implies Samad
A logical proof linking Ahad and Samad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of Samad (independence) serves as the proof for God's oneness (Ahad) in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
God's oneness is proven by His absolute independence from composition.

In this passage, the author presents a concise logical argument: the oneness of God is not merely asserted but demonstrated through His attribute of Samad, meaning He is free from all composition. This establishes a foundational link between two key divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the dialogue format. Notice the hypothetical question and answer.
This passage is very short — read it twice.
2
Identify the question and answer
The passage sets up a question: 'What is the proof that He is Ahad?' The answer is: 'Because He is Samad.'
The question is implied in the dialogue.
3
Understand the meaning of Samad
Samad here means 'free from all aspects of compositions' — i.e., God is not composed of parts.
4
Connect Ahad and Samad
Recognize that if God is free from composition, He cannot be multiple or divisible; He is necessarily One (Ahad).
This is the logical inference.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is important: it shows that divine attributes are interconnected and that oneness is rationally demonstrable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكأنه لما قال : إنه أحد قيل له : وما الدليل عليه ؟ قال : لأنه صمد بمعنى كونه بريئاً عن جميع جهات التركيبات
It is as if when He said that He is 'Ahad' (One), it was said to Him: 'What is the proof of that?' He said: 'Because He is Samad', meaning He is free from all aspects of compositions.

Commentary
This passage functions as a proof. It presents a logical dialogue: If someone asks for the proof that God is One (Ahad), the answer is that He is Samad, meaning free from composition. This demonstrates that oneness is proven by independence from composition.

Three Branches of Divine Oneness
Presenting three consequences of tawhid: no offspring, not begotten, no equal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three consequences of tawhid regarding divine nature: God has no offspring, is not begotten, and has no equal.

Core Idea
Tawhid demands not only belief in one God, but also rejection of offspring, being begotten, and any equal.

Having established that Allah is al-Samad (the Eternal, Self-Sufficient), the author now draws out three necessary implications. These branches protect the absolute uniqueness of Allah by negating any form of association or composition. In this passage, the author systematically refutes any notion of offspring, being generated, or having a peer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the author uses the phrase 'واعلم أنه سبحانه لما بين ذلك' which connects this to the previous discussion on al-Samad.
2
Identify the three branches
The passage enumerates three branches (فروع). Find each one and note how they are introduced: 'فالأول', 'والفرع الثاني', 'والفرع الثالث'.
Each branch is a negation: no offspring, not begotten, no equal.
3
Understand the reasoning
For each branch, the author gives a reason. First branch: having a child requires parts and generation, impossible for God. Second: being master implies independence. Third: two necessary existents would lead to composition.
The third branch includes a proof: if there were two necessary existents, they would share necessity but differ in individuation, making each composite.
4
Reflect on deeper meaning
Note that 'lam yalid' also negates any necessitative causality, not just literal offspring. Reflect on how these negations uphold tawhid.
This connects to the earlier discussion of al-Samad.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the third branch's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه سبحانه لما بين ذلك فرع على هذا الأصل ثلاثة أنواع من الفروع : فالأول : أنه ليس له ولد ، لأن الولد عبارة عما ينفصل من الشيء ، جزءاً من أجزائه ثم يتولد عن ذلك الجزء ما يماثل ذلك الكل . وهذا إنما يعقل في الشيء الذي يقبل الجزء والبعض وذلك في حق الله محال . وفيه تفسير آخر - على ما سبق - وهو : أن المؤثر إذا كان موجباً بالذات كان الأثر كالشيء المتولد منه . فقوله : ( لم يلد ) إشارة إلى نفي هذا المعنى ، أعني نفي أن يكون تأثيره في مخلوقاته على سبيل الإيجاب والتوليد . والفرع الثاني : أنه لم يتولد عن غيره ، وهو قوله : ( ولم يولد ) لأن كونه سيداً يقتضي استغناؤه عن كل ما سواه . والفرع الثالث : أنه ليس في الوجود أحد يساويه في صفاته ، فإنا بينا : أنه لو حصل في الوجود موجود آخر ، واجب الوجود لذاته ، لاشتركا في ذلك الوجوب ، ولاختلفا في ذلك التعيين (۱) ، فيلزم منه كون كل واحد منها مركباً . وذلك يقدح في كونه سيداً على الإطلاق
Know that when He, glory be to Him, made that clear, He branched from this principle three types of branches: First: that He has no child, because the child is an expression of something that separates from a thing, a part of its parts, then from that part there is generated that which resembles the whole. And this is only conceived in a thing that accepts parts and portions, and that is impossible with respect to God. And there is another interpretation—according to what preceded—which is: that the agent, if it is necessary by essence, the effect is like something generated from it. So His saying: (lam yalid – He did not beget) is an indication of negating this meaning, i.e., negating that His effect in His creatures is by way of necessity and generation. Second: that He did not beget from other than Him, which is His saying: (wa lam yulad – and He was not begotten) because His being Master (sayyid) necessitates His independence from everything other than Him. Third: that there is no one in existence equal to Him in His attributes, for we have shown: if there existed another existent, necessary of existence by itself, they would share in that necessity, and differ in that individuation, and that would necessitate that each of them is composite. And that invalidates His being absolutely Master.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a classification: it lists three consequences (furu') derived from the principle that God is al-Samad (free from composition). Each branch negates a type of association. The first denies offspring because having a child would require composition and generation, which is impossible for God. The second denies being begotten because being a master implies complete independence. The third denies any equal in existence, because the existence of two necessary existents would entail each being composite, which contradicts absolute mastery.

Key Terms
لم يلد
لم يولد
مساوي
واجب الوجود
تعيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'lam yalid' only negates literal begetting and not other forms of causal necessity.
Correction: The author explains that 'lam yalid' also negates any necessitative causal relationship, based on the earlier discussion of al-Samad.

Checkpoint
What is the third branch that derives from divine oneness?
A) God has no partners in worship.
B) There is no one equal to God in His attributes.
C) God is not composed of parts.
D) God is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The three negations—no offspring, no generation, no equal—protect the absolute uniqueness and independence of Allah, which is the core of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Divine Name Al-Wahid: Grammar and Interpretations
Interpreting 'wahid' (the Unique) as a divine name with three explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical basis for considering 'al-Wahid' as a divine name and its three theological interpretations.

Core Idea
'Al-Wahid' is a divine name rooted in a Quranic verse's grammatical possibility.

This lesson examines how a single Quranic phrase can be read as applying to Allah, yielding a divine name. We will explore the grammatical argument and then unpack the three meanings of this name as explained by the author.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the grammatical analysis of the word 'wahidan' and the two possibilities mentioned.
Look at the accusative case and the term 'hal' (circumstantial accusative).
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage says 'wahidan' can be a circumstantial accusative from the Creator or from the creature. Understand why the first option makes 'al-Wahid' a divine name.
3
Examine the three interpretations
List the three meanings of 'al-Wahid' as a divine name: pre-eternal existence, absolute independence, and uniqueness in perfect attributes.
Each interpretation highlights a different aspect of Allah's nature.
4
Reflect on doctrinal implications
Consider why this discussion matters for tawhid and the names of Allah. How does each interpretation reinforce the concept of Allah's oneness?
This connects to the broader theme of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الرابع : الكلام في الوحيد ، قال تعالى : ( ذرني ومن خلقت وحيداً ) (۲) فقوله ( وحيداً ، نصب على الحال على بعض الأقوال . وعلى هذا التقدير فيحتمل أن يكون حالاً من الخالق ، وأن يكون حالا من المخلوق . فإذا قلنا : إن وحيداً نصب على الحال من الخالق كان القرآن وارداً بجعل الوحيد إسماً من أسماء الله . وفي تفسيره (۱) وجوه : الأول : أنه سبحانه كان موجوداً في الأزل . والثاني : أنه سبحانه مستقل بتدبير الملك والملكوت ، ولا يحتاج في التكوين والتخليق إلى مادة ، ومدة ، وآلة ، وعدة . والثالث : أنه متوحد بصفات الجلال ونعوت الكمال
The fourth branch: discussion of al-Wahid (the Unique). He said exalted: (ذرني ومن خلقت وحيداً – Leave Me and him whom I created alone). So His word (وحيداً – alone) is in the accusative case as a circumstantial accusative (hal) according to some opinions. On this estimation, it is possible that it is a circumstantial accusative from the Creator, and that it is a circumstantial accusative from the creature. So if we say that 'wahidan' is a circumstantial accusative from the Creator, then the Quran would have come with making 'al-Wahid' a name from the names of God. And in its interpretation there are aspects: First: that He, glory be to Him, existed in pre-eternity. Second: that He, glory be to Him, is independent in managing the kingdom and the dominion, and does not need in formation and creation a material, a duration, a tool, or apparatus. Third: that He is unique in the attributes of majesty and the qualities of perfection.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage discusses the name 'al-Wahid' (the Unique). It begins by citing a Quranic verse and analyzing the grammatical state of 'wahidan'. It proposes two possibilities: it could be a circumstantial accusative either from the Creator or from the creature. If from the Creator, then 'al-Wahid' becomes a divine name. The passage then gives three interpretations of this name: pre-eternal existence, absolute independence in managing all things, and uniqueness in perfect attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'wahidan' in the verse can only refer to the created being (the disbeliever), and not to Allah.
Correction: Grammatically, 'wahidan' can be a circumstantial accusative either from the Creator or the creature. The author shows that interpreting it as referring to the Creator establishes 'al-Wahid' as a divine name.

The Name 'As-Samad': Two Interpretations
Presenting two interpretations of 'as-samad': one who is sought and one with no cavity.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Al-Samad means both the One sought in needs and the One free from composition.

This lesson explores the divine name 'al-Samad', which appears in Surah al-Ikhlas. Scholars have offered two linguistic interpretations for its meaning, each pointing to a distinct aspect of Allah's perfection. By understanding both derivations, we gain a deeper appreciation for this name.

Guided Path

Read the Arabic passage and translation

2

The first derivation is supported by an Arabic phrase.
3
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
The second derivation must be interpreted in a way that negates any physical composition from Allah. This is an important principle of tanzih.
The passage explicitly says 'يجب حمله في حق الله تعالى منزهاً عن التركيب والتألف'.

The First Meaning of al-Badi': Peerlessness

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Allah is al-Badi'—the One without like or peer.

The first meaning of 'al-Badi' emphasizes God's incomparability. The Arabic word 'badi'' is used for something unprecedented, and here it affirms that Allah has no equal. This lesson unpacks that foundational concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic phrase defining the first meaning and its translation. Notice how 'badi'' is applied to something without a peer.
The verse citation is from Surah al-Baqarah 2:117.
2
Identify the key term 'mithl'
The term 'mithl' (like) and 'shabih' (peer) are negated. Reflect on how this establishes tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثاني عشر : البديع . قال سبحانه : ( بديع السموات والأرض (۳) وفيه وجهان : الأول : أنه الذي لا مثل له ، ولا شبيه ، يقال هذا شيء بديع ، إذا كان عديم المثل . والثاني : أن البديع هو المبدع، فعيل بمعنى مفعل . ومعنى المبدع أن تكون أفعاله واقعة دفعة واحدة من غير سبق مادة ، ومدة ، وآلة وعدة

And the meaning of al-Mubdi' is that His actions occur all at once without the precedence of a material, duration, tool, or apparatus.

It gives two meanings: first, that God has no peer or likeness; second, that He is the originator (mubdi') who creates things without pre-existing materials, tools, or time. The word 'badi'' is of the form fa'il but means muf'il, indicative of His power to bring about creation instantaneously.

The Name 'al-Wahid' and Its Three Meanings
Lists three interpretations of the divine name al-Wahid from the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scriptural interpretations of the divine name al-Wahid: preeternity, independence in creation, and uniqueness in attributes.

Core Idea
Al-Wahid means not only 'One' but also uniquely eternal, self-sufficient, and unparalleled in perfection.

The Quran uses 'al-Wahid' as a name of God that goes beyond mere numerical oneness. In this passage, the author explains three distinct aspects of what it means for Allah to be 'al-Wahid,' each rooted in His absolute sovereignty and perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation to get the author's own words. Try to identify the three aspects mentioned.
The passage begins with a grammatical remark before listing the aspects.
2
Examine the three interpretations
Go through each of the three aspects (wujuh). Notice how each one highlights a different dimension of divine oneness.
The first aspect focuses on eternity, the second on independence, the third on perfection.
3
Reflect on the corrective value
Consider how each aspect corrects a common misunderstanding. For example, the second aspect refutes the idea that God needs tools or materials to create.
This shows that al-Wahid is more than a number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : إن وحيداً نصب على الحال من الخالق كان القرآن وارداً بجعل الوحيد إسماً من أسماء الله . وفي تفسيره وجوه : الأول : أنه سبحانه كان موجوداً في الأزل . والثاني : أنه سبحانه مستقل بتدبير الملك والملكوت ، ولا يحتاج في التكوين والتخليق إلى مادة ، ومدة ، وآلة ، وعدة . والثالث : أنه متوحد بصفات الجلال ونعوت الكمال
We say: 'That 'Wahid' was set in the accusative as a circumstantial accusative from 'the Creator' (al-Khaliq). The Quran came by making 'al-Wahid' a name among the names of God. And in its interpretation there are aspects: The first: that He, exalted, existed in preeternity (al-azal). The second: that He, exalted, is independent in managing the kingdom (al-mulk) and the dominion (al-malakut), and does not need in formation and creation any matter, duration, tool, or means. The third: that He is unique (mutawahhid) with the attributes of majesty (al-jalal) and the perfections (nu'ut al-kamal).'
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name 'al-Wahid' (the One). It first notes that the Quran uses 'Wahid' as a name of God, citing its grammatical construction as a circumstantial accusative (hal) from the Creator. Then it gives three interpretations. The first interpretation: He was existent in preeternity (al-azal), meaning He has no beginning. The second: He is independent in managing the entire cosmos (the visible and invisible realms) and does not require any material, time, tools, or instruments for creation. The third: He is unique in possessing all attributes of majesty and perfection, i.e., no one shares in His perfections.

أزل
ملكوت
جلال

Misconception & Correction

The Divine Name as-Samad: Two Interpretations
Two etymological interpretations of as-Samad with theological clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two different etymological interpretations of the divine name as-Samad and how the second interpretation affirms Allah's absolute simplicity.

Core Idea
As-Samad means both the One sought for needs and the One free from composition.

The name as-Samad appears in Surah al-Ikhlas and carries profound meaning. Scholars have proposed two linguistic interpretations: one focusing on His being the ultimate destination for all needs, and the other on His being free from any hollowness or composition. This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents both views and insists that the second must be understood as affirming God's transcendence over composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the two interpretations introduced by 'qīla' (it is said) and 'wa ath-thānī' (and the second).
2
Understand the first interpretation
The first interpretation derives as-Samad from the verb 'sammada', meaning to intend or aim for someone in needs. Thus, as-Samad is the One to Whom all creatures turn for their needs.
The example 'samadtu samada hadha al-amr' illustrates the linguistic usage.
3
Understand the second interpretation
The second interpretation says as-Samad means 'He who has no cavity (jawf)'. This negation implies that Allah is not composed of parts or elements, affirming His absolute simplicity.
The text adds that this must be applied to God as transcendent over composition (tarkib) and combination (ta'alluf).
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why both interpretations are important: the first highlights Allah's role as the ultimate provider and refuge, the second safeguards His oneness and incompositeness.
This lesson shows how a name can have multiple layers of meaning that reinforce tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الحادي عشر : الصمد . قيل : إنه مأخوذ من صمده، إذا قصده في الحوائج، يقال صمدت صمد هذا الأمر، أي قصدت قصده. والثاني : أنه الذي لا جوف له، ويجب حمله في حق الله تعالى منزهاً عن التركيب والتألف
The eleventh name: as-Samad. It is said: it is derived from 'sammada' (صمده) meaning to intend him in needs. It is said: 'samadtu samada hadha al-amr' meaning I intended its purpose. And the second: that He is the one who has no cavity (jawf), and it must be applied to God, exalted, transcendent (munazzahan) over composition (tarkib) and combination (ta'alluf).
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This lesson explains the divine name 'as-Samad'. It gives two interpretations. The first: it derives from the verb 'sammada', meaning to intend or aim for someone in needs; thus as-Samad is the One to whom all turn for their needs. The second: as-Samad means 'He who has no cavity (jawf)', i.e., no interior emptiness, which when applied to God must be understood as negating any composition or combination (tarkib and ta'alluf), affirming His absolute simplicity.

Key Terms
صمد
جوف
تركيب
تألف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think as-Samad literally means 'solid' or 'massive', implying a physical body.
Correction: The second interpretation 'no cavity' is metaphorical: it negates any hollowness or composition, not a material body. Both interpretations affirm God's transcendence and simplicity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The name as-Samad carries dual meaning: the One to Whom all turn for needs and the One free from any composition or hollowness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
As-Samad has no cavity, no emptiness, no fragmentation. Where in your soul do you feel hollow or divided? Are you trying to fill that void with anything other than as-Samad? Will you, right now, ask Him to fill that specific empty space with His sufficiency?
?
The name as-Samad means all creatures seek Him. Is there a person you have been avoiding or even resenting because you feel they are not seeking you? Could your bitterness be rooted in forgetting that you are also a seeker of as-Samad, not a source? Will you forgive that person and ask as-Samad to be your sufficiency instead?

Think about why both aspects are necessary for a complete understanding of al-Badi'.

الإسم الثاني عشر : البديع . قال سبحانه : ( بديع السموات والأرض ) وفيه وجهان : الأول : أنه الذي لا مثل له، ولا شبيه، يقال هذا شيء بديع، إذا كان عديم المثل . والثاني : أن البديع هو المبدع، فعيل بمعنى مفعل . ومعنى المبدع أن تكون أفعاله واقعة دفعة واحدة من غير سبق مادة، ومدة، وآلة وعدة
The twelfth name: al-Badi'. He, glorified, said: 'Originator (Badi') of the heavens and the earth.' And it has two aspects: First: that He has no like (mithl) and no resemblance (shabih); it is said: this is a badi' thing when it is without peer. Second: that al-Badi' is al-mubdi' (the originator); the form fa'il is in the sense of muf'il. And the meaning of al-mubdi' is that His acts occur all at once (daf'atan wahidatan) without any preceding matter, duration, tool, or means.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This lesson discusses the name 'al-Badi''. It cites the Quranic verse 'Badi' of the heavens and the earth' (al-Baqarah 117) and gives two interpretations. The first: al-Badi' means the One who has no like or resemblance—something unique. The second: grammatically, fa'il (بديع) is equivalent to muf'il (مبدع), meaning 'originator' or 'innovator'. The originator is one whose actions occur instantly (daf'atan wahidatan) without any preexisting matter, time, tools, or instruments. This emphasizes creation ex nihilo.
