The Name al-Ghani: Proof of Divine Self-Sufficiency
Proves that the Necessary Being is intrinsically rich, independent of all else, using modal argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that Allah is al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient) and why everything other than Him is inherently needy.

Core Idea
Allah's necessity of existence makes Him utterly independent of all else.

This lesson explores the divine name al-Ghani, often translated as 'the Rich,' but meaning ontological self-sufficiency. The proof builds on the established fact that Allah is necessary of existence by Himself. By analyzing what it means to be necessary, we see that dependence on another would undermine that necessity. The passage uses a modal dilemma to show that the Necessary Being cannot be made necessary by another, and therefore has no connection to anything outside Himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument starts with Quranic verses before the rational proof.
2
Identify the key premise
The premise is that Allah is wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi (necessary by Himself). Understand why this is the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the dilemma
The proof presents a dilemma: if the Necessary Being depended on another, what would happen if that other were removed? Work through both possibilities and see why each leads to contradiction.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since both possibilities are impossible, the Necessary Being has no connection to anything else. Everything else needs Him in essence, existence, and attributes.
Conclude that He alone is al-Ghani, and all others are poor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثالث عشر : الغني . قال تعالى ( سبحانه هو الغني ) وقال : ( والله الغني، وأنتم الفقراء ) ولما ثبت أنه واجب الوجود لذاته . قالوا : الواجب لذاته يمتنع أن يصير واجباً لغيره، لأن عند فرض عدم ذلك الغير - إن بقي - لم يكن واجباً بغيره، وإن لم يبق لم يكن واجباً لذاته، وكل ذلك محال . فإذن الواجب بذاته لا تعلق له بغيره البتة، وكل ما سواه فهو يحتاج إليه في ماهيته، وفي وجوده، وفي صفاته. فظهر بهذا البيان : أنه سبحانه هو الغني، وكل ما سواه فهو فقير وغني بإغناء الله
The thirteenth name: al-Ghani (the Rich/Independent). He, exalted, said: 'Glorified is He, He is al-Ghani' and He said: 'And God is al-Ghani, and you are the poor (fuqara').' And since it is established that He is necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud) by Himself, they said: 'The necessary by Himself is impossible to become necessary through another, because when supposing the non-existence of that other—if He remains, He would not be necessary through another; and if He does not remain, He would not be necessary by Himself; and all of that is impossible. So then the necessary by Himself has no connection (ta'allaq) to another at all, and everything else is in need of Him in its essence (mahiyyah), its existence, and its attributes. So it became clear by this exposition: that He, exalted, is al-Ghani, and everything else is poor (faqir) and rich only through God's enrichment (ighna' Allah).'
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This lesson proves the name 'al-Ghani' (the Self-Sufficient) through a modal argument. It begins by citing Quranic verses where God declares His own independence and human poverty. It then builds on the established premise that God is necessary of existence by Himself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). The argument runs: a necessary being cannot become necessary through another, because if that other were absent, either the first would still exist (meaning it was not truly dependent) or it would not exist (meaning it was not necessary by itself). Both outcomes are impossible for a necessary being. Therefore, the necessary by essence has no connection (ta'allaq) to anything else whatsoever. Everything other than Him needs Him in its very essence, existence, and attributes. The conclusion: He alone is al-Ghani, and all others are poor (fuqara') and only rich through God's enrichment.

Key Terms
غني
فقير
واجب الوجود
البتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ghani means 'rich' in the sense of material wealth, like a wealthy person.
Correction: Al-Ghani refers to ontological self-sufficiency: Allah has no need of anything and everything depends on Him.

Checkpoint
What is the key argument that the Necessary Being cannot be made necessary by another?
A) If another exists, they would compete
B) If the other is removed, either He remains (contradicting need) or ceases (contradicting necessity)
C) The Necessary Being must have no cause
D) The Necessary Being must be one

Key Takeaway
Allah's self-sufficiency (al-Ghani) follows logically from His necessity of existence: He depends on nothing, and all else depends on Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is al-Ghani, utterly independent of you, yet you are completely dependent on Him in your essence, existence, and attributes, what does that mean for your sense of self-reliance? In what area of your life are you still acting as if you are not poor before Him?
?
The proof shows that Allah's necessity makes it impossible for Him to be connected to others in a way that benefits Him. Are there ways you have imagined that your worship could benefit Allah? How does this passage correct that and reorient your intention?
?
If every good you have is only through Allah's enrichment (ighna'), then what does that say about your achievements, your wealth, your intellect? How does this affect your gratitude and humility right now?

The Name 'al-Qayyum' and Its Dual Meaning
Sanusi explains the name al-Qayyum as combining self-subsistence and sustaining others, with a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine name al-Qayyum combines two essential meanings: God's absolute self-subsistence and His being the sustainer of all creation, and the rational proof for both aspects.

Core Idea
Al-Qayyum means God stands by Himself and sustains everything else.

The name al-Qayyum is one of the most profound divine names, appearing in the famous Ayat al-Kursi. As we will see, it is not simply 'eternal' but carries the rich meaning of God being both self-sufficient and the maintainer of all existence. This lesson unpacks these two dimensions and provides a rational argument for why they must apply to the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the source passage carefully to get an overall sense of Sanusi's argument. Notice how he introduces the name, cites Qur'anic evidence, and then breaks down the meaning into two components.
2
Identify the two component meanings
The passage states that the perfection of al-Qayyum requires two matters: qiyamuhu bi-dhatihi (self-subsistence) and kawnuhu qayyiman bi-ghayrihi (sustaining others). Make sure you understand each.
These are the core of the name.
3
Follow the rational proof
The author provides a proof: since the Necessary Being is one and everything else is contingent, it must be dependent on Him. This establishes both that God is self-subsistent and sustains all others.
4
Consider the implication of transcendence
The passage concludes that self-subsistence entails freedom from composition and directions. Reflect on why this is important.
This guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع عشر : القيوم . قال تعالى : ( الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم ) وقال : ( وعنت الوجوه للحي القيوم ) وقرأ عمر بن الخطاب : القيام، واعلم أن القيوم مبالغة في القيام، وكمال هذا المعنى إنما يحصل عند اجتماع أمرين: أحدهما : قيامه بذاته، كما قال : ( قائماً بالقسط ) بمعنى استغنائه عن كل ما سواه . والثاني : كونه قيماً بغيره، بمعنى كونه مقوماً لغيره، ومجموع هذين المعنيين هو القيوم . ولما دللنا على أن واجب الوجود واحد، وثبت أن ما سواء فهو ممكن لذاته، فلا بد له من مؤثر، فكل ما سوى ذلك الواحد فهو مفتقر إليه فيثبت أنه تعالى قائم بذاته . مقوم لكل ما سواه، فهو القيوم الحي بذاته ولذاته . واعلم أن كونه قائماً بذاته، يقتضي أن يكون منزهاً عن جهات التركيب
The fourteenth name: al-Qayyum. He, exalted, said: 'God, there is no god but He, the Living, the Self-Subsisting (al-Qayyum)' and He said: 'And faces will be humbled before the Living, the Self-Subsisting.' And 'Umar ibn al-Khattab recited: al-Qayyam. And know that al-Qayyum is an intensive form (mubalaghah) of standing (al-qiyam). And the perfection of this meaning is only attained when two matters come together: One of them: His standing (qiyamuhu) by Himself, as He said: 'Standing with justice' meaning His independence from everything else. And the second: His being a sustainer (qayyim) of others, meaning His being a mughawwim (one who sets things straight) for others. And the combination of these two meanings is al-Qayyum. Since we argued that the Necessary of Existence is one, and it is established that what is other than Him is possible (mumkin) by itself, it must have an influencer (mu'aththir). So everything other than that One is in need of Him, so it is established that He, exalted, is standing (qa'im) by Himself, a sustainer (muqawwim) of everything else. So He is al-Qayyum, the Living by Himself and for Himself. And know that His being standing (qa'im) by Himself entails that He is transcendent (munazzah) over the directions of composition (jihaat al-tarkib).
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This lesson covers the name 'al-Qayyum'. It cites Quranic verses (Al 'Imran 2? Actually from Ayat al-Kursi 2:255 and Taha 111). It mentions that 'Umar ibn al-Khattab read it as 'al-Qayyam' (a variant). The name is an intensive form (mubalaghah) of 'qiyam' (standing or subsistence). The perfection of this meaning requires two aspects: First, His standing (qiyam) by Himself, meaning absolute self-sufficiency (istighna'), as indicated by the verse 'qa'iman bi'l-qist' (standing with justice). Second, His being a sustainer (qayyim) of others, meaning He is the one who establishes and maintains all things (muqawwim). The combination of these is al-Qayyum. The author then gives a proof: since the Necessary Being is one and all else is possible (contingent), everything else needs an influencer (mu'aththir) to bring it into existence and sustain it. Therefore, God is qa'im bi-dhatihi (self-subsisting) and muqawwim for all others. The passage concludes with a note that being self-subsisting entails being free from any composition or directions (jihaat al-tarkib).

Key Terms
قيوم
قيام
مقوم
مستقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Qayyum simply means eternal or everlasting, devoid of active sustaining.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Qayyum is an intensive form combining both self-subsistence (qa'im bi-dhatihi) and being a sustainer for others (qayyim li-ghayrihi), actively maintaining creation.

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Al-Qayyum uniquely captures God's dual nature of being completely self-sufficient and actively sustaining every other being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If Allah is the only One who stands by Himself and sustains all, why do you still seek ultimate security in anything other than Him? What would it look like to entrust your entire existence to al-Qayyum tomorrow?

Definition of the Divine Name Al-Qayyum
Defines al-Qayyum as combining self-subsistence and sustaining others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine name al-Qayyum combines self-subsistence and the sustenance of all creation, and how this is proven rationally.

Core Idea
Al-Qayyum means both self-sufficient and sustainer of all.

This source passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah defines the divine name al-Qayyum. It begins by citing Quranic verses and a unique recitation by Umar ibn al-Khattab, then explains that the name is a hyperbole of 'standing', requiring two meanings: self-subsistence and sustaining others. The author also provides a rational proof from the unity of the Necessary Existent. Let's read the text carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the two aspects
Find where the author lists the two meanings of al-Qayyum and note them.
3
Follow the rational proof
Trace the argument from the unity of the Necessary Existent to the conclusion that He is al-Qayyum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع عشر : القيوم . قال تعالى : ( الله لا إله إلا هو الحي القيوم ) (٣) وقال : ( وعنت الوجوه للحي القيوم ) ( وقرأ عمر بن الخطاب : القيام ، واعلم أن القيوم مبالغة في القيام ، وكمال هذا المعنى إلما يحصل عند اجتماع أمرين: أحدهما : قيامة بذاته ، كما قال : ( قائماً بالقسط ) (1) بمعنى استغنائه عن كل ما سواه . والثاني : كونه قيماً بغيره ، بمعنى كونه مقوماً لغيره ، ومجموع هذين المعنيين هو القيوم . ولما دللنا على أن واجب الوجود واحد ، وثبت أن ما سواء فهو ممكن لذاته ، فلا يد له من مؤثر ، فكل ما سوى ذلك الواحد فهو مفتقر إليه فيثبت أنه تعالى قائم بذاته . مقوم لكل ما سواه ، فهو القيوم الحي بذاته ولذاته .
The fourteenth name: Al-Qayyum. He said: 'Allah, there is no god but He, the Living, the Self-Subsisting.' (3) And He said: 'Faces shall be humbled before the Living, the Self-Subsisting.' (4) And Umar ibn al-Khattab recited: 'al-Qayyam'. Know that al-Qayyum is a hyperbole of standing (qiyam), and the perfection of this meaning is only attained when two things come together: One: His being self-subsistent, as He said: 'maintaining justice' (1), meaning His self-sufficiency from everything else. Two: His being the sustainer of others, meaning He is the one who establishes others. And the combination of these two meanings is al-Qayyum. And since we have proven that the Necessary Existent is one, and it is established that whatever is other than Him is contingent in itself, so it cannot do without an effecter, so everything other than that One is in need of Him, so it is established that He is self-subsistent and sustainer of everything else, so He is al-Qayyum, the Living by Himself and for Himself.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Qayyum. It begins by presenting Quranic evidence for the name, from Surat al-Baqara and Surat Ta-Ha, and notes a variant recitation by Umar ibn al-Khattab as 'al-Qayyam'. The author then explains that al-Qayyum is a hyperbole of 'qiyam' (standing), and its complete meaning requires two aspects: first, standing by Himself, meaning His self-sufficiency; second, standing as the sustainer of others. The author then provides a rational proof: since we have established that the Necessary Existent is one, and that everything else is contingent and dependent on an effecter, it follows that He is self-subsistent and sustainer of all, hence al-Qayyum.

Divine Knowledge: A Corollary of Self-Subsistence
Derives divine omniscience from self-subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine knowledge is derived from Allah's self-subsistence, and why His knowledge of Himself entails knowledge of all creation.

Core Idea
Allah's self-subsistence guarantees His perfect self-knowledge, which extends to all creation.

This passage presents the fourth corollary of al-qayyumiyya: divine knowledge. It begins by defining knowledge as the presence of the reality of the known to the knower. Using this definition, the author shows that because Allah is self-subsistent, His own reality is present to Him, making Him knowledgeable of Himself. Then, because He is the cause of all things, His self-knowledge necessarily includes knowledge of everything He creates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise logical flow: definition, application to Allah, then extension to creation.
The passage uses the term 'al-qayyumiyya' (self-subsistence) as the foundational attribute.
2
Identify the definition of knowledge
Find the phrase that defines knowledge. What is the essence of knowledge according to this passage? Write it down in your own words.
The definition is: 'presence of the reality of the known to the knower.'
3
Trace the logical derivation
Follow the argument: How does self-subsistence lead to self-knowledge? How does self-knowledge lead to knowledge of everything else? Map out the steps.
The key link is that Allah's essence is the cause of all others, so knowledge of the cause implies knowledge of its effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع من لوازم القيومية : قال بعضهم : لا معنى للعلم إلا حضور حقيقة المعلوم عند العالم ، فإذا كان قائماً بنفسه كانت حقيقته حاصلة له ، لا للغير، فكان عالماً بذاته ثم ذاته علة لغيره ، فيلزم من علمه بذاته علمه بغيره إلى آخر المراتب .
The fourth type of corollaries of al-qayyumiyya: Some said: Knowledge is nothing but the presence of the reality of the known to the knower. So if He is self-subsistent, His reality is present to Him, not to another, so He is knowledgeable of Himself, and then His essence is the cause of others, so from His knowledge of Himself follows His knowledge of others to the end of the chain.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth corollary: divine knowledge. It cites a definition of knowledge as the presence of the reality of the known to the knower. Since Allah is self-subsistent (qa'im bi nafsihi), His own reality is present to Him, making Him knowledgeable of Himself. Then, because His essence is the cause of all other existents, knowledge of Himself entails knowledge of everything He creates, from the highest to the lowest.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage shows that His knowledge is essential, derived from His self-subsistence, not acquired.

Key Takeaway

The Dual Classification of Al-Qayyum
Classifies al-Qayyum as salbi and idafi with spiritual reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine name 'al-Qayyum' is classified into both negative (salbi) and relational (idafi) categories, and the spiritual significance of recognizing this name.

Core Idea
Al-Qayyum combines independence from others and sustaining others, a dual classification that deepens tawhid.

In this passage, the author explains that the name 'al-Qayyum' carries two distinct theological dimensions. On one hand, it affirms Allah's complete self-sufficiency; on the other, it emphasizes His role as the Sustainer of all creation. The author then shares profound spiritual insights from Ibn Abbas and Abu Yazid al-Bistami about the transformative effect of truly understanding this name.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the two-part classification.
2
Identify the two aspects of al-Qayyum
The passage explicitly states that al-Qayyum indicates two things: Allah's self-sufficiency (salbi) and His sustaining of others (idafi). Locate these two descriptions in the text.
These two categories are fundamental in kalam for understanding divine attributes.
3
Reflect on Ibn Abbas' saying
Read the quote from Ibn Abbas that al-Hayy al-Qayyum is the greatest name of Allah. Consider what it means that knowing this name cuts one's heart off from creation.
This spiritual reflection links theological understanding with practical reliance on Allah.
4
Consider Abu Yazid's incomplete saying
The passage ends with 'حسبك' (sufficient for you). Reflect on how this short phrase, though incomplete, emphasizes that knowledge of Allah suffices the believer.

Sixth Implication of al-Qayyum: Qada and Qadr Necessary
Explains the sixth implication: everything depends on God so qada and qadr are necessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attribute al-Qayyum necessarily leads to the belief in qada and qadr and how it encompasses all major theological topics.

Core Idea
Because God is the Sustainer of all, everything depends on Him, making divine decree inevitable.

This passage presents the final implication of God's attribute al-Qayyum (the Self-Subsisting Sustainer). It argues that since God sustains all possible things, everything is dependent on Him either directly or indirectly, and this makes the doctrine of divine decree (qada and qadr) a necessary consequence. The passage concludes by affirming that the combined name 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum' covers the entirety of theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the original Arabic text and its literal English translation once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the technical terms: القيوم, القضاء والقدر, الممكنات.
2
Trace the logical argument
Identify the premise: God is Qayyum over all possible things. Then see the conclusion: all things depend on Him, so qada and qadr are necessary.
The argument is a simple conditional: if God is Qayyum, then everything depends on Him; if everything depends on Him, then divine decree is necessary.
3
Reflect on the concluding statement
Consider why the author says that the name 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum' encompasses all lofty theological investigations.
This shows the centrality of these two attributes in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السادس من لوازم القيومية: أنه لما كان قيوماً لكل الممكنات، استندت كل الممكنات إليه، إما بواسطة أو بغير واسطة، وعلى التقديرين كان القول بالقضاء والقدر لازماً. فظهر بما ذكرنا: أن قولنا: (الحي القيوم) محيط بجميع المباحث العالية المعتبرة في الإلهيات.
The sixth type of the implications of al-Qayyumiyya: that since He is Qayyum over all possible things, all possible things are dependent on Him, either through an intermediary or without an intermediary, and on both suppositions the affirmation of qada' and qadr is necessary. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned: that our saying 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum' encompasses all the lofty investigations considered in theology.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth and final implication of God's attribute al-Qayyum (the Self-Subsisting Sustainer). The argument begins with the premise that because God is Qayyum over all possible things (al-mumkinat), all such things must ultimately be dependent on Him. This dependence can be either direct or through some intermediate cause. In either case, the doctrine of divine decree and predestination (al-qada' wa al-qadr) necessarily follows, because all things are ultimately in God's control. The passage then concludes that the combined name 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum' encompasses all the important topics of theology (al-ilahiyyat).

Al-Qayyum: Self-Subsistence and Sustainer
Analyzes al-Qayyum as indicating both negative and relational attributes, with sayings of Ibn Abbas and Abu Yazid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine name al-Qayyum encompasses both God's independence (negative attribute) and His sustaining of creation (relational attribute), and how this understanding deepens reliance on Allah.

Core Idea
Al-Qayyum: God's independence and His sustaining power — two sides of one name.

The name al-Qayyum is one of the greatest names of Allah, and classical scholars have analyzed it from two perspectives: what it says about Allah in Himself, and what it says about His relation to creation. This lesson explores these dimensions and draws out their spiritual implications for reliance on God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author first connects al-Qayyum to two categories of attributes.
2
Identify the two attribute categories
Look for the words 'bab al-salub' and 'bab al-idafat'. Understand why al-Qayyum falls under both: negation of need (salub) and relation of sustaining (idafat).
The same name can have multiple aspects in kalam analysis.
3
Reflect on the sayings of Ibn Abbas and Abu Yazid
Consider how Ibn Abbas elevates the name and how Abu Yazid's saying connects recognition of al-Qayyum to tawakkul (reliance). Ask yourself: How does this change your perspective on dependency?
This shows the practical fruit of theological knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا. فالقيوم من حيث إنه يدل على استغنائه عن الغير وقيامه بذاته كان من باب السلوب، ومن حيث إنه يدل على كونه مقوماً لغيره، كان من باب الإضافات. وعن ابن عباس أنه كان يقول: «أعظم أسماء الله تعالى الحي القيوم». واعلم أن من عرف أنه سبحانه هو القائم والقيم والقيوم، انقطع قلبه عن الخلق بالكلية. وقال أبو يزيد البسطامي رضوان الله عليه: (حسبك من التوكل أن لا ترى لنفسك ناصراً غيره، ولا لرزقك حارثاً غيره، ولا لعملك شاهداً غيره).
If you know this. So 'al-Qayyum' — insofar as it indicates His independence of others and His self-subsistence is of the category of negations (salub), and insofar as it indicates that He is the sustainer of others is of the category of relations (idafat). Commentary
This passage provides a dual analysis of the divine name al-Qayyum. From another aspect, it indicates that He sustains and maintains everything else — this falls under relational attributes (idafat), which describe God's relation to creation. The passage then reinforces the importance of this name with a narration from Ibn Abbas, who called it the greatest of Allah's names, and a statement that true recognition of God as al-Qaim, al-Qayyim, and al-Qayyum severs the heart from all creatures. Finally, a saying of Abu Yazid al-Bistami is quoted, emphasizing that perfect trust in God involves seeing no helper, provider, or witness besides Him.

The Names al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal: Meanings and Interpretations
Defines al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal, explains meanings as active, passive, and causative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal, their occurrence in the Quran, and the three possible interpretations of the pattern fa'il in relation to Allah.

Core Idea
The names al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal point to Allah's perfect attributes — negative and positive.

In this lesson, we explore two closely related divine names: al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal. While al-Jalil itself does not appear in the Quran, Dhu al-Jalal is mentioned twice, emphasizing Allah's majesty and honor. The passage distinguishes these names from al-Kabir and al-Azim and explains the rich meanings behind the pattern of the word.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the Quranic occurrence
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that al-Jalil is not in the Quran, but Dhu al-Jalal appears twice in Surah al-Rahman. Understand the textual footnote about their identity.
The passage begins by clarifying that al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal are the same name.
2
Distinguish al-Kabir, al-Jalil, and al-Azim
The passage defines three names: al-Kabir for perfection of Essence, al-Jalil for perfection of attributes, and al-Azim for perfection of Essence, attributes, and actions. Compare them.
3
Understand al-Jalil as perfection of negative and positive attributes
Al-Jalil indicates that Allah is transcendent (negative: no peer, place, time) and has perfect knowledge and power (positive). Reflect on how these two aspects combine.
Negative attributes negate imperfection; positive affirm perfection.
4
Examine the three meanings of the fa'il pattern
The word 'Jalil' is on the pattern fa'il. It can mean: (a) a verb sense: He magnifies believers, (b) passive object: He deserves to be magnified, (c) active subject: He possesses majestic attributes. Read the passage's explanation for each.
The three sense correspond to active, passive, and causative interpretations.
5
Reflect on the sayings about al-Jalil
The passage ends with two sayings that inspire awe and love. Consider what they teach about the relationship between the servant and this name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الخامس عشر والسادس عشر: الجليل. وذو الجلال. (اعلم أن الجليل غير وارد في القرآن، لأن الجليل هو بعينه ذو الجلال) وهذا اللفظ وارد في القرآن في سورة الرحمن مرتين: قال تعالى: (ويبقى وجه ربك ذو الجلال والإكرام) وقال في آخر السورة: (تبارك اسم ربك ذي الجلال والإكرام). واعلم أن الكبير اسم لكمال الذات والجليل اسم لكمال الصفات والعظيم اسم لكمال الذات والصفات والأفعال، فالجليل يفيد كمال الصفات السلبية والثبوتية، أما كمال الصفات السلبية فهو أنه تعالى منزه عن الضد والند والشريك والمكان والزمان وأما كمال الصفات الثبوتية فهو كونه تعالى موصوفاً بالعلم التام والقدرة التامة. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: الجليل فعيل، فيحتمل أن يكون بمعنى الفعل، أو بمعنى المفعول، أو بمعنى الفاعل. أما بمعنى الفعل فهو أنه بمعني أنه يجل المؤمنين ويكرمهم ويعظمهم (فعل هذا التفسير) فقوله ذو الجلال والإكرام) معناه: ذو الجلال والإكرام وأما بمعنى المفعول فمعناه أنه سبحانه يستحق أن يجل ويكرم، ولا يجحد وجوده، ولا يكفر بالميته. وأما بمعنى الفاعل فمعناه كونه موصوفاً بصفات الجلال. قال بعضهم (الجليل الذي جلّ من قصده، وذل من طرده، وقيل: (الجليل الذي جل قدره في قلوب العارفين، وعظم خطره في نفوس المحبين).
The fifteenth and sixteenth names: al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal. (Know that al-Jalil does not occur in the Qur'an, because al-Jalil is exactly Dhu al-Jalal.) And this word occurs in the Qur'an in Surah al-Rahman twice: He said: 'And there remains the Face of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor' (55:27) and He said at the end of the surah: 'Blessed is the name of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor' (55:78). And know that al-Kabir is a name for perfection of the Essence, al-Jalil is a name for perfection of the attributes, and al-Azim is a name for perfection of the Essence, attributes, and actions. So al-Jalil indicates perfection of negative and positive attributes. As for perfection of negative attributes, it is that He, exalted, is transcendent above opposition, equal, partner, place, and time. And as for perfection of positive attributes, it is that He, exalted, is described with perfect knowledge and perfect power. If you know this, we say: al-Jalil is of the pattern fa'il, so it is possible that it is in the sense of the verb, or in the sense of the passive object, or in the sense of the active subject. As for the sense of the verb, it means that He magnifies the believers, honors them, and glorifies them (this interpretation is an action). So His saying 'Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram' means: Possessor of Majesty and Honor. As for the sense of the passive object, it means that He, exalted, deserves to be magnified and honored, and His existence should not be denied, nor should one disbelieve in His death. As for the sense of the active subject, it means that He is described with attributes of majesty. Some said: 'al-Jalil is the one who is majestic to whoever seeks Him, and humbled is whoever is repelled by Him.' And it is said: 'al-Jalil is the one whose status is great in the hearts of the knowers, and whose importance is tremendous in the souls of the lovers.'
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines the divine names al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal. It first notes that al-Jalil itself is not found in the Quran, but Dhu al-Jalal appears twice in Surah al-Rahman. The passage distinguishes three names: al-Kabir denotes perfection of the divine Essence, al-Jalil perfection of the attributes, and al-Azim perfection of Essence, attributes, and actions. Al-Jalil specifically indicates perfection of both negative attributes (transcendence above contrary, equal, partner, place, time) and positive attributes (perfect knowledge and power). The passage then offers a morphological analysis: the pattern fa'il can have three senses. 1) As a verb (meaning of 'he does'), al-Jalil means that God magnifies believers (thus 'Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram' indicates He bestows majesty and honor). 2) As a passive object, it means God deserves to be magnified and honored. 3) As an active subject, it means God is described with majestic attributes. Finally, two lyrical sayings conclude, emphasizing the awe and love that this name inspires.

ذو الجلال
الكبير
العظيم

Correction: The passage clarifies they are essentially the same, with Dhu al-Jalal found in the Quran.

Al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal refer to Allah's perfect attributes, encompassing both transcendence and perfection.

The Name al-Azim: Greatness Without Physicality
Defines al-Azim as perfection of essence, attributes, and actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine name al-Azim signifies God's perfection in essence, attributes, and actions, and is not a reference to physical size.

Core Idea
al-Azim means absolute perfection, not physical magnitude.

This lesson introduces the seventeenth divine name al-Azim. The author immediately clarifies a potential misunderstanding—that greatness implies bodily size—and instead explains that God's greatness is a matter of perfection. We will examine the Quranic basis and the precise meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the author's clear statement.
2
Identify the negation of physical greatness
Notice the author explicitly denies that God's greatness is due to a large body. Understand why this is impossible for God.
Physical greatness is impossible for God because He is not a body.
3
Understand the threefold perfection
The meaning of al-Azim is unpacked as perfection of essence, attributes, and actions. Reflect on how these three categories cover all aspects of God's greatness.
This name encompasses the full perfection of God in every respect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السابع عشر: العظيم. قال تعالى: (وهو العلي العظيم) واعلم أنه سبحانه ليس عظيماً بسبب عظم الجثة، لأن هذا محال في حقه، بل (معنى كونه) عظيماً (أنه عظيم في كمال ذاته وصفاته وأفعاله).
The seventeenth name: al-Azim. He said, exalted: 'And He is the Most High, the Most Great' (2:255). And know that He, exalted, is not great due to greatness of the body, because that is impossible in His case, but rather the meaning of His being 'Azim is that He is great in the perfection of His essence, His attributes, and His actions.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Azim. It cites the Quranic verse 'He is the Most High, the Most Great' (al-Baqarah 2:255). The author immediately clarifies that God's greatness is not physical — having a large body is impossible for God. Instead, al-Azim means that God is great in the perfection (kamal) of His essence (dhat), His attributes (sifat), and His actions (af'al). This name thus encompasses the full perfection of God in every respect, unlike al-Kabir which is specifically for the essence and al-Jalil for the attributes.

Key Terms
العظيم
كمال الذات

كمال الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage explicitly states that physical greatness is impossible for God; His greatness is the perfection of His essence, attributes, and actions.

C) Because the Qur'an does not use 'great' for God.
D) Because greatness only applies to bodies.

Key Takeaway
al-Azim defines God's greatness as perfection in essence, attributes, and actions, not physical dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you revere His essence? Do you praise His attributes? Do you submit to His actions? Make a concrete commitment today to focus on the dimension you have neglected.

The Name al-'Azim: Greatness in Essence, Attributes, and Actions
Definition and threefold classification of al-'Azim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three aspects of divine greatness implied by the name al-'Azim and how each is categorized within the framework of negations, relations, and actions.

Core Idea
God's greatness is not physical but lies in the perfection of His essence, attributes, and actions.

This lesson explains the divine name al-'Azim (the Great). The author first clarifies that God's greatness is not a matter of physical size, as that would be impossible. Instead, greatness is understood in three dimensions: essence, attributes, and actions. Each dimension is then placed into a specific theological category—negations, relations, or actions—showing the depth of this attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Pay attention to the three 'As for' sections that structure the explanation.
2
Identify the three aspects of greatness
The passage lists three aspects in which God is 'Azim: in essence, in attributes, and in actions. Notice how each is introduced with 'As for...'.
3
See the rejection of physical magnitude
The author explicitly denies that God's greatness is due to physical size, calling that impossible. Understand why this is important for tanzih (transcendence).
This guards against anthropomorphism.
4
Examine the final categorization
At the end, each aspect is assigned to a theological category: sulub (negations), idafat (relations), af'al (actions). Reflect on why each fits.
These categories are foundational in kalam discussions of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السابع عشر : العظيم . قال تعالى : وهو العلي العظيم . واعلم أنه سبحانه ليس عظيماً بسبب عظم الجثة، لأن هذا محال في حقه، بل معنى كونه عظيماً أنه عظيم في كمال ذاته وصفاته وأفعاله . فقد يقال للرجل السيد : إنه عظيم قومه . أما أنه عظيم في ذاته ، فلانه الجسم الذي لا يحيط البصر بأطرافه يقال إنه عظيم. والحق سبحانه لا يحيط العقل والفهم بكنه ذاته ، فكان أعظم من كل عظيم . وأما أنه عظيم في صفاته ، فلانه يقال : فلان عظيم قومه إذا كان أكثرهم علماً وقدرة واستغناء ، والحق سبحانه لا مناسبة لعلمه وقدرته إلى أحد فكان أعظم الموجودات ، وأما أنه عظيم في أفعاله فلان من فعل أفعالا لا يعجز عنها غيره ، يقال : إنه أعظم من غيره . ولهذا المعنى يقال : فلان أعظم ملوك الأرض ، إذا حصل له من الملك والبسطة ما ليس لغيره . والله - سبحانه - مالك الملك في الحقيقة فكان أعظم الموجودات لهذا الاعتبار. إذا عرفت هذا فالعظيم بالاعتبار الأول من باب السلوب ، وبالاعتبار الثاني من باب الإضافات ، وبالاعتبار الثالث من باب الأفعال .
The seventeenth name: al-'Azim. He said, Exalted: 'And He is the Most High, the Most Great.' And know that He, Glorified, is not great due to the magnitude of the body, because that is impossible in His case. Rather, the meaning of His being 'azim is that He is great in the perfection of His essence, attributes, and actions. For it may be said of a noble man: 'He is great among his people.' As for His being great in His essence, it is because a body whose extremities the sight cannot encompass is called great. And the Truth, Glorified, cannot be encompassed by intellect and understanding regarding the essence of His essence, so He is greater than every great thing. As for His being great in His attributes, it is because it is said: 'So-and-so is great among his people if he is the most knowledgeable, powerful, and self-sufficient among them.' And the Truth, Glorified, has no comparison to anyone in His knowledge and power, so He is the greatest of existent things. As for His being great in His actions, it is because one who does deeds that no other can do is said to be greater than others. For this meaning it is said: 'So-and-so is the greatest of the kings of the earth,' if he has attained such sovereignty and power as no other has. And Allah, Glorified, is the true Owner of Sovereignty, so He is the greatest of existent things by this consideration. When you know this, then al-'Azim in the first consideration is from the category of negations (al-sulub), in the second consideration from the category of relations (al-idafat), and in the third consideration from the category of actions (al-af'al).
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines the name al-'Azim. The author begins by citing the Qur'anic verse 'He is the Most High, the Most Great' (al-Baqara 2:255). He immediately clarifies that God's greatness is not physical—He is not great because of a large body, as that is impossible for God. Rather, greatness applies to God in three ways: (1) Greatness in essence: just as a huge object that the eye cannot encompass is called great, God's essence cannot be fully grasped by any intellect, so He is greater than everything. (2) Greatness in attributes: a person is called great among his people if he excels in knowledge, power, and self-sufficiency; God's knowledge and power have no parallel, so He is the greatest. (3) Greatness in actions: one who performs deeds that no one else can is considered greater; God alone is the true owner of sovereignty and power. The author then categorizes these three aspects: the first (essence) belongs to 'negations' (sulub) because it involves denying that His essence can be fully known; the second (attributes) belongs to 'relations' (idafat) because it involves comparison; the third (actions) belongs to 'actions' (af'al).

السلوب
الإضافات
الأفعال
كمال الذات
كمال الصفات

Correction: The author explicitly rejects physical magnitude as impossible for God; greatness is about perfection of essence, attributes, and actions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which consideration of al-'Azim is categorized under the heading 'al-sulub' (negations)?
A) Greatness in essence
B) Greatness in attributes
C) Greatness in actions
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The name al-'Azim signifies greatness in essence, attributes, and actions, each belonging to a specific theological category, and is never to be understood as physical magnitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allahu Akbar' each day, do you ever catch yourself imagining a physically immense being? How will you train your mind to focus on the true meaning of greatness—perfection in essence, attributes, and actions—starting from your next prayer?

?
You claim God is the greatest in actions, yet do you live as if He truly owns all sovereignty? In what area of your life—work, family, finances—are you acting as if you are the ultimate owner rather than a trustee? Write down one concrete change you will make by tonight to acknowledge His absolute ownership.

Al-Majid and Al-Majeed: Honor and Generosity
Author enumerates two names, explains derivation, and gives two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the derivation of al-Majid and al-Majeed and the two interpretations of their root meaning: complete honor and spacious generosity.

Core Idea
Divine names al-Majid and al-Majeed combine honor and boundless generosity.

The names al-Majid and al-Majeed appear in the Qur'an and are derived from the same root. The author explains how al-Majeed is formed from al-Majid and then presents two classic interpretations of their meaning: one emphasizing complete honor, the other emphasizing spaciousness and generosity. This lesson unpacks those meanings and their significance for understanding Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: first naming the names, then the Qur'anic verse, then the derivation, then the two interpretations.
The passage cites Surah al-Buruj 85:14-15 and possibly Surah Qaf 50:1.
2
Understand the derivation pattern
The author notes that al-Majeed follows the pattern fa'il from al-Majid, like al-'Alim from al-'Alim. This shows the connection between the two names.
3
Identify the two interpretations
Find the two sayings: (1) al-Majd means complete honor, (2) al-Majd originally means spaciousness, which leads to generosity. The commentary explains each.
The second meaning is less obvious but important for understanding divine generosity.
4
Reflect on the combined meaning
Consider how both interpretations apply to Allah. The passage concludes that al-Majeed indicates abundance of beneficence and generosity. How does honor relate to generosity?
This integrates the two meanings into a coherent attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثامن عشر والتاسع عشر : الماجد . والمجيد . قال تعالى : وهو الغفور الودود ذو العرش المجيد . واعلم أن المجيد فعيل من الماجد كالعليم من العالم ، والقدير من القادر . وفي المجيد قولان: أحدهما المجد : هو الشرف التام الكامل . قال تعالى : [فوق؟] والقرآن المجيد أي الشريف . والله تعالى له الشرف والمجد والعلو والعظمة. والثاني : أن المجد في الأصل عبارة عن السعة ، يقال رجل ماجد إذا كان سخياً مفضالاً كثير الخير. فعلى هذا المجيد في صفة الله تعالى يدل على كثرة إحسانه وجوده.
The eighteenth and nineteenth names: al-Majid and al-Majeed. He said, Exalted: 'And He is the Forgiving, the Loving, Owner of the Throne, the Glorious.' And know that al-Majeed is on the pattern fa'il from al-Majid, like al-'Alim from al-'Alim and al-Qadir from al-Qadir. And concerning al-Majeed there are two sayings: One of them is that al-Majd is complete and perfect honor. He said, Exalted: '[Fawq?] and the Glorious Qur'an' i.e., the noble. And Allah, Exalted, has honor, majesty, highness, and greatness. The second is that al-Majd originally denotes spaciousness. It is said 'rajul majid' if he is generous, bountiful, and abundant in good. So on this, al-Majeed as an attribute of Allah, Exalted, indicates the abundance of His beneficence and generosity.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage covers al-Majid and al-Majeed. The author cites the verse 'He is the Forgiving, the Loving, Owner of the Throne, the Glorious' (al-Buruj 85:14-15). He notes that al-Majeed is derived from al-Majid, analogous to al-'Alim from al-'Alim. Two interpretations are given: (1) al-Majd means complete honor, citing the Qur'anic phrase 'the Glorious Qur'an' (Qaf 50:1, though the text has an apparent corruption). (2) al-Majd originally means spaciousness, so a 'majid' person is generous and bountiful. Applied to God, al-Majeed indicates the abundance of His kindness and generosity. The passage thus combines the themes of divine honor and expansive generosity.

The Divine Name al-Subbuh: Derivation and Usage
Author introduces al-Subbuh, notes its absence from the Quran, its basis in 'Subhan Allah', and its morphological pattern.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Subbuh is a divinely attributed name accepted by consensus, derived from 'Subhan Allah', and that it follows the fu'ool morphological pattern with shadda on the 'ayn.

Core Idea
Al-Subbuh is a consensus-based divine name, not from the Quran, but from 'Subhan Allah'.

The author introduces the twentieth divine name, al-Subbuh, noting that it does not appear in the Quran but is unanimously accepted by the Muslim community. He traces its origin to the phrase 'Subhan Allah' and begins explaining its exact morphological pattern, using another word as an illustration.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the author's direct style.
The passage is short and ends mid-sentence.
2
Understand the name's origin and status
The author states that al-Subbuh is not in the Quran but is agreed upon by the community. It is derived from 'Subhan Allah'. Ask yourself: Why is this distinction important?
This shows that not all divine names are necessarily in the Quran; some come from consensus.
3
Analyze the morphological pattern
The name follows the pattern fu'ool (فعول) with a shadda on the 'ayn. The first letter (fa') takes a fatha. Look at the example 'sufood' (سفود) to see the pattern.
The passage cuts off, so only the beginning of the morphological explanation is given.
4
Connect to broader lessons
Reflect on how the name 'al-Subbuh' relates to Allah's attribute of being free from imperfection. Consider why 'Subhan Allah' is a common phrase of glorification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم العشرون : السبوح . وهذا الإسم غير وارد في القرآن لكن أجمعت الأمة على ذكره في حق الله تعالى ، وهو مأخوذ من قولك : سبحان الله وكل اسم على فعول ـ بتشديد العين - فالفاء فيه منصوب كقولك : سفود
The twentieth name: al-Subbuh. This name is not mentioned in the Qur'an, but the community has agreed upon mentioning it regarding Allah, Exalted. It is taken from your saying: 'Subhan Allah'. And every noun on the pattern of fu'ool — with the 'ayn having shadda — its first letter is in the accusative, like your saying: 'sufood'.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of the name al-Subbuh. The author states that this name does not occur in the Qur'an, but the Muslim community (umma) has agreed to use it for God. It is derived from the phrase 'Subhan Allah' (Glory be to Allah). The author then comments on its morphological pattern: it is on the form fu'ool (فعول) with the 'ayn (middle consonant) having a shadda (doubling). In such nouns, the first letter (the fa') takes a fatha (accusative case marker), as in the example 'sufood' (a spit for roasting). The passage ends here, leaving the full explanation incomplete.

Correction: The author clarifies it is not directly in the Qur'an but is traditionally accepted by consensus.

Key Takeaway
Al-Subbuh is a consensus-based divine name derived from 'Subhan Allah', and it follows the fu'ool morphological pattern.

The Name al-ʿAẓīm and Its Three Aspects
The author explains the name al-ʿAẓīm through three aspects: essence, attributes, actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three aspects of the divine name al-ʿAẓīm and how they correspond to categories of negation, relation, and action.

Core Idea
Allah's greatness transcends all comparisons and encompasses essence, attributes, and actions.

The name al-ʿAẓīm (the Great) is often understood simply as a superlative, but classical scholars analyze it through three distinct lenses. In this passage, the author unpacks how God's greatness applies to His essence (beyond comprehension), His attributes (incomparable), and His actions (unique sovereignty). This lesson will guide you through these three considerations and their classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the analogies with human greatness.
2
Identify the three aspects
The passage presents three considerations: greatness in essence, attributes, and actions. Find each one and note how the author defines them using comparisons with created things.
Each aspect shows how God's greatness is absolute.
3
Understand the classification
After explaining the three aspects, the author classifies them into categories: negations (sulūb), relations (iḍāfāt), and actions (afʿāl). Reflect on why each aspect falls into its category.
The first aspect (essence) is a negation because it denies that His essence can be encompassed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال للرجل السيد : إنه عظيم قومه . أما أنه عظيم في ذاته ، فلانه الجسم الذي لا يحيط البصر بأطرافه يقال إنه عظيم. والحق سبحانه لا يحيط العقل والفهم بكنه ذاته ، فكان أعظم من كل عظيم . وأما أنه عظيم في صفاته ، فلانه يقال : فلان عظيم قومه إذا كان أكثرهم علماً وقدرة واستغناء ، والحق سبحانه لا مناسبة لعلمه وقدرته إلى أحد فكان أعظم الموجودات ، وأما أنه عظيم في أفعاله فلان من فعل أفعالا (١) لا لا يعجز عنها غيره ، يقال : إنه أعظم من غيره . ولهذا المعنى يقال : فلان أعظم ملوك الأرض ، إذا حصل له من الملك والبسطة ما ليس لغيره . والله - سبحانه - مالك الملك في الحقيقة فكان أعظم الموجودات لهذا الاعتبار. إذا عرفت هذا فالعظيم بالاعتبار الأول من باب السلوب ، وبالاعتبار الثاني من باب الإضافات ، وبالاعتبار الثالث من باب الأفعال .
It is said of a man who is a master: He is the great one of his people. As for being great in essence, it is because the body whose extremities the sight cannot encompass is said to be great. But the Truth, exalted is He, the intellect and understanding do not encompass the reality of His essence, so He is greater than every great one. And as for being great in His attributes, it is because it is said: So-and-so is the great one of his people if he is the most knowledgeable, capable, and self-sufficient among them, and the Truth, exalted is He, His knowledge and power have no comparison to anyone, so He is the greatest of beings. And as for being great in His actions, it is because whoever does actions that no one else is incapable of, it is said: He is greater than others. And for this meaning it is said: So-and-so is the greatest of the kings of the earth, if he obtains sovereignty and power that no one else has. And Allah, exalted is He, is the owner of sovereignty in reality, so He is the greatest of beings for this consideration. If you understand this, then al-ʿAẓīm according to the first consideration is from the category of negations (sulūb), according to the second consideration from the category of relations (iḍāfāt), and according to the third consideration from the category of actions (afʿāl).
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-ʿAẓīm by analyzing three aspects: greatness in essence, in attributes, and in actions. The author begins by noting that a human leader is called 'great' due to his people. But true greatness in essence belongs to that which cannot be fully perceived; God's essence is beyond comprehension, making Him the greatest. Next, greatness in attributes: a leader is great if he excels in knowledge, power, and self-sufficiency; God's knowledge and power have no comparison, so He is greatest. Finally, greatness in actions: one who does acts that no one else can do is called greater; God alone is the true owner of sovereignty. The author concludes by classifying these aspects: the first (essence) falls under negations (sulūb), the second under relations (iḍāfāt), and the third under actions (afʿāl).

Key Terms
سلوب
إضافات
أفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Greatness always implies comparison to others.
Correction: God's greatness in essence is absolute and beyond comparison because His essence is beyond comprehension.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which category does the greatness of God's essence fall?
A) Attributes (iḍāfāt)
B) Actions (afʿāl)
C) Negations (sulūb)
D) Relations

Key Takeaway
The name al-ʿAẓīm encompasses three distinct aspects: essence (negation of comprehension), attributes (incomparability), and actions (unique sovereignty).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the greatness of Allah—through His essence, attributes, or actions—which aspect moves your heart to humility and awe right now? How can you actively reflect on that aspect today in your daily life?
?
The passage says Allah's essence is beyond the grasp of intellect. Are you truly comfortable with not being able to comprehend Him fully, or do you sometimes try to reduce Him to what you can understand? What would it mean to trust His greatness even when you cannot grasp it?

Allah is described as 'the owner of sovereignty in reality.' Does your daily planning and worry reflect that you truly believe He is in control? What is one specific area where you need to surrender your plans to His sovereignty starting today?

The Names al-Mājid and al-Majīd
Sanusi defines al-Mājid and al-Majīd, giving two interpretations of al-Majīd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names al-Mājid and al-Majīd, including the two interpretations of al-Majīd as complete honor and abundant generosity.

Core Idea
Al-Majīd means both complete honor and abundant generosity.

In this lesson, we explore two beautiful names of Allah: al-Mājid and al-Majīd. The second name, al-Majīd, is particularly rich, carrying two distinct interpretations rooted in Arabic linguistics. Understanding both dimensions deepens our appreciation of Allah's majesty and bounty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the names are introduced, then the intensive form, then two interpretations.
Pay attention to the Qur'anic citations.
2
Understand the linguistic relationship
The author explains that al-Majīd is an intensive form (faʿīl) from al-Mājid, similar to al-ʿAlīm from al-ʿĀlim. This reinforces that al-Majīd expresses a heightened attribute.
3
Identify the first interpretation
The first meaning of al-Majd is complete and perfect honor (sharaf). The author cites the Qur'anic phrase 'the glorious Qur'an' to support this.
Honor here implies exalted status and majesty.
4
Identify the second interpretation
The second meaning traces al-Majd to spaciousness (saʿa), indicating generosity. A mājid person is generous and abundant in good. Thus, Allah's attribute al-Majīd signifies His infinite beneficence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثامن عشر والتاسع عشر : الماجد . والمجيد . قال تعالى ( وهـو الغفور الودود ذو العرش المجيد (۲) ) واعلم أن المجيد فعيل من الماجد كالعليم من العالم ، والقدير من القادر . وفي المجيد قولان: أحدهما المجد : هو الشرف التام الكامل . قال تعالى : فوق والقرآن المجيد (۳) ) أي الشريف . والله تعالى له الشرف والمجد والعلو والعظمة. والثاني : أن المجد في الأصل عبارة عن السعة ، يقال رجل ماجد إذا كان سخياً مفضالاً كثير الخير. فعلى هذا ( المجيد ) في صفة الله تعالى يدل على كثرة إحسانه وجوده .
The eighteenth and nineteenth names: al-Mājid and al-Majīd. He said, exalted is He: 'And He is the Forgiving, the Loving, the Possessor of the Throne, the Glorious (al-Majīd).' And know that al-Majīd is an intensive form (faʿīl) from al-Mājid, like al-ʿAlīm from al-ʿĀlim and al-Qadīr from al-Qādir. Concerning al-Majīd there are two sayings: One of them: al-Majd is complete and perfect honor. He said, exalted is He: 'Nay, by the Glorious Qur'an' meaning the honorable. And Allah, exalted is He, has honor, majesty, exaltation, and greatness. The second: that al-Majd originally denotes spaciousness. It is said: A man is mājid if he is generous, very liberal, abundant in good. According to this, al-Majīd as an attribute of Allah, exalted is He, indicates abundance of His beneficence and generosity.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage discusses two divine names: al-Mājid and al-Majīd. The author notes that al-Majīd is an intensive form (faʿīl) from al-Mājid, similar to al-ʿAlīm from al-ʿĀlim and al-Qadīr from al-Qādir. Then two interpretations are given for al-Majīd: first, al-majd means complete and perfect honor (sharaf tāmm kāmil), citing the Qur'anic phrase 'the glorious Qur'an'. Second, al-majd originally denotes expansiveness or generosity (saʿa), so a man is called mājid if he is generous and abundant in good. Therefore, al-Majīd in God's attribute indicates abundance of His beneficence and generosity.

Key Terms
ماجد
مجيد
شرف
سعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Majīd means only glorious or honorable.
Correction: It also signifies abundant generosity based on the root meaning of spaciousness.

Checkpoint
What are the two interpretations of al-Majīd given in the passage?
A) Honor and mercy
B) Honor and generosity
C) Power and wisdom
D) Eternity and oneness

Key Takeaway
Al-Majīd encompasses both perfect honor and infinite generosity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Majīd, do you feel the weight of His majesty and the warmth of His generosity? Which of these two meanings shapes your current relationship with Him — and what would change if you truly embraced the other?
?
If al-Majīd means 'spaciousness,' why does your heart feel so narrow and constricted when you face your sins? How can you expand your hope in Allah's generosity today, starting with one concrete act of trust?
?
You have learned that al-Majīd includes both honor and generosity. Yet in your daily life, do you approach Allah more as a Judge to be feared or as a Generous Giver? What would it take for you to hold both realities together in your next prayer?

The Divine Name al-Subbūḥ
The author introduces the twentieth divine name al-Subbūḥ and its linguistic features.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the status, derivation, and unique linguistic form of the divine name al-Subbūḥ.

Core Idea
Al-Subbūḥ is a community-agreed name of Allah with a unique grammatical form.

The name al-Subbūḥ, though not directly mentioned in the Qur'an, has been unanimously accepted by the Muslim community as a name of Allah. This lesson explores its derivation from the phrase 'subḥāna llāh' and its unusual pattern, highlighting three exceptions in Arabic morphology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage describing al-Subbūḥ and its derivation, then read the English translation to understand the overall meaning.
2
Identify the status of the name
Notice that the author states the name is not in the Qur'an but the community has agreed upon it. This shows that divine names can be derived from other sources.
3
Understand the derivation and pattern
The name comes from the phrase 'subḥāna llāh'. The author explains the fuʿūl pattern and its exceptions: subbūḥ, quddūs, and dhū rūḥ. Pay attention to the irregularity.
The pattern typically has fatḥa on the fā, but these three have ḍamma?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم العشرون : السبوح . وهذا الإسم غير وارد في القرآن لكن أجمعت الأمة على ذكره في حق الله تعالى ، وهو مأخوذ من قولك : سبحان الله وكل اسم على فعول ـ بتشديد العين - فالفاء فيه منصوب كقولك : سفود وتنور ، إلا ثلاثة أحرف : سبوح ، وقدوس ، وذو روح - لواحد الذراريج - واعلم أن التسبيح مصدر ، سبحان . اسم كأنك قلت أسبحك تسبيحاً ، فوضعت لفظة سبحان لهذا المجموع وفي تفسير التسبيح وجهان : الأول : سئل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن معناه ، فقال : ( إبعاد الله عن كل سوء » أي تنزيه من كل سوء . والوجه الثاني : أن اشتقاق التسبيح من سبح في الأرض إذا ذهب فيها قال تعالى : ( إن لك في النهار سبحاً طويلاً (۱) ) فإذا حملنا التسبيح على التنزيه فأفضل الأذكار : تنزيه الله تعالى أن لا ترى أنه تعالى حكى الملائكة أنهم يسبحون الليل والنهار (()) وحكى عنهم أنهم قالوا في قصة آدم : ( ونحن نسبح بحمدك ، ونقدس لك ) وفي موضع آخر : ( وإنا لنحن الصافون وإنا لنحن المسبحون (4) ) وفي بعض الأخبار : ( أن أهل السماء الدنيا قيام إلى يوم القيامة يقولون : سبحان ذي الملك والملكوت . وأهل السماء الثانية ركوع إلى يوم القيامة يقولون : سبحان ذي العزة والجبروت . وأهل السماء الثالثة سجود إلى يوم القيامة ، يقولون : ( سبحان الحي الذي لا يموت ، واعلم . أن هذه الألفاظ المراد منها شرح تفاوت درجات الملائكة في مقامات المعرفة والمحبة والعبودية .
The twentieth name: al-Subbūḥ. This name is not found in the Qur'an, but the community has agreed on mentioning it concerning Allah, exalted is He. It is derived from your saying 'subḥāna llāh'. Every noun on the pattern fuʿūl — with the ʿayn doubled — has the fāʾ with fatḥa like your saying safūd (spit) and tannūr (oven), except three letters: subbūḥ, quddūs, and dhū rūḥ (for one kind of medicine for worms). And know that al-tasbīḥ is the verbal noun; subḥān is a noun as if you said 'I glorify You (usabbiḥuka) with a tasbīḥ', so the word subḥān was placed for this composite meaning. In the interpretation of tasbīḥ there are two aspects: The first: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was asked about its meaning, so he said: 'Removing Allah from every evil' i.e., declaring Him free from every evil. The second aspect: that the derivation of tasbīḥ is from sabḥa fī l-arḍ (to travel in the land). He said, exalted is He: 'Indeed, for you in the daytime is prolonged travel (sabḥan ṭawīlan).' So if we take tasbīḥ to mean tanzīh (declaring free), then the best of dhikr is declaring Allah exalted is He free from that you do not see... [Note: the Arabic continues] that He, exalted, mentioned the angels that they glorify night and day, and mentioned from them that they said in the story of Adam: 'And we glorify Your praise and sanctify You.' And in another place: 'And indeed we are the ones who stand in rows, and indeed we are the ones who glorify.' And in some reports: 'The inhabitants of the lowest heaven are standing until the Day of Resurrection saying: Glorified be the Possessor of sovereignty and dominion. And the inhabitants of the second heaven are bowing until the Day of Resurrection saying: Glorified be the Possessor of might and power. And the inhabitants of the third heaven are prostrating until the Day of Resurrection saying: Glorified be the Living who does not die.' And know that these expressions are intended to explain the different ranks of the angels in the stations of knowledge, love, and servitude.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage covers the divine name al-Subbūḥ and the meaning of tasbīḥ. The author states that al-Subbūḥ is not in the Qur'an but the community agrees on using it for God. It is derived from 'subḥāna llāh'. The form fuʿūl (with doubled ʿayn) typically has fatha on the fā, like safūd and tannūr, except three words: subbūḥ, quddūs, and dhū rūḥ (for one type of medicine). Then the author explains that tasbīḥ is the verbal noun, and subḥān is a noun used like a verbal noun. Two interpretations of tasbīḥ: first, the Prophet explained it as 'removing God from all evil' (tanzīh). Second, it may derive from sabḥ in the land (to travel). If we take it as tanzīh, then the best dhikr is declaring God free from any imperfection. The author then cites verses about angels tasbīḥ and a hadith describing the angels in different heavens continuously glorifying God, explaining that these expressions indicate the different ranks of angels in knowledge, love, and servitude.

Key Terms
سبوح
سبحان
فعول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a name must be explicitly in the Qur'an to be used for Allah.
Correction: The author clarifies that al-Subbūḥ is not in the Qur'an but the community has agreed on its usage, showing that consensus can establish a divine name.

Checkpoint
How many letters break the normal fuʿūl pattern according to the passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The name al-Subbūḥ is a community-agreed name of Allah with an irregular Arabic pattern shared only with quddūs and dhū rūḥ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The name al-Subbūḥ is unanimously agreed upon, yet it is not in the Qur'an. Have you ever dismissed a truth about Allah simply because it wasn't explicitly in a verse? What will you do this week to embrace the full scope of what the community has agreed upon about His attributes?
?
Al-Subbūḥ reminds you that Allah is utterly perfect and beyond any flaw. Think of a recent moment when you complained about your life—did your complaint imply that Allah was not perfect in His plan? How can you use the word 'subḥāna llāh' to reorient your heart when frustration arises?

Two Interpretations of Tasbīḥ
The author explains the linguistics of tasbīḥ and presents two exegetical interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of tasbīḥ: exaltation (tanzīh) and motion, and how they are derived.

Core Idea
Tasbīḥ means both declaring God free from imperfection and moving about in the land.

The word tasbīḥ is derived from the noun subḥān and is commonly understood as exaltation. However, the author presents a second interpretation based on the root 's-b-ḥ' meaning to travel. This lesson explains both meanings and their bases in the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on tasbīḥ definition
Start by reading the Arabic text that defines tasbīḥ as a verbal noun and introduces the two interpretations.
2
First interpretation: tanzīh
The Prophet explained tasbīḥ as 'removing Allah from every evil', i.e., declaring Him free from imperfections. This is the primary meaning.
3
Second interpretation: motion
The author suggests an alternative derivation from 'sabḥa' (to travel), citing the Qur'anic verse about 'prolonged travel' (sabḥan ṭawīlan).

Angels' Tasbīḥ and Their Ranks
The author cites scriptural evidence for angels' tasbīḥ and connects it to spiritual hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the angels' continuous tasbīḥ reflects their different ranks in knowledge, love, and servitude.

Core Idea
Angels glorify Allah in different postures according to their spiritual station.

After explaining the meaning of tasbīḥ, the author cites verses about angels glorifying Allah and a hadith describing the inhabitants of the heavens in different postures (standing, bowing, prostrating) saying specific glorifications. This lesson explores how these expressions indicate the varying degrees of angels in knowledge, love, and servitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about angels' tasbīḥ
Read the Arabic section that begins after the two interpretations, focusing on the angels' glorification and the hadith.
2
Identify the Qur'anic references
The author quotes verses about angels glorifying night and day, and the story of Adam. Locate these in the passage.
3
Analyze the hadith about heavenly inhabitants
The hadith describes three heavens with different postures and formulas. Notice how each posture (standing, bowing, prostrating) corresponds to a specific formula.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that these expressions explain the different ranks of angels in knowledge, love, and servitude. Reflect on how the postures and formulas might reflect these ranks.

Are you content with standing, or are you striving to bow and prostrate in humility? Identify one specific way to increase your servitude (ubudiyya) this month.

The Divine Name al-Tahir: Pure from All Defects
Definition of the name al-Tahir with Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Tahir (the Pure) and how it encompasses both physical and moral purity, supported by Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Allah is absolutely pure, beyond every physical and moral imperfection.

This lesson examines one of the beautiful names of Allah: al-Tahir (the Pure). The author explains that purity (taharah) in the Quran refers not only to physical cleanliness but also to spiritual and moral purity. Understanding this name deepens our appreciation of Allah's transcendence and absolute perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then review the English translation. Notice how the author defines the name using two key terms: munazzah (transcendent) and mubarra' (acquitted).
These terms emphasize complete removal of defects.
2
Identify the two dimensions of purity
The passage distinguishes between purification from physical impurities and purification from blameworthy attributes. Reflect on how both are related to Allah.
This dual usage is rooted in Quranic language.
3
Examine the Quranic verses
Look at the two verses cited: one about the purification of the Prophet's household, and one calling polytheists 'impure.' Consider how these verses illustrate the concept of moral purity.
The second verse shows that shirk is a spiritual impurity.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Understand that calling Allah 'al-Tahir' affirms His absolute transcendence above any deficiency or fault, whether physical or moral.
This name complements other names like al-Quddus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الحادي والعشرون : الطاهر. ومعناه : المنزه عن النقائص ، المبرأ عن العيوب ، فإن لفظ الطهارة كما ورد في البراءة عن القاذورات الحسية ، فكذلك ورد في البراءة عن الصفات الذميمة ، قال تعالى في صفة أهل بيت النبي : ( ويطهركم تطهيرا ) وسمى الصفات المذمومة نجاسة ، فقال : ( إنما المشركون نجس )
The twenty-first name: al-Tahir (the Pure). And its meaning: the one who is transcendent (munazzah) from defects, acquitted (mubarra') from faults. For the word taharah (purity) is used, just as it is used for acquittal from physical impurities, likewise it is used for acquittal from blameworthy attributes. Allah said concerning the description of the People of the Prophet's household: 'And purify you with a thorough purification (tathiran).' And He called blameworthy attributes impurity (najasah), so He said: 'Indeed the polytheists are impure (najis).'
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage defines one of God's beautiful names, al-Tahir. It explains that the term 'taharah' (purity) applies both to physical cleanliness and to moral/spiritual purity. The author cites two Quranic verses: one about the purification of the Prophet's household, and another where polytheists are described as impure, showing that blameworthy attributes are considered impurity. Thus, God is absolutely pure from any defect or imperfection.

Key Terms
طاهر
تنزيه
طهارة
نجاسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Purity (taharah) only refers to physical cleanliness.
Correction: The Quran uses taharah for moral and spiritual purity as well, as seen in the verse about the Prophet's household and the designation of polytheists as impure due to their shirk.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of the divine name al-Tahir?
A) The Forgiving
B) The Pure, transcendent from all defects and faults
C) The Strong
D) The Knowing

Key Takeaway
Allah is al-Tahir, absolutely pure from every physical and moral imperfection, as affirmed by the Quranic usage of taharah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah is free from any imperfection, even when your life seems full of injustice and suffering? How does the certainty that Allah is al-Tahir change your response to trials?
?
If Allah is absolutely pure from blameworthy attributes, then what does it mean for your own accountability for sin? Are you willing to admit that your sins are a form of impurity that only His purification can cleanse? What practical step will you take today to seek His purification?
?
Consider the verse that calls polytheists impure. Is there any form of shirk in your heart that you need to repent from, so that you may be purified? How will you actively purify your intention (niyyah) today?

The Divine Name al-Ṭāhir: Transcendent Above All Imperfection
Definition of al-Ṭāhir with Quranic evidence for dual usage of ṭahāra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine name al-Ṭāhir signifies Allah's transcendence above all defects and that the concept of purity (ṭahāra) includes both physical and moral innocence.

Core Idea
Al-Ṭāhir means Allah is absolutely free from every imperfection and blameworthy attribute.

The name al-Ṭāhir (The Pure) is one of Allah's beautiful names. At first glance, 'purity' might seem to refer only to physical cleanliness, but the author shows that the Quran uses the same word for moral and spiritual purity. This lesson explores the definition and scriptural basis for this name, helping you appreciate the depth of its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage quietly, then the literal English translation. Notice how the author begins by stating the name and immediately gives its meaning.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the definition
The author defines al-Ṭāhir as 'the one transcendent above imperfections, absolved of defects'. This sets the central meaning.
These two phrases complement each other.
3
Note the two applications of ṭahāra
The author explains that ṭahāra is used for both physical purity and moral purity, citing Quranic verses for each.
The same word covers both domains.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
The first verse (33:33) speaks of purifying the Prophet's family; the second (9:28) calls polytheists 'impure'. This proves that impurity can be moral and spiritual.
This supports the extended meaning of purity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الحادي والعشرون : الطاهر. ومعناه : المنزه عن النقائص ، المبرأ عن العيوب ، فإن لفظ الطهارة كما ورد في البراءة عن القاذورات الحسية ، فكذلك ورد في البراءة عن الصفات الذميمة ، قال تعالى في صفة أهل بيت النبي : ( ويطهركم تطهيرا ) وسمى الصفات المذمومة نجاسة ، فقال : ( إنما المشركون نجس )
The twenty-first name: al-Ṭāhir. Its meaning: the one who is transcendent above imperfections, absolved of defects. For the word ṭahāra (purity) is used for innocence from physical filth, and similarly it is used for innocence from blameworthy attributes. Allah Almighty said describing the family of the Prophet: 'and purify you with a thorough purification' and He called blameworthy attributes impurity, saying: 'Indeed the polytheists are impure.'
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Ṭāhir (The Pure). The author explains that it signifies being free from all deficiencies and faults. The concept of purity (ṭahāra) originally applies to physical cleanliness, but is also used metaphorically for moral and spiritual purity. The author quotes two Quranic verses: one about the purification of the Prophet's household (Sūrat al-Aḥzāb 33:33) and another calling the polytheists impure (Sūrat al-Tawba 9:28). This establishes that the term ṭahāra extends to moral purity, and thus al-Ṭāhir means God is utterly free from any defect or imperfection.

Key Terms
الطاهر
الطهارة
المنزه
المبرأ
القاذورات الحسية
الصفات الذميمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That purity (ṭahāra) only refers to physical cleanliness.
Correction: The author shows that ṭahāra is also used for moral and spiritual purity, as seen in the Quranic references to the purification of the Prophet's family and the impurity of polytheists.

Checkpoint
