A) The one who purifies others.
B) The pure in physical body.
C) The transcendent above defects and imperfections.
D) The one who loves purity.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ṭāhir means Allah is absolutely free from every imperfection and blameworthy attribute, and the word ṭahāra in the Quran covers both physical and moral purity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is absolutely pure and free from all defects, what does that mean for your own pursuit of purity? Are you striving to purify yourself from blameworthy attributes, or are you content with merely outward cleanliness? What specific blameworthy attribute will you work to remove from your character starting today?
?
The verse declares polytheists as impure—not physically, but spiritually. Does your heart harbor any form of shirk (like showing off or loving worldly status) that makes you spiritually impure? How will you cleanse yourself of it, knowing that Allah is al-Ṭāhir and loves those who purify themselves?
?
Reflect on the fact that Allah's purity is absolute—He has no imperfections whatsoever. How does this contrast with your own constant need for purification and repentance? Does this realization drive you to rely on Him more in your struggle against sin? What is one concrete step you can take today to seek His purification?

The Divine Name al-Ṭayyib (The Good)
Al-Ṭayyib defined, distinguished from al-Ṭāhir, and linked to benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Ṭayyib, how it differs from al-Ṭāhir, and why it emphasizes both purity and beneficence.

Core Idea
Al-Ṭayyib means pure and beneficial — all good comes from Him.

In this lesson, we explore the twenty-second divine name: al-Ṭayyib (The Good). The author begins with a prophetic hadith that links God’s goodness to the acceptance of only good deeds. He then carefully distinguishes this name from al-Ṭāhir (The Pure), showing that al-Ṭayyib adds the idea of benefit. Through a simple analogy of a pleasant fragrance, he explains that God is not only free from defects but also the source of all benefits.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the definition
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that the author first cites the hadith: 'Indeed Allah is good and accepts only what is good.' Then read the translation.
The hadith sets the foundation for the name.
2
Identify the distinction between al-Ṭāhir and al-Ṭayyib
The author gives a clear difference. Find the two terms and understand what each implies. Al-Ṭāhir is free from defect, while al-Ṭayyib adds benefit.
The analogy of fragrance helps illustrate the added benefit.
3
Reflect on the implication for Allah
The passage concludes that for Allah, al-Ṭayyib means He is transcendent above defects and all benefits emanate from Him. Think about what this means for how we relate to Him.
This name encourages both reverence (for His purity) and hope (for His goodness).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثاني والعشرون : الطيب . قال عليه السلام : ( إن الله طيب لا يقبل إلا الطيب )، والفرق بينه وبين الطاهر : أن الطاهر هو المبرأ عن العيب ، أما الطيب فهو الذي تحصل معنى الطهارة فيه ، مع أنه يكون منتفعاً به ، لأن الطيب هو الذي تحصل منه رائحة طيبة ، وذلك نوع نفع ، فالطيب في حق سبحانه : أنه المنزه من جميع العيوب وتكون جميع المنافع صادرة عنه
The twenty-second name: al-Ṭayyib. The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed Allah is good (ṭayyib) and accepts only what is good.' The difference between it and al-Ṭāhir is that al-Ṭāhir is the one absolved of defect, while al-Ṭayyib is that in which the meaning of purity is present, along with being beneficial. For al-ṭayyib (the good/pleasant) is that from which a pleasant fragrance is obtained, and that is a kind of benefit. So al-Ṭayyib with respect to the Exalted is: that He is transcendent above all defects and all benefits emanate from Him.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage defines the name al-Ṭayyib (The Good) and distinguishes it from al-Ṭāhir (The Pure). The author cites the hadith: 'Verily Allah is Good and accepts only what is good.' The key difference: al-Ṭāhir signifies mere freedom from defects, while al-Ṭayyib includes that freedom plus benefit. The author draws an analogy: something good (ṭayyib) gives a pleasant fragrance, which is a benefit. Thus for God, al-Ṭayyib means He is free from all defects and also all benefits originate from Him.

Key Terms
الطيب
الفرق بينه وبين الطاهر
المنزه
المنافع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ṭāhir and al-Ṭayyib are synonyms.
Correction: The author clarifies that al-Ṭayyib carries the additional connotation of beneficence.

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between al-Ṭāhir and al-Ṭayyib according to the passage?
A) al-Ṭāhir refers to physical purity while al-Ṭayyib refers to moral purity.
B) al-Ṭāhir is only for God while al-Ṭayyib can be used for creation.
C) al-Ṭayyib includes both purity and benefit.
D) al-Ṭāhir is more comprehensive than al-Ṭayyib.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ṭayyib means that Allah is both absolutely pure and the source of all benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a blessing, do you truly attribute it to Allah as the source, or do you sometimes feel a sense of personal entitlement? How can you train your heart to see every good thing as coming from al-Ṭayyib alone?
?
The hadith says Allah accepts only what is good (ṭayyib). Examine the deeds you performed today: were they pure in intention and beneficial in outcome? What specific actions can you change tomorrow to make them more acceptable to al-Ṭayyib?
?
You believe Allah is pure from all defects, but do you live as if He is also the giver of every benefit? When something good happens, do you immediately thank Him, or do you look elsewhere? Commit now to a daily practice of saying 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' with full awareness that the benefit came from Him.

The Names al-Fard and al-Witr: Definitions and Hadith
Definitions of al-Fard and al-Witr and the hadith on God's love for oddness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-Fard and al-Witr, the related hadith, and the mathematical meaning of oddness as it relates to divine attributes.

Core Idea
Al-Fard and al-Witr both signify God's unique oneness, reflected in the mathematical concept of indivisibility.

The author begins by introducing two divine names: al-Fard (the Individual) and al-Witr (the Odd). He provides brief definitions and cites a prophetic hadith that Allah is Witr and loves oddness. Then he clarifies that these terms are similar and explains the mathematical meaning of oddness, which sets the stage for the proofs of its superiority that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions and hadith
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the definitions of al-Fard and al-Witr and their opposites. Note the hadith quoted.
The hadith establishes the divine association with oddness.
2
Understand the mathematical meaning
The author explains the term according to mathematicians: indivisible into two equal halves. Reflect on how this relates to God's oneness.
3
Connect to divine attributes
Ponder why God being 'odd' is significant. It underscores His uniqueness and self-sufficiency, unlike created things which often come in pairs.
This connection will be deepened in the proofs lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثالث والعشرون : الفرد . وهو في مقابلة الزوج . الإسم الرابع والعشرون : الوتر . وهو في مقابلة الشفع . قال عليه السلام : ( إن الله وتر يحب الوتر ) واعلم أن الوتر والفرد متقاربان . ومعناه عند أهل الحساب أنه : الذي لا ينقسم نصفين متساويين . واعلم أن الوتر أشرف من الشفع ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الفردانية صفة الله سبحانه ، والزوجية صفة الممكنات ، وصفة الحق - سبحانه ـ أشرف من صفة الخلق . والثاني : إن كل زوج فهو محتاج إلى الواحد ( وهو فرد ) والواحد غني عن كل الأعداد ، فالفرد أشرف من الزوج ، والثالث : إن الفرد مشتمل على الزوج والفرد معاً ، لأن العدد الفرد يكون أحد قسميه زوجاً والآخر فرداً . أما الزوج ، فإما أن يكون كلا قسميه زوجاً ، أو يكون كلاهما فرداً ، والمشتمل على النوعين أشرف من المشتمل على أحدهما . الرابع : إن الفرد لا يقبل التنصيف ، والزوج يقبله ، وعدم قبول القسمة قوة، وقبولها ضعف. الخامس : إن جميع الأعداد إنما تتكون عن الواحد، لأن الواحد إذا ضم إليه واحد آخر حصل إثنان ، وإذا ضم إليهما واحد آخر حصلت الثلاثة ، وقس الباقي عليه ، فيثبت أن الواحد علة لجميع الأعداد . لكن الواحد فرد . فالفرد أشرف . السادس : إن الوتر غالب . وذلك لأن الشفع والوتر إذا اجتمعا حصل الوتر ، لا الشفع . فدل على أن الوتر غالب على الشفع ، فكان الوتر
The twenty-third name: al-Fard (the Individual). It is the opposite of the pair (al-zawj). The twenty-fourth name: al-Witr (the Odd). It is the opposite of the even (al-shaf‘). The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed Allah is odd (witr) and loves oddness.' Know that al-Witr and al-Fard are close in meaning. Its meaning according to mathematicians is: that which cannot be divided into two equal halves. Know that al-Witr is nobler than al-Shaf‘, and there are proofs for this: First: Individuality (al-fardāniyya) is an attribute of God, while pairing (al-zawjiyya) is an attribute of contingent things, and the attribute of the Truth, exalted, is nobler than the attribute of creation. Second: Every pair is in need of the one (which is individual), and the one is independent of all numbers, so the individual is nobler than the pair. Third: The individual contains both the pair and the individual together, because an odd number has one part as even and the other as odd. As for the even number, either both parts are even or both are odd, and that which contains both types is nobler than that which contains only one. Fourth: The individual does not accept division into halves, while the pair accepts it; non-divisibility is strength, divisibility is weakness. Fifth: All numbers are generated from the one, because if you add another one to the one you get two, and if you add another one to them you get three, and so on; so the one is the cause of all numbers. But the one is individual, so the individual is nobler. Sixth: Al-Witr is predominant. This is because if the even and the odd combine, the result is odd, not even. This shows that the odd dominates the even, so al-Witr is ... (text cuts off)
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage covers two divine names: al-Fard (the Individual) and al-Witr (the Odd). The author first defines each briefly, then cites a hadith that God is Witt (Odd) and loves oddness. He then notes that al-Witr and al-Fard are similar, and defines the mathematical concept of odd as indivisible into two equal halves. The main focus is proving that al-Witr (odd) is nobler (ashraf) than al-Shaf‘ (even). He provides six proofs, each building on the idea of oneness and self-sufficiency. The proofs: 1) Individuality is an attribute of God, pairing of contingent things; 2) Every pair needs the individual; 3) The individual contains both pair and individual, while the even only contains one type; 4) The individual cannot be divided (strength), the pair can be divided (weakness); 5) All numbers proceed from the one, which is individual; 6) The odd predominates when combined with even. The sixth proof is cut off at the end of the page, but the argument is clear. The passage thus exalts the names al-Fard and al-Witr as signifying God's unique oneness and self-sufficiency.

Key Terms
الفرد
الوتر
الزوج
الشفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that odd and even are mere mathematical categories with no theological significance.
Correction: The author uses these mathematical concepts to reflect profound truths about God's unique oneness and self-sufficiency, as the hadith directly associates God with the odd.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith in this passage, what does Allah love?
A) Al-Fard (the Individual)
B) Al-Witr (the Odd)
C) Al-Zawj (the Pair)
D) Al-Shaf‘ (the Even)

Key Takeaway
Al-Fard and al-Witr denote God's indivisibility and uniqueness, as affirmed by the Prophet and mathematical definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God as al-Witr—the Odd who cannot be divided—does your heart truly surrender to His absolute oneness? Are there areas of your life where you have 'divided' your trust or worship between Him and something else? What is one step you will take today to realign your loyalty to Him alone?
?
The Prophet said that Allah loves oddness. In your daily routines, do you seek the odd—the unique moments of sincerity—or do you prefer the comfort of following the crowd? How can you bring more oddness (wholehearted devotion) into your prayer or charity today?

Six Proofs for the Superiority of al-Witr
Six proofs that al-Witr is nobler than al-Shaf‘, with partial sixth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six rational and symbolic proofs demonstrating that al-Witr (odd) is nobler than al-Shaf‘ (even).

Core Idea
Oddness is nobler than evenness because it reflects God's self-sufficiency, indivisibility, and causal priority.

After establishing the definitions, the author delves into demonstrating that al-Witr (the Odd) is superior to al-Shaf‘ (the Even). He presents six proofs, each drawing from mathematics, causality, and divine attributes. The text cuts off mid-way through the sixth proof, but the arguments are clear and build a compelling case for the nobility of oddness.

Guided Path
1
Read the six proofs sequentially
Read the Arabic text listing the six proofs. Notice how each proof is numbered and builds on the previous one.
The sixth proof is incomplete; focus on the logical structure.
2
Analyze each proof's logic
For each proof, identify the premise and conclusion. For example, the first proof: God's attribute (individuality) is nobler than creatures' attribute (pairing).
Some proofs are theological, others mathematical.
3
Reflect on the cumulative argument
Consider how these proofs collectively elevate the concept of oddness as symbolizing divine perfection.
The incomplete sixth proof about predominance is a fitting conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثالث والعشرون : الفرد . وهو في مقابلة الزوج . الإسم الرابع والعشرون : الوتر . وهو في مقابلة الشفع . قال عليه السلام : ( إن الله وتر يحب الوتر ) واعلم أن الوتر والفرد متقاربان . ومعناه عند أهل الحساب أنه : الذي لا ينقسم نصفين متساويين . واعلم أن الوتر أشرف من الشفع ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الفردانية صفة الله سبحانه ، والزوجية صفة الممكنات ، وصفة الحق - سبحانه ـ أشرف من صفة الخلق . والثاني : إن كل زوج فهو محتاج إلى الواحد ( وهو فرد ) والواحد غني عن كل الأعداد ، فالفرد أشرف من الزوج ، والثالث : إن الفرد مشتمل على الزوج والفرد معاً ، لأن العدد الفرد يكون أحد قسميه زوجاً والآخر فرداً . أما الزوج ، فإما أن يكون كلا قسميه زوجاً ، أو يكون كلاهما فرداً ، والمشتمل على النوعين أشرف من المشتمل على أحدهما . الرابع : إن الفرد لا يقبل التنصيف ، والزوج يقبله ، وعدم قبول القسمة قوة، وقبولها ضعف. الخامس : إن جميع الأعداد إنما تتكون عن الواحد، لأن الواحد إذا ضم إليه واحد آخر حصل إثنان ، وإذا ضم إليهما واحد آخر حصلت الثلاثة ، وقس الباقي عليه ، فيثبت أن الواحد علة لجميع الأعداد . لكن الواحد فرد . فالفرد أشرف . السادس : إن الوتر غالب . وذلك لأن الشفع والوتر إذا اجتمعا حصل الوتر ، لا الشفع . فدل على أن الوتر غالب على الشفع ، فكان الوتر
The twenty-third name: al-Fard (the Individual). It is the opposite of the pair (al-zawj). The twenty-fourth name: al-Witr (the Odd). It is the opposite of the even (al-shaf‘). The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed Allah is odd (witr) and loves oddness.' Know that al-Witr and al-Fard are close in meaning. Its meaning according to mathematicians is: that which cannot be divided into two equal halves. Know that al-Witr is nobler than al-Shaf‘, and there are proofs for this: First: Individuality (al-fardāniyya) is an attribute of God, while pairing (al-zawjiyya) is an attribute of contingent things, and the attribute of the Truth, exalted, is nobler than the attribute of creation. Second: Every pair is in need of the one (which is individual), and the one is independent of all numbers, so the individual is nobler than the pair. Third: The individual contains both the pair and the individual together, because an odd number has one part as even and the other as odd. As for the even number, either both parts are even or both are odd, and that which contains both types is nobler than that which contains only one. Fourth: The individual does not accept division into halves, while the pair accepts it; non-divisibility is strength, divisibility is weakness. Fifth: All numbers are generated from the one, because if you add another one to the one you get two, and if you add another one to them you get three, and so on; so the one is the cause of all numbers. But the one is individual, so the individual is nobler. Sixth: Al-Witr is predominant. This is because if the even and the odd combine, the result is odd, not even. This shows that the odd dominates the even, so al-Witr is ... (text cuts off)
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage covers two divine names: al-Fard (the Individual) and al-Witr (the Odd). The author first defines each briefly, then cites a hadith that God is Witt (Odd) and loves oddness. He then notes that al-Witr and al-Fard are similar, and defines the mathematical concept of odd as indivisible into two equal halves. The main focus is proving that al-Witr (odd) is nobler (ashraf) than al-Shaf‘ (even). He provides six proofs, each building on the idea of oneness and self-sufficiency. The proofs: 1) Individuality is an attribute of God, pairing of contingent things; 2) Every pair needs the individual; 3) The individual contains both pair and individual, while the even only contains one type; 4) The individual cannot be divided (strength), the pair can be divided (weakness); 5) All numbers proceed from the one, which is individual; 6) The odd predominates when combined with even. The sixth proof is cut off at the end of the page, but the argument is clear. The passage thus exalts the names al-Fard and al-Witr as signifying God's unique oneness and self-sufficiency.

Correction: The proofs are deeply theological, using mathematical analogies to illustrate divine attributes like self-sufficiency, causefood, and perfection.

The divine name al-Tayyib and its distinction from al-Tahir
Author defines al-Tayyib and distinguishes it from al-Tahir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Tayyib and how it differs from al-Tahir.

Core Idea
Al-Tayyib is purity with benefit; al-Tahir is purity alone.

This lesson explores the twenty-second divine name, al-Tayyib (the Good). The author distinguishes it from the name al-Tahir (the Pure), showing that while al-Tahir indicates freedom from defects, al-Tayyib adds the idea of being beneficial and the source of all good. A hadith reinforces that Allah is Tayyib and accepts only what is good.

Guided Path
1

The passage is concise; every word matters.
2

3
Apply to Allah
Reflect on how the name al-Tayyib applies to Allah: He is free from all defects and all benefits come from Him. Consider the implications for worship—Allah accepts only good deeds.
This connects the attribute to practical belief.

The Divine Names al-Fard and al-Witr and the Seven Proofs of Superiority of the Odd
Author defines al-Fard and al-Witr then gives seven proofs of superiority of al-Witr over the even.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names al-Fard and al-Witr, and the seven rational arguments that demonstrate the superiority of the odd over the even, as presented by the author.

Core Idea
The odd number's superiority points to the divine attributes of uniqueness and independence.

This lesson explores two closely related names of Allah: al-Fard (the Unique) and al-Witr (the Odd). After defining these terms and citing a relevant hadith, the author presents seven rational proofs drawn from arithmetic to demonstrate that al-Witr is superior to al-Shaf' (the even). These proofs are not merely mathematical; they reflect deeper theological truths about Allah's attributes of oneness and self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions and the hadith
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the definitions of al-Fard and al-Witr and the prophetic saying that Allah is Witr and loves the odd.
Notice that the author notes al-Witr and al-Fard are close in meaning.
2
Understand the arithmetic meaning
The author gives the arithmetic definition of odd: not divisible into two equal halves. This sets the stage for the proofs that follow.
3
Go through each of the seven proofs
Read each proof carefully, one by one. Each proof is a distinct argument. Try to understand the logical reasoning behind each.
The proofs build on concepts like need, composition, divisibility, generation, dominance, and necessity.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
After covering the proofs, consider how each proof connects to the divine attribute of being odd (al-Witr) and why these mathematical observations point to Allah's perfection.
The common misunderstanding is to see these as mere number games; they are intended to illustrate divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثالث والعشرون : الفرد . وهو في مقابلة الزوج .
الإسم الرابع والعشرون : الوتر . وهو في مقابلة الشفع . قال عليه السلام : ( إن الله وتر يجب الوتر ) واعلم أن الوتر والفرد متقاربان . ومعناه عند أهل الحساب أنه : الذي لا ينقسم نصفين متساويين . واعلم أن الوتر أشرف من الشفع ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الفردانية صفة الله سبحانه ، والزوجية صفة الممكنات ، وصفة الحق سبحانه أشرف من صفة الخلق . والثاني : إن كل زوج فهو محتاج إلى الواحد ( وهو فرد ) والواحد غني عن كل الأعداد ، فالفرد أشرف من الزوج . والثالث : إن الفرد مشتمل على الزوج والفرد معاً ، لأن العدد الفرد يكون أحد قسميه زوجاً والآخر فرداً . أما الزوج ، فإما أن يكون كلا قسميه زوجاً ، أو يكون كلاهما فرداً ، والمشتمل على النوعين أشرف من المشتمل على أحدهما . الرابع : إن الفرد لا يقبل التنصيف ، والزوج يقبله ، وعدم قبول القسمة قوة، وقبولها ضعف. الخامس : إن جميع الأعداد إنما تتكون عن الواحد، لأن الواحد إذا ضم إليه واحد آخر حصل إثنان ، وإذا ضم إليهما واحد آخر حصلت الثلاثة ، وقس الباقي عليه ، فيثبت أن الواحد علة لجميع الأعداد . لكن الواحد فرد . فالفرد أشرف . السادس : إن الوتر غالب . وذلك لأن الشفع والوتر إذا اجتمعا حصل الوتر ، لا الشفع . فدل على أن الوتر غالب على الشفع ، فكان الوتر أشرف . السابع : إن الوحدة لازمة لجميع مراتب الأعداد ، فإن كل مرتبة من مراتب الأعداد إذا أخذت من حيث إنها تلك المرتبة كانت واحدة ، كقولك عشرة واحدة ، ومائة واحدة ، والوحدة وتر . فالوتر لازم الجميع مراتب الأعداد ، والزوج ليس كذلك . فكان الوتر أفضل من الشفع . والله أعلم
The twenty-third name: al-Fard (the Unique). It is the opposite of the pair (al-zawj). The twenty-fourth name: al-Witr (the Odd). It is the opposite of the even (al-shaf'). He (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed Allah is Witr (Odd) and loves the odd (witr).' Know that al-Witr and al-Fard are close in meaning. Their meaning according to the people of arithmetic is that which is not divisible into two equal halves. Know that al-Witr is more excellent than al-Shaf'. Evidence for this is from several aspects: First: that uniqueness (al-fardaniyya) is an attribute of Allah the Exalted, and evenness (al-zawjiyya) is an attribute of contingent things, and the attribute of the Truth the Exalted is more excellent than the attribute of creation. Second: every even (zawj) is in need of the one (which is a fard/odd), while the one is independent of all numbers, so the odd is more excellent than the even. Third: the odd contains both even and odd together, because in an odd number one of its two parts is even and the other is odd. As for the even, either both its parts are even or both are odd, and that which contains both types is more excellent than that which contains only one. Fourth: the odd does not accept division into halves, while the even accepts it, and non-acceptance of division is strength, while acceptance is weakness. Fifth: all numbers are only generated from the one, because when one is added to another one, two results; and when another one is added to them, three results; and so on; thus it is established that the one is the cause of all numbers. But the one is odd, so the odd is more excellent. Sixth: the odd is dominant. That is because when the even and odd come together, the result is odd, not even. So this indicates that the odd dominates the even, thus the odd is more excellent. Seventh: oneness (al-wahda) is necessary for all ranks of numbers, for every rank of numbers when taken as being that rank is one, such as you say: ten is one, hundred is one, and oneness is odd. So the odd is necessary for all ranks of numbers, while the even is not so. Thus the odd is superior to the even. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage defines two divine names: al-Fard (the Unique) and al-Witr (the Odd). It first gives brief definitions, then cites a hadith that Allah is Witr and loves the witr. It notes that al-Witr and al-Fard are close in meaning. Then it explains the arithmetic meaning of odd (not divisible into two equal halves). The main focus is proving that al-Witr is superior to al-Shaf' (the even) through seven rational arguments. Each argument is presented in order: (1) uniqueness is an attribute of Allah, evenness of contingent things; (2) the even needs the odd, the odd is independent; (3) the odd contains both even and odd while the even does not; (4) the odd is indivisible, the even is divisible; (5) all numbers originate from the one, which is odd; (6) combining even and odd gives odd, showing dominance; (7) oneness is necessary for all numbers and oneness is odd. The passage concludes with 'And Allah knows best.' This is a multi-proof demonstration of the superiority of the odd, reflecting a divine attribute.

الوتر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These seven proofs are just numerical curiosities with no theological depth.
Correction: Each proof is deliberately crafted to reflect a divine attribute: uniqueness, independence, strength, causality, dominance, and necessity. They demonstrate why the odd (al-Witr) is a fitting name for Allah and why it is superior to the even.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is dominant over the even.

C) Because it is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The seven proofs demonstrate that the attribute of being odd (al-Witr) reflects Allah's uniqueness, self-sufficiency, strength, and perfection, making it superior to evenness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider your own life: do you see the 'evenness' of your desires, always needing a partner (like the even number needing the one)? Are you clinging to something that is divisible — a job, a relationship, a reputation — as if it is independent? What would it mean for you to truly embody the attribute of al-Witr in your trust in Allah alone?
?
The odd number dominates the even when they come together. In your daily interactions, which 'even' aspects of your life — your habits, your comforts, your plans — are you allowing to dominate your 'odd' core of tawheed? What one action can you take tonight to assert the dominance of your faith over your worldly attachments?

Names Indicating Power: al-Qadir and al-Qadir
Classification and linguistic analysis of two divine names of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth category of divine names (power) and the linguistic distinction between al-Qadir and al-Qadir based on Arabic morphological patterns.

Core Idea
Arabic morphology reveals that al-Qadir denotes inherent power, not just ability.

We now turn to the fourth category of divine names: those indicating power. The author presents two key names, al-Qadir and al-Qadir, and then dives into Arabic morphology to show how patterns like fa‘īl convey an inherent, necessary attribute. This lesson connects divine names to linguistic principles, deepening your appreciation of the precision of divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through before interpreting.
Notice that the passage has two main parts: listing names and linguistic analysis.
2
Identify the two names and their Quranic evidence
Find where the author mentions al-Qadir and al-Qadir. Note the Quranic verses cited for each. Ask yourself: why does the author point out that al-Qadir is not among the 99 names?
The verses show both names are used in the Quran.
3
Analyze the linguistic patterns
Focus on the discussion of patterns: fu‘āl (فعال), fa‘īl (فعيل), and fa‘ūl (فعول). Understand what each pattern conveys: fu‘āl for intensity of action, fa‘īl/fa‘ūl for inherent attribute.
Examples like haddād (blacksmith) and qadīr (All-Powerful) illustrate the patterns.
4
Connect linguistics to theology
Reflect on how the linguistic analysis distinguishes al-Qadir from al-Qadir. Why does fa‘īl imply a stronger, more inherent attribute?
This helps understand why al-Qadir is a more intensive name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع من أسماء الله : الأسماء الدالة على القدرة : وهي كثيرة :
فالأول : القادر . قال تعالى : (قل : هو القادر على أن يبعث عليكم عذاباً من فوقكم ) وقال تعالى : ( فنعم القادرون )
الثاني: القدير . وهو غير وارد في الأسماء التسعة والتسعين إلا أنه كثير الورود في القرآن . قال تعالى : ( تبارك الذي بيده الملك ، وهو على كل شيء قدير )
واعلم أنه قد يوجد في اللغة العربية أمثلة مختلفة مأخوذة من الأفعال جعلت نحوتاً، وكان المقصود من بعض تلك الأمثلة تأكيد الفعل والمبالغة في الوصف، ومن بعضها كون ذلك الفعل كالصفة اللازمة له ، فمن ذلك : بناء فعال . إذا أراد تكثير الفعل ، ولهذا السبب أخذوا أسماء الصناع من هذا المثال كقولهم : حداد وبناء ونجار وفلاح وخياط ليدل ذلك على أن تكون هذه من هؤلاء الفاعلين. ومن ذلك صرفهم لفظ فاعل إلى فعيل مرة ، وفعول أخرى ، إذا أرادوا أن يجعلوا الصفة المضافة إلى الموصوف كالجية [؟] اللازمة والخلقة اللازمة ، وذلك مثل صرفهم لفظ قادر إلى قدير ، وخابر إلى خبير ، وناصر إلى نصير ، وصرفهم : صابر إلى صبور ، وشاكر إلى شكور ، وغافر إلى غفور . والسبب في ذلك : أن كل من فعل فعلاً ، قل ذلك الفعل أو كثر ،
The fourth section of the names of Allah: the names indicating power (al-qudra). They are many: The first: al-Qadir (the Powerful). Allah the Exalted said: 'Say: He is al-Qadir to send upon you a punishment from above you' (Quran 6:65) and He said: '(How) excellent are the Qadirun (the powerful ones)' (Quran 77:23). The second: al-Qadir (the All-Powerful). It is not among the ninety-nine names, but it occurs frequently in the Quran. Allah the Exalted said: 'Blessed is He in whose hand is the kingdom, and He is over all things Qadir (All-Powerful)' (Quran 67:1). Know that there exist in the Arabic language different patterns derived from verbs, made as forms (nuhāt?), and the intended meaning of some of those patterns is the emphasis of the action and exaggeration in description, and from some of them is that that action becomes like an inherent attribute for him. Among that is the pattern fu‘āl (فعال): when they intend to intensify the action, and for this reason they took the names of craftsmen from this pattern such as they say: haddād (blacksmith), bannā’ (builder), najjār (carpenter), fallāḥ (farmer), khayyāṭ (tailor), to indicate that these are among these actors. And from that is their conversion of the word fā‘il (active participle) to fa‘īl on one occasion, and fa‘ūl on another, when they intend to make the attribute added to the described one like a necessary trait and an innate characteristic, such as their conversion of the word qādir to qadīr, and khābir to khabīr, and nāṣir to naṣīr, and their conversion: ṣābir to ṣabūr, and shākir to shakūr, and ghāfir to ghafūr. The reason for that is that everyone who performs an action, whether that action is little or much...
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth category of divine names: those indicating power (qudra). It lists the first two names: al-Qadir and al-Qadir, providing Quranic evidence for each. It notes that al-Qadir, while not among the 99 names, appears frequently in the Quran. Then it provides a linguistic analysis of Arabic morphological patterns. It explains that patterns like fu‘āl (فعال) are used to intensify action (e.g., craftsmen names). Then it discusses the conversion of the active participle fā‘il to patterns fa‘īl and fa‘ūl to indicate an inherent or necessary attribute. Examples include qādir to qadīr, etc. The reason given is that everyone who does an action, whether little or much... (the passage cuts off). This analysis shows that al-Qadir implies a more intensive and inherent power than al-Qadir. The passage is a definition with linguistic justification.

القادر
القدير
القدرة
فعال
فعيل
فعول
فاعل
مبالغة
صفة لازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Qadir and al-Qadir are identical in meaning.
Correction: Linguistic analysis shows that al-Qadir (fa‘īl pattern) indicates an inherent, necessary attribute, implying a stronger and more essential power than al-Qadir (fā‘il pattern), which denotes mere ability.

Checkpoint
What does the Arabic pattern fa‘īl (فعيل) indicate according to the passage?
A) Intensity and exaggeration in action.

D) A profession.

Key Takeaway
The pattern fa‘īl (فعيل) in Arabic indicates an inherent attribute, so al-Qadir signifies Allah's power as an essential quality, making it more emphatic than al-Qadir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The linguistic distinction between قادر and قدير shows that Allah's power is essential, not accidental. Does your heart feel the weight of that? Or do you still rely on your own limited 'ability' before turning to Him?

Superiority of the Odd over the Even
Explains why odd numbers are superior based on unity necessary in all ranks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for the superiority of odd numbers over even numbers based on the necessity of unity in all numerical ranks.

Core Idea
Odd numbers are superior because unity, which is odd, is essential to every number.

This passage presents a concise logical proof for the superiority of the odd (al-witr) over the even (al-shaf'), often invoked in discussions of divine names such as al-Witr. The author argues that unity is inherent in every numerical rank, and since unity is odd, oddness is universal. Before delving into the text, consider why something as abstract as numerical parity might carry theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from premise to conclusion.
The passage begins with 'The seventh [point]' — it is part of a larger list.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the Arabic terms: الوحدة (unity), الوتر (odd), الشفع (even), الأعداد (numbers). Understand their meanings in this context.
Unity (الوحدة) is a key concept — it is not just numerical 'one' but a philosophical principle.
3
Trace the logical structure
Break down the argument: premise (unity necessary for all ranks) → example (ten one, hundred one) → intermediate conclusion (odd necessary for all ranks) → final conclusion (odd better than even).
The author uses the fact that every rank is 'one' in its own category.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this argument matters for theology. Allah is called al-Witr (the Odd). This passage provides a rational basis for seeking odd numbers in worship and understanding divine oneness.
The argument connects abstract mathematics to divine attributes.

أشرف . السابع : إن الوحدة لازمة لجميع مراتب الأعداد ، فإن كل مرتبة من مراتب الأعداد إذا أخذت من حيث إنها تلك المرتبة كانت واحدة ، كقولك عشرة واحدة ، ومائة واحدة ، والوحدة وتر . فالوتر لازم الجميع مراتب الأعداد ، والزوج ليس كذلك . فكان الوتر أفضل من الشفع . والله أعلم
The seventh [point]: Unity is necessary for all ranks of numbers. For every rank of numbers, when taken in terms of that rank, is one, as you say 'ten one,' 'hundred one,' and unity is odd. So the odd is necessary for all ranks of numbers, while the even is not. Thus the odd is better than the even. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument establishing the superiority of odd numbers over even numbers, likely in the context of divine names (such as al-Witr). The author states that unity (al-wahda) is necessary for all ranks of numbers. Every rank, when considered as that specific rank, is singular (e.g., ten as a ten is one ten, and hundred as a hundred is one hundred). Unity itself is odd (witr), so the odd is intrinsic to all numerical ranks, whereas the even is not. Consequently, the odd is better (afdal) than the even. The passage concludes with 'Allah knows best', indicating the author's humility before divine knowledge.

The Fourth Category: Names of Power (al-Qadir and al-Qadeer)
Introduces the fourth category of divine names: names of power, listing al-Qadir and al-Qadeer with Qur'anic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth category of Allah's names—names of power—and the specific names al-Qadir and al-Qadeer, including their occurrence in the Qur'an and their relative emphasis.

Core Idea
Allah's power is named in two ways: al-Qadir and al-Qadeer, the latter being more emphatic.

The author now turns to the fourth group of divine names: those that signify power (qudra). He lists two primary names—al-Qadir and al-Qadeer—and cites Qur'anic verses to ground each. Notice that al-Qadeer, while not among the commonly cited ninety-nine names, appears frequently in the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the first two names
Read the Arabic passage identifying the two names. Note that al-Qadir is mentioned first, then al-Qadeer. Observe the verses cited for each.
Al-Qadeer is not part of the famous ninety-nine names list, yet it is abundant in the Qur'an.
2
Compare the verses
The verse for al-Qadir emphasizes Allah’s power to send punishment. The verse for al-Qadeer emphasizes His power over all things. Reflect on the different contexts.
3
Prepare for the linguistic discussion
The author hints at a linguistic explanation for why both names exist. Think about why Arabic has multiple patterns for the same root.
This will be addressed in the next lesson.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع من أسماء الله : الأسماء الدالة على القدرة : وهي كثيرة : فالأول : القادر . قال تعالى : (قل : هو القادر على أن يبعث عليكم عذاباً من فوقكم ) وقال تعالى : ( فنعم القادرون ) الثاني: القدير . وهو غير وارد في الأسماء التسعة والتسعين إلا أنه كثير الورود في القرآن . قال تعالى : تبارك الذي بيده الملك ، وهو على كل شيء قدير ) واعلم أنه قد يوجد في اللغة العربية أمثلة مختلفة مأخوذة من الأفعال جعلت نحوتاً، وكان المقصود من بعض تلك الأمثلة تأكيد الفعل والمبالغة في الوصف، ومن بعضها كون ذلك الفعل كالصفة اللازمة له ، فمن ذلك : بناء فعال . إذا أراد تكثير الفعل ، ولهذا السبب أخذوا أسماء الصناع من هذا المثال كقولهم : حداد وبناء ونجار وفلاح وخياط ليدل ذلك على أن تكون هذه من هؤلاء الفاعلين. ومن ذلك صرفهم لفظ فاعل إلى فعيل مرة ، وفعول أخرى ، إذا أرادوا أن يجعلوا الصفة المضافة إلى الموصوف كالجية اللازمة والخلقة اللازمة ، وذلك مثل صرفهم لفظ قادر إلى قدير ، وخابر إلى خبير ، وناصر إلى نصير ، وصرفهم : صابر إلى صبور ، وشاكر إلى شكور ، وغافر إلى غفور . والسبب في ذلك : أن كل من فعل فعلاً ، قل ذلك الفعل أو كثر، ضعف فيه او قوي . فإنه يجوز أن يشتق منه اسم الفاعل ، كمن يقول : دخل فهو داخل ، وخرج فهو خارج . فإذا احتيج إلى تميز بين الفعل الذي يظهر من الفاعل مرة واحدة ، وبين الذي يظهر منه غالباً ، وكذلك إذا احتيج إلى أن يميز بين الفعل الذي يظهر منه على سبيل التكلف وبين الذي يظهر منه على سبيل الخلق والعادة ، وجب العدول إلى هذه الأمثلة ، ليتميز بواسطتها بعض هذه الأقسام عن بعض . ومما يدل على أن الفعل أوكد من الفاعل ، أنه يقال سمع فهو سامع ، ورحم فهو راحم . وأما بناء الفعيل فإنه لا يستعمل إلا عند قصد تأكيد الفعل ، لأنا إذا قلنا سميع رحيم دل ذلك على تأكيد معنى الرحمة ، وتمكن هذا الفعل من طباع الموصوف به ، كالخلق الثابت ، وكالطبع اللازم . ولهذا السبب كان لفظ القدير في القرآن أكثر من لفظ القادر ، ولفظ العليم أكثر من العالم .
The fourth category of Allah's names: the names indicating power. They are many. The first: al-Qadir. He said: 'Say: He is al-Qadir to send upon you punishment from above you' and He said: 'So how excellent are the Qadirun.' The second: al-Qadeer. It is not included in the ninety-nine names, but it is frequent in the Quran. He said: 'Blessed is He in Whose hand is the sovereignty, and He is over all things Qadeer.' Know that in the Arabic language there are various patterns derived from verbs, made as patterns. The purpose of some of these patterns is to emphasize the action and exaggerate the description, and of some to make that action like an inherent attribute. Among that is the pattern fa''al when they intend to multiply the action, and for this reason they took names of craftsmen from this pattern, like their saying: haddad, banna', najjar, fallah, khayyat, to indicate that the action is frequent from these doers. And among that is their transformation of the word fa'il into fa'il once and fa'ul another time, when they intend to make the attribute attached to the described like an inherent quality and innate disposition. That is like their transformation of the word qadir into qadeer, khabir into khabeer, nasir into naseer, and their transformation: sabir into sabur, shakir into shakur, ghafir into ghafur. The reason for this is that whoever does an action, whether little or much, weak or strong, it is permissible to derive from it the active participle (ism fa'il), like one who says: dakhala so he is dakhil, and kharaja so he is kharij. But when there is need to distinguish between an action that appears from the doer once and that which appears from him habitually, and similarly when there is need to distinguish between an action that appears from him with effort and that which appears from him as nature and custom, it becomes necessary to turn to these patterns, so that by means of them some of these categories are distinguished from others. And among what indicates that the verb is more emphatic than the active participle is that it is said: sami'a so he is sami', and rahima so he is rahim. As for the pattern fa'il, it is only used when intending to emphasize the action, because when we say sami' rahim, that indicates emphasis of the meaning of mercy and that this action is established in the nature of the described, like a fixed innate characteristic and a necessary disposition. For this reason the word qadeer occurs in the Quran more than qadir, and the word 'alim more than 'alim.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage classifies the fourth category of Allah's names: those indicating power (qudra). The author lists two names: al-Qadir and al-Qadeer. For al-Qadir, he cites two Quranic verses: 'Say: He is al-Qadir...' (6:65) and 'How excellent are the qadirun' (77:23). For al-Qadeer, he notes that it is not listed among the ninety-nine names but is frequent in the Quran, citing verse 67:1. Then he provides a detailed linguistic analysis of Arabic morphological patterns. He explains that different patterns (wazn) are used for specific purposes: pattern fa''al (e.g., haddad, banna') indicates frequency of action. Pattern fa'il and fa'ul indicate that the attribute becomes inherent and necessary (like a natural disposition). He gives examples: qadir becomes qadeer, khabir becomes khabeer, nasir becomes naseer; and sabir becomes sabur, shakir becomes shakur, ghafir becomes ghafur. The reason is to distinguish between occasional actions and habitual or inherent ones. The pattern fa'il (like sami', rahim) is specifically for emphasizing the action and showing it is deeply rooted in the character. Thus, al-Qadeer is more emphatic than al-Qadir, which is why it appears more often in the Quran.

Key Terms
القادر
القدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is not among the ninety-nine names but is frequent in the Quran.
C) It is the same as al-Qadir.
D) It is derived from a different root.

Key Takeaway
Allah's power is signified by two names: al-Qadir (mentioned in the Quran) and al-Qadeer (frequent in the Quran, though not in the 99 names).

The Arabic Patterns: Understanding the Emphatic Meaning of al-Qadeer
Explains Arabic morphological patterns used to derive divine names, showing how al-Qadeer emphasizes inherent power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Arabic morphological patterns (fa''al, fa'il, fa'ul) are used to express emphasis and inherent qualities, and why al-Qadeer is more emphatic than al-Qadir.

Core Idea
Arabic word patterns like fa'il and fa'ul turn a simple action into an inherent attribute, giving names like al-Qadeer deeper emphasis.

The author delves into Arabic linguistics to explain why Allah has two names of power. He shows that different patterns—fa''al, fa'il, fa'ul—serve to either multiply the action or make it an inherent quality. Understanding this helps us appreciate the depth of names like al-Qadeer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the patterns
Read the passage and identify the three patterns mentioned: fa''al (e.g., haddad), fa'il (e.g., qadir → qadeer), and fa'ul (e.g., sabir → sabur). Understand the purpose of each.
Pattern fa''al indicates frequency; fa'il/fa'ul indicate inherent quality.
2
Understand the need for distinction
The author explains that active participles (ism fa'il) can refer to any action, but to distinguish occasional from habitual or forced from natural actions, these patterns are used.
This is the conceptual core.
3
Recognize the emphasis of fa'il
The pattern fa'il (like sami', rahim) is specifically for emphasizing the action as an innate characteristic. Hence al-Qadeer carries more emphasis than al-Qadir.
This directly answers why al-Qadeer appears more in the Quran.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Qadeer and al-Qadir are exact synonyms with no difference in meaning.
Correction: Al-Qadeer uses the pattern fa'il which emphasizes the attribute as inherent and permanent, while al-Qadir is a simple active participle indicating power without that emphasis.

The Nuance of al-Muqtadir: Perfect Power without Hindrance
Defines al-Muqtadir and explains the nuance of the افتعل pattern compared to power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Muqtadir (the All-Able) is distinguished from al-Qadir by indicating perfect, unimpeded power, drawing on the linguistic pattern ifta'ala.

Core Idea
Al-Muqtadir means power that faces no obstacle — a perfect, unhindered ability.

The name al-Muqtadir appears in the Quran and is often translated simply as 'the All-Powerful.' But careful scholars note a deeper nuance: the Arabic pattern ifta'ala (yielding mufta'il) conveys perfection and completeness. In this passage, the author explains this distinction using the analogy of earning (kasaba vs. iktasaba), showing how the same pattern intensifies the meaning. By understanding this, we appreciate the precision of divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation once to get a sense of the argument. Notice the definition of al-Muqtadir and the Quranic citation.
The passage ends abruptly due to a corrupted line.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author mentions that some scholars treat qadara and iqtadara as synonyms, but the investigators differentiate based on the pattern ifta'ala. What does the pattern indicate?
The pattern ifta'ala typically indicates intensity or perfection.
3
Connect to the kasaba/iktasaba analogy
See how the same pattern works in another verb pair: kasaba (earn for self/others) vs. iktasaba (earn solely for self). Reflect on why evil is associated with iktisaba.
This analogy reinforces the idea that ifta'ala adds exclusivity and intensity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : المقتدر . قال تعالى : ( في مقعد صدق عند مليك مقتدر ) وروزنه مفتعل من القدرة . قال بعضهم : قدر ، واقتدر ، بمعنى واحد . وأما المحققون فقد فرقوا بينهما ، لأن بناء افتعل دلالة على كمال حال القدرة ، فقولنا : اقتدر معناه : أن الفاعل متصرف في فعله كيف شاء وأراد ، لا يعوقه عن ذلك عائق ، ونظيره قوله كسب واكتسب . فإن كسب يستعمل فيها يكسبه الإنسان لنفسه ولغيره . وأما اكتسب فهو مختص بما يفعله الإنسان لنفسه وقال تعالى : ( لها ما كسبت وعليها ما اكتسبت ) وإنما خص الخير بالكسب ، والشر بالاكتساب ، لأن الخير قد يعمله الإنسان لنفسه ولغيره ، فلا جرم لا يكون فيه الجد والرغبة كاملاً ، وأما الشر فلا يعمله إلا لنفسه ، ليلتذبه ، فلا جرم كان الجد فيه أكمل ، فلا جرم خص بلفظ الاكتساب . وأيضاً : أفعال الشريمتنع
The third: al-Muqtadir. He said: 'In a seat of truth in the presence of a Sovereign, Muqtadir.' And its pattern is mufta'il from qudra. Some said: qadara and iqtadara are the same meaning. But the investigators differentiated between them, because the pattern ifta'ala indicates perfection of the state of power. So our saying: iqtadara means that the agent acts in his action as he wills and desires, with no hindrance preventing him. Its analogue is the saying kasaba and iktasaba. For kasaba is used for what one earns for himself and others. But iktasaba is specific to what one does for himself. And He said: 'For her what she earned (kasaba) and against her what she earned (iktasaba).' Indeed, good is specified with kasaba and evil with iktisaba, because good one may do for himself and others, so inevitably the earnestness and desire in it are not complete. As for evil, one does it only for himself to enjoy it, so inevitably the earnestness in it is more complete, so inevitably it is specified with the word iktisaba. And also: the actions of evil are prevented...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the third name: al-Muqtadir, citing Quran 54:55. The author explains that the word follows the pattern mufta'il from qadara, and notes a disagreement: some say qadara and iqtadara are synonyms, but the investigators differentiate. The pattern ifta'ala (which yields mufta'il) indicates perfection (kamaal) in the state of power. So iqtadara means the agent has complete control to act as he wills without any obstacle. The author illustrates with the pair kasaba and iktasaba: kasaba can be for self or others, while iktasaba is exclusively for oneself, supported by Quran 2:286. Good is associated with kasaba because it may be done for others, so the personal earnestness is less; evil is associated with iktasaba because it is done purely for oneself, so earnestness is greater. The passage ends abruptly with a reference to evil actions being prevented (likely continuing the comparison).

Superiority of Fa'eel Over Fa'il in Divine Names
Author explains the linguistic superiority of fa'eel over fa'il in divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Arabic pattern fa'eel is used in divine names to emphasize the permanence and strength of Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
The fa'eel pattern in divine names shows that Allah's attributes are permanent and perfect.

This passage explores a subtle linguistic point that has deep theological implications. The author explains that the fa'eel form (like Sami', Rahim) conveys a stronger, more inherent quality than the fa'il form. Understanding this helps us appreciate why the Qur'an prefers certain patterns for divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the examples of fa'il and fa'eel.
2
Identify the two patterns
Find the words 'fa'il' (اسم الفاعل) and 'fa'eel' (بناء الفعيل) in the passage. Note the examples: dakhil vs sami', rahim.
The passage distinguishes between these two forms.
3
Understand the distinction
The author explains that fa'il is used when the action is occasional or effortless, while fa'eel emphasizes permanence and inherent nature.
Think about the theological implications.
4
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
The author cites that 'al-Qadeer' and 'al-'Aleem' occur more often in the Qur'an than 'al-Qadir' and 'al-'Alim', supporting the idea that fa'eel is preferred for divine attributes.
Why would the Qur'an choose a stronger form for Allah?
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this linguistic point reinforces the belief that Allah's attributes are perfect and unchanging.
This is not just grammar; it's about tawhid.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضعف فيه او قوي . فإنه يجوز أن يشتق منه اسم الفاعل ، كمن يقول : دخل فهو داخل ، وخرج فهو خارج فإذا احتيج إلى تميز بين الفعل الذي يظهر من الفاعل مرة واحدة ، وبين الذي يظهر منه غالباً ، وكذلك إذا احتيج إلى أن يميز بين الفعل الذي يظهر منه على سبيل التكلف وبين الذي يظهر منه على سبيل الخلق والعادة ، وجب العدول إلى هذه الأمثلة ، ليتميز بواسطتها بعض هذه الأقسام عن بعض ومما يدل على أن الفعل أوكد من الفاعل ، أنه يقال سمع فهو سامع ، ورحم فهو راحم . وأما بناء الفعيل فإنه لا يستعمل إلا عند قصد تأكيد الفعل ، لأنا إذا قلنا سميع رحيم دل ذلك على تأكيد معنى الرحمة ، وتمكن هذا الفعل من طباع الموصوف به ، كالخلق الثابت ، وكالطبع اللازم . ولهذا السبب كان لفظ القدير في القرآن أكثر من لفظ القادر ، ولفظ العليم أكثر من العالم .
Weakness in it or strength. For it is permissible to derive the active participle from it, like one who says: entered so he is entering, and exited so he is exiting. So when there is need to distinguish between the action that appears from the agent once and that which appears from it habitually, and likewise when there is need to distinguish between the action that appears from it with effort and that which appears from it by nature and custom, it is necessary to turn to these patterns, so that some of these categories are distinguished from others by means of them. And among what indicates that the action is stronger than the agent is that it is said: he heard so he is hearing, and he had mercy so he is merciful. As for the construction of fa'eel, it is not used except when intending to emphasize the action, because if we say 'Sami'un Rahimun' that indicates emphasizing the meaning of mercy and the firm establishment of this action in the nature of the described, like a fixed character and an inherent disposition. For this reason the word 'al-Qadeer' occurs in the Qur'an more than the word 'al-Qadir', and the word 'al-'Aleem' more than 'al-'Alim'.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological analysis of the patterns fa'il and fa'eel as they apply to divine names. The author begins by stating the general principle that when there is a need to distinguish between an action that occurs once versus habitually, or with effort versus naturally, one must use different derived forms. He then notes that the action noun (fa'l) is stronger than the active participle (fa'il), citing the examples 'sami'a' (he heard) giving 'sami'' (hearing) and 'rahima' (he had mercy) giving 'rahim' (merciful). The pattern fa'eel, he explains, is used specifically to emphasize the action and indicate its firm establishment in the nature of the described, like a fixed character. He concludes that this linguistic principle explains why the Qur'an uses al-Qadeer more often than al-Qadir, and al-'Aleem more than al-'Alim, since the fa'eel form conveys a stronger, more permanent attribute.

The Divine Name Al-Muqtadir and Its Nuance
Author defines al-muqtadir and distinguishes it from al-qadir using the kasb analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir, and why al-Muqtadir implies perfect power without impediment.

Core Idea
Al-Muqtadir means perfect power without obstacles, like acquiring good deeds for oneself.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah explores a subtle distinction within the divine attribute of power. The author explains that while al-Qadir denotes ability, al-Muqtadir (from the pattern ifta'ala) indicates a perfection of that ability — acting without any hindrance. To clarify, he draws an analogy with the Arabic verbs kasaba and iktasaba, showing how the extra letters intensify the meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then the translation. Let the author's flow come through.
Pay attention to the key terms: al-Muqtadir, kasaba, iktasaba.
2
Identify the two claims
Note that some say qadara and iqtadara are synonymous, but the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) distinguish them. This distinction is central.
3
Understand the kasb analogy
The author uses kasaba (earn) and iktasaba (acquire) to illustrate. Notice how iktasaba implies personal effort and exclusivity.
The verse 'To her what she has earned…' is key.
4
Apply the analogy to good and evil
Good deeds are called kasb because they can be done for others; evil is iktisab because it is done solely for oneself. This shows greater resolve for evil.
The author extends this to religious obligations, which require even greater effort.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider how this nuance enhances your understanding of Allah's power: it is not just ability, but perfect, unimpeded power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لفظ الثالث : المقتدر . قال تعالى : ( في مقعد صدق عند مليك مقتدر ) ووزنه مفتعل من القدرة . قال بعضهم : قدر، واقتدر، بمعنى واحد . وأما المحققون فقد فرقوا بينهما، لأن بناء افتعل دلالة على كمال حال القدرة، فقولنا : اقتدر معناه : أن الفاعل متصرف في فعله كيف شاء وأراد، لا يعوقه عن ذلك عائق، ونظيره قوله كسب واكتسب . فإن كسب يستعمل فيما يكتسبه الإنسان لنفسه ولغيره . وأما اكتسب فهو مختص بما يفعله الإنسان لنفسه قال تعالى : ( لها ما كسبت وعليها ما اكتسبت ) وإنما خص الخير بالكسب، والشر بالاكتساب، لأن الخير قد يعمله الإنسان لنفسه ولغيره، فلا جرم لا يكون فيه الجد والرغبة كاملاً، وأما الشر فلا يعمله إلا لنفسه، ليلتذ به، فلا جرم كان الجد فيه أكمل، فلا جرم خص بلفظ الاكتساب . وأيضاً : أفعال الشريعة تمنع عنها بالزواجر الشرعية . فلا بد من الجد في إدخالها في الوجود، وإلا لم تحصل أفعال الخير لأن الموانع عنها دائمة . والله أعلم
The third term: al-Muqtadir. He said, the Exalted: 'In a seat of truth near a Sovereign, Omnipotent' and its pattern is muf'tal from power. Some said: qadara and iqtadara are the same meaning. But the verifiers have distinguished between them, because the construction of ifta'ala indicates the perfection of the state of power. So our saying 'iqtadara' means that the agent acts in his action as he wills and desires, no obstacle hindering him from that. Its analogue is the saying 'kasaba' and 'iktasaba'. For 'kasaba' is used for what a person earns for himself and others. As for 'iktasaba', it is specific to what a person does for himself. He said, the Exalted: 'To her what she has earned and against her what she has acquired.' And the reason good deeds are specified with 'kasb' and evil with 'iktisab' is that good deeds a person may do for himself and others; consequently the exertion and desire in it are not complete. As for evil, he only does it for himself to enjoy it; consequently the exertion in it is more complete, so it is specifically designated with the word 'iktisab'. Also, the actions of the Law are prevented by legal deterrents, so exertion is necessary to bring them into existence, otherwise acts of good would not occur because the obstacles are constant. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Muqtadir and explains its nuance. The author begins by citing the Qur'anic verse 'In a seat of truth near a Sovereign, Omnipotent' (al-Qamar 54:55) and notes that the word follows the pattern muf'tal from qudra (power). He mentions that some consider qadara and iqtadara synonymous, but the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) distinguish them: the form ifta'ala indicates perfect power, meaning the agent acts without any hindrance. To illustrate, he compares kasaba (to earn) and iktasaba (to acquire), showing that iktasaba implies personal effort and exclusivity, as in the verse 'To her what she has earned and against her what she has acquired' (al-Baqarah 2:286). He explains that good deeds are called kasb because they can be done for others, while evil is called iktisab because it is done solely for oneself, requiring greater resolve. He extends this to religious obligations, which require even greater effort because of legal deterrents.

Correction: The verifiers distinguish them: al-Muqtadir implies perfect power without obstacle, as shown by the ifta'ala pattern.

The Divine Names Al-Qawi and Al-Matin
Author defines al-qawi and al-matin, distinguishing active and passive strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names al-Qawi (the Strong) and al-Matin (the Firm), and how they relate to active power and impassibility.

Core Idea
Allah's strength encompasses both perfect action and perfect impassibility.

In this passage, the author clarifies two complementary names: al-Qawi, which emphasizes Allah's active power to effect, and al-Matin, which emphasizes His immunity from being affected. This distinction is rooted in a linguistic analysis of strength and firmness, and it culminates in a theological conclusion grounded in Allah's necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two definitions of strength
Read the Arabic passage and translation. The author first gives two everyday meanings of 'strong': one who can effect things, and something that resists being affected, like a stone. Identify these two senses.
These two meanings are the key to understanding the two divine names.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation of 'Matin'
The author explains that 'matin' (firmness) is derived from 'matn' (the back), because the back gives stability. This shows that al-Matin implies an unyielding firmness that cannot be overcome.
The linguistic root reinforces the idea of impassibility.
3
Apply the two meanings to Allah
The author now assigns the first meaning (active power) to al-Qawi, and the second meaning (impassibility) to al-Matin. He then explains why Allah must be al-Matin: because He is necessary of existence, He cannot be affected by anything else.
This connects the attributes directly to tawhid and the concept of wajib al-wujud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع والخامس : القوي المتين. واعلم أن القادر على الشيء المؤثر فيه يسمى قوياً عليه. وأيضاً : الشيء الذي لا يتأثر عن غيره يسمى قوياً كما يقال : هذا الحجر قوي . فالحاصل : أن القوة الفاعلة تسمى قوة، والإستعداد الشديد لعدم الانفعال يسمى أيضاً قوة، وقد يسمى أيضاً : متانة . ومعنى المثانة : الصلابة، وهي مأخوذة من المتن، الذي هو الظهر، لأن استمساك أكثر الحيوان يكون بالظهر . فالقوة في حق الله تعالى . عبارة عن كونه قادراً على التأثير. والمتين في حقه عبارة عن امتناع تأثره عن غيره، وذلك لأنه واجب الوجود لذاته؛ في ذاته، وفي جميع صفاته، فيمتنع تأثره عن الغير والله أعلم
The fourth and fifth names: al-Qawi and al-Matin. Know that the one who is capable over a thing and effects it is called strong over it. Also, a thing that is not affected by others is called strong, as is said: this stone is strong. So the conclusion: that the active power is called strength, and the intense readiness for non-affectedness is also called strength, and it is also called firmness. The meaning of 'matan' is hardness, derived from 'matn', which is the back, because the stability of most animals is through the back. So 'al-quwwa' with respect to Allah, the Exalted, is an expression of His being capable of effecting. And 'al-matin' with respect to Him is an expression of the prevention of His being affected by others, and that is because He is necessary of existence in Himself, in His essence, and in all His attributes; so His being affected by others is impossible. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines two divine names: al-Qawi (the Strong) and al-Matin (the Firm). The author begins by giving two meanings of 'strong': one who has power to effect an action, and something that is not affected by others, like a strong stone. He summarizes that strength includes both active power (al-quwwa al-fa'ila) and intense readiness to resist being affected, which is also called firmness (matana). He derives 'matan' from the back (al-matin), since animals rely on the back for stability. Applying this to Allah, 'al-Qawi' means He is capable of effecting, while 'al-Matin' means He is immune to being affected by others. This immunity is a consequence of His being necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud) in His essence and all attributes.

Key Terms
القوي
المتين
القوة الفاعلة
الاستعداد لعدم الانفعال
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'strong' implies a need to exert effort or struggle, or that Allah's strength is like human strength.
Correction: The author clarifies that Allah's strength is perfect: it includes both the power to effect without any exertion and the absolute inability to be affected by anything else, due to His necessary existence.

Checkpoint

B) Because He is firm in His promises.
C) Because He is impossible to be affected by others due to His necessary existence.
D) Because He supports His creation like a back.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qawi signifies Allah's active power, while al-Matin signifies His impassibility, both grounded in His necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you commit today to surrendering your need to control outcomes and instead trust in His complete power and unassailable nature?

The Fifth Category: Names Indicating Divine Knowledge
Author enumerates three names from the category of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three key names from the fifth category of divine names that relate to Allah's attribute of knowledge.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is affirmed through names like al-'Aleem and al-'Allam.

In this lesson, we begin exploring the fifth category of divine names: those that indicate knowledge. The author introduces several words that fall under this category, emphasizing that they are numerous. القسم الخامس من أسماء الله تعالى : الأسماء الدالة على العلم :
وهذه الألفاظ كثيرة :
أحدها : إثبات العلم الله تعالى. قال تعالى : ( إن الله عنده علم الساعة ) وقال : ( ولا يحيطون بشيء من علمه ) وقال : ( أنزله بعلمه ) وقال : ( ولا تضع إلا بعلمه ) وقال : ( فاعلموا أنما أنزل بعلم الله )
وثانيها : العالم . قال تعالى : ( هو الله الذي لا إله إلا هو عالم الغيب والشهادة )
وثالثها : العلام . قال تعالى : ( إنك أنت علام الغيوب )
The fifth category of the names of Allah, the Exalted: the names indicating knowledge. And these words are many: One of them: the affirmation of knowledge for Allah, the Exalted. He said, the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah - with Him is the knowledge of the Hour.' And He said: 'And they encompass not of His knowledge.' And He said: 'He sent it down with His knowledge.' And He said: 'And no female conceives except with His knowledge.' And He said: 'So know that what was sent down is with the knowledge of Allah.' The second: al-'Aleem. He said, the Exalted: 'He is Allah, other than whom there is no deity, Knower of the unseen and the witnessed.' The third: al-'Allam. He said, the Exalted: 'Indeed, You are the All-Knower of the unseen.'
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth category of divine names: those related to knowledge. The author states that these expressions are many, then lists three foundational ones. First is 'ithbat al-'ilm' (affirmation of knowledge), citing Qur'anic verses affirming God's knowledge of the Hour, His encompassing knowledge, sending down revelation with His knowledge, and His knowledge of all creation. Second is 'al-'Aleem' (the All-Knowing), with the verse from al-Hashr 59:22. Third is 'al-'Allam' (the All-Knower of the unseen), from al-Ma'idah 5:116? Actually it's from al-Ma'ida? The verse is 'You are the All-Knower of the unseen' from Surah al-Ma'ida 5:116 or Surah al-Hashr? Correctly, 'Innaka anta 'Allamu al-ghuyub' appears in Surah al-Ma'ida 5:116. This passage is introductory, listing the first names in this category.

?

?

Does your daily life reflect a genuine awareness that you are always before the All-Knowing? What would change if you lived every moment conscious of His sight?
?
Al-'Aleem and Al-'Allam are among His beautiful names. How often do you address Him by these names in your du'a? Are you comfortable knowing that nothing you ask—no matter how small or hidden—escapes His knowledge? Commit to calling upon Him as Al-'Aleem this week, asking Him for guidance in an area where you feel lost.

The Names al-Qawiyy and al-Matin: Divine Power and Impassibility
Defines the divine names al-Qawiyy and al-Matin, distinguishing active power from impassibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between active power and impassibility in the divine names al-Qawiyy and al-Matin, and why both are necessary for affirming God's transcendence.

Core Idea
God's strength is active power; His firmness is utter impassibility.

This passage explores two closely related divine names: al-Qawiyy (the Strong) and al-Matin (the Firm). The author first clarifies the linguistic range of 'strength'—it can mean either the ability to affect or the inability to be affected. By applying both meanings to God, we affirm that He is both all‑powerful over creation and completely beyond being influenced by it. This safeguards His transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two distinct meanings of 'strong' that the author presents.
Pay attention to the examples: the agent who effects change vs. the stone that resists change.
2
Trace the derivation of matāna
The author explains that the second sense of strength is also called matāna, derived from matn (back). Reflect on why the back is used as a metaphor for firmness.
3
Apply the names to God
Understand how each name applies: al-Qawiyy means He can effect, al-Matin means He cannot be affected. Link this to His being the Necessary Existent.
The commentary will clarify the theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع والخامس : القوي المتين. واعلم أن القادر على الشيء المؤثر فيه يسمى قوياً عليه. وأيضاً : الشيء الذي لا يتأثر عن غيره يسمى ( قوياً ) كما يقال : هذا الحجر قوي . فالحاصل : أن القوة الفاعلة تسمى قوة ، والإستعداد الشديد لعدم الانفعال يسمى أيضاً قوة ، وقد يسمى أيضاً : متانة . ومعنى المثانة : الصلابة ، وهي مأخوذة من المتن ، الذي هو الظهر ، لأن استمساك أكثر الحيوان يكون بالظهر . فالقوة في حق الله تعالى . عبارة عن كونه قادراً على التأثير. والمتين في حقه عبارة عن امتناع تأثره عن غيره ، وذلك لأنه واجب الوجود لذاته ؛ في ذاته ، وفي جميع صفاته ، فيمتنع تأثره عن الغير ( والله أعلم )
The fourth and fifth names: al-Qawiyy (the Strong) and al-Matin (the Firm). Know that the one who is able to do something and effects it is called strong (qawiyy) with respect to it. Also: a thing that is not affected by others is called strong (qawiyy), as is said: 'This stone is strong.' So the conclusion: the active power is called quwwa (strength), and the intense readiness to not being affected is also called quwwa, and it may also be called matāna (firmness). The meaning of matāna is hardness, and it is derived from matn (back), because the stability of most animals is through the back. So quwwa with respect to God Almighty is an expression of His being able to effect. And al-Matin with respect to Him is an expression of the impossibility of His being affected by others, because He is Necessary Existence by Himself; in His essence and in all His attributes, it is impossible for Him to be affected by others. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines two divine names: al-Qawiyy and al-Matin. The author begins by distinguishing two linguistic uses of 'strong' (qawiyy): (1) one who has power over something and affects it, and (2) something that resists being affected by others, like a hard stone. From this, he derives that 'quwwa' can mean either active power or passive firmness, the latter also called 'matāna'. He then explains that 'matāna' comes from 'matn' (back), alluding to how animals derive stability from their backs. Applying this to God, 'quwwa' means He is powerful to effect, while 'al-Matin' means He is impossible to be affected by anything else, because He is the Necessary Existent in His essence and all His attributes. This safeguards divine transcendence against any notion of passivity.

Key Terms
القوي
المتين
القوة
المتانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's strength is like physical strength or that being 'firm' implies physical solidity.
Correction: The passage explains that al-Qawiyy means He has power to effect, and al-Matin means He cannot be affected—neither implies physicality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'al-Matin' mean when applied to God?
A) That He is physically solid like a stone
B) That He is able to do everything
C) That He cannot be affected by anything else
D) That He has a strong back

Key Takeaway
The divine names al-Qawiyy and al-Matin together affirm that God is both all‑powerful over creation and completely impervious to being affected by it, because He is the Necessary Existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God is absolutely impassible – He cannot be affected by anything. What does this mean for your habits of speech when you pray? Do you sometimes talk to God as if He needs your praise or as if your words change His attitude toward you? How should your prayers change, knowing that God is utterly self-sufficient and unaffected?
?
If God's power is not like physical strength, how do you instinctively imagine God's control over the universe? Have you ever thought of God as 'trying hard' or 'struggling' to manage creation? How does the distinction between active power and impassibility correct that mental picture, and what does that demand of your trust in Him?
?
The source says God cannot be affected in His essence or attributes. That means your sin does not 'hurt' God or cause Him to 'react' emotionally. But you still need to repent. Why? If God is unchanged by your disobedience, what is the real purpose of your turning back to Him? Let that question pierce your excuses and lead to genuine repentance today.
