Divine Names Indicating Knowledge: Permissible and Impermissible Forms
Enumerates seven words for God's knowledge with rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which Arabic words for knowledge can be used as divine names and why some forms are impermissible.

Core Idea
Not every 'knowledge' word fits Allah; linguistic nuance guides divine naming.

This passage from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliyah systematically lists seven words related to knowledge found in the Qur'an or Arabic usage. For each, the author provides a Qur'anic verse and then rules on whether it may be used as a name of Allah. This careful selection shows the importance of linguistic precision in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the pattern: each item is introduced by a number, followed by a verse, and sometimes a ruling.
The passage is a numbered list; pay attention to the transitions.
2
Identify the seven words
List the seven words: العلم (affirming knowledge), العالم, العلام, الأعلم, العلم (as noun), العليم, العلامة. Note which ones are permissible and which are not.
3
Understand the rulings
For each word, check the author's reason for permissibility or impermissibility. The key is that some forms imply deficiency (العلم as a noun) or acquisition through effort (العلامة).
4
Apply the principle
Ask yourself: why is العليم permissible but العلامة not? The difference lies in the form's connotations. This teaches that divine names must be free from any hint of imperfection.
This connects to the broader rule of tawqif in divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الخامس من أسماء الله تعالى : الأسماء الدالة على العلم : وهذه الألفاظ كثيرة : أحدها : إثبات العلم الله تعالى. قال تعالى : ( إن الله عنده علم الساعة ) وقال : ( ولا يحيطون بشيء من علمه ) وقال : ( أنزله بعلمه ) وقال : ( ولا تضع إلا بعلمه ) وقال : ( فاعلموا أنما أنزل يعلم الله ) وثانيها : العالم . قال تعالى : ( هو الله الذي لا إله إلا هو عالم الغيب والشهادة ) وثالثها : العلام . قال تعالى : ( إنك أنت علام الغيوب ) ورابعها : الأعلم . قال تعالى : ( ربكم أعلم بكم ) وخامسها : العلم . قال تعالى : ( الرحمن علم القرآن ) و ( علمك ما لم تكن تعلم ) إلا أن هذا اللفظ لا يذكر في حق الله تعالى ، لأنه لفظ يوهم نوعاً من الدناءة . وسادسها : العليم . قال تعالى : ( وأسروا قولكم أو اجهروا به . إنه عليم بذات الصدور ) وسابعها : العلامة . يقال : رجل علامة إذا وصف بكثرة العلم ، ولا يجوز استعماله في حق الله تعالى . لأن هذه اللفظة وضعت لمن خرج عن القلة والنقصان إلى الكثرة والكمال ، بسبب التكلف والارتباض . وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال .
The fifth section of the names of God Almighty: the names indicating knowledge. These words are many: First: affirming knowledge ('ilm) for God Almighty. He said: 'Indeed, Allah [alone] has knowledge of the Hour' (31:34) and 'they encompass not a thing of His knowledge' (2:255) and 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (4:166) and 'no female conceives except with His knowledge' (35:11) and 'know that what is revealed is from Allah' (11:14). Second: al-'Ālim (the Knower). He said: 'He is Allah, other than whom there is no deity, Knower of the unseen and the witnessed' (59:22). Third: al-'Allām (the All-Knowing). He said: 'Indeed, You are the All-Knowing of the unseen' (5:116). Fourth: al-A'lam (the Most Knowing). He said: 'Your Lord is most knowing of you' (17:54). Fifth: al-'Ilm (Knowledge). He said: 'The Most Merciful taught the Qur'an' (55:1-2) and 'He taught you that which you did not know' (4:113), but this word is not mentioned with respect to God Almighty, because it suggests a degree of lowliness. Sixth: al-'Alīm (the All-Knowing). He said: 'And conceal your speech or publicize it; indeed, He is Knowing of that within the breasts' (67:13). Seventh: al-'Allāma (the very learned). It is said: a man is 'allāma when described with much knowledge, but its use for God Almighty is not permissible, because this word was coined for one who emerges from paucity and deficiency to abundance and perfection through effort and training. That is impossible with respect to God Almighty.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage categorizes divine names that express knowledge. The author lists seven words that appear in the Qur'an or Arabic usage. For each, he provides a Qur'anic proof. He then rules on permissibility: (1) 'Ilm (knowledge itself) is affirmed but the verbal noun form is not used directly for God because it can imply a deficiency (as if knowledge comes and goes). (2) 'Ālim, 'Allām, A'lam, 'Alīm are all permissible. (3) 'Allāma (intensive form) is not permissible because it normally describes a human who acquires knowledge through effort, which cannot apply to God. This shows careful sensitivity to linguistic nuance in divine naming.

Key Terms
العالم
العلام
الأعلم
العليم
العلامة
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any Arabic word meaning 'knowing' can be used as a name of Allah.
Correction: The passage shows that some forms like العلامة and العلم (verbal noun) are impermissible due to their connotations of deficiency or acquisition.

Checkpoint
Why is the word 'al-ʿAllāma' not used for Allah?
A) It is not found in the Qur'an
B) It implies acquiring knowledge through effort
C) It means 'the most knowing' and only Allah knows the unseen
D) It indicates a deficiency in power

Key Takeaway
Divine names must be free from any connotation of imperfection; not every knowledge-related word is suitable for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah using His names, how often do you pause to consider whether the name you use truly reflects His perfection without any hint of deficiency? What might change if you were more deliberate about this?
?
The passage teaches that even a superlative form like 'allāma is unsuitable for Allah because it implies effort and acquisition. Is there any attribute you have unknowingly ascribed to Allah that carries a hidden trace of createdness? How will you correct that starting today?
?
If the names of Allah must be free from any taint of imperfection, what does that demand of your own speech when you describe Allah in your du'a or daily conversations? Commit to one specific name from this lesson that you will use more consciously this week.

The First Meaning of al-Latif: Rejecting Physical Smallness
Explains rejection of physical smallness and affirmation of tanzih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal meaning of 'smallness' cannot apply to Allah and how it is reinterpreted as an attribute of transcendence (tanzih).

Core Idea
Allah is not small; He is beyond sensory perception.

This lesson examines the first meaning of the divine name 'al-Latif'. The author begins by stating a literal meaning that is impossible for Allah, then reinterprets it as a negative attribute. We'll see how this establishes the principle of tanzih, which is foundational in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice how the author first presents the literal meaning, then rejects it, and finally offers an alternative interpretation.
The verse at the beginning provides context for the name.
2
Identify the rejected meaning
Find where the author says the literal meaning is impossible for Allah. Understand why physical smallness cannot be attributed to the Creator.
3
Understand the reinterpretation
The author replaces the rejected meaning with a necessary consequence: that the senses cannot perceive Allah. This is an attribute of tanzih (transcendence). Reflect on how this shifts the meaning from a physical to a metaphysical quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثاني : اللطيف . قال تعالى : ( ألا يعلم من خلق ؟ وهو اللطيف الخبير ) واعلم أن « اللطيف » له معاني . فالأول : أن الشيء الصغير جداً ، الذي لا يحس به لغاية صغره يسمى لطيفاً . وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال . فوجب حمله على لازمة ، وهو أن الحواس لا تقف عليه لكونه لطيفاً ، وبهذا التفسير يكون من صفات التنزيه . الثاني : اللطيف هو العالم بدقائق الأمور وغوامضها ، يقال فلان لطيف اليد. إذا كان حاذقاً في صنعته ، مهتدياً إلى ما يشكل على غيره ، وعلى هذا التقدير فكونه لطيفاً عبارة عن علمه . والثالث : اللطيف هو البر بعباده الذي يلطف بهم من حيث لا يعلمون ، وي هيء مصالحهم من حيث إنهم لا يحتسبون ، ومنه قوله تعالى : الله لطيف بعباده ) والرابع : قال الغزالي - رحمه الله - : وهذا الاسم ...
The second name: al-Latif (the Subtle). He said: 'Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Acquainted?' (67:14). Know that 'al-Latif' has meanings. First: that something very small, which is not perceived due to its extreme smallness, is called latif. This is impossible with respect to God Almighty. So it must be taken as its necessary consequence, namely that the senses do not perceive Him because He is subtle (latif), and by this interpretation it is among the attributes of transcendence. Second: al-Latif is the one who knows the fine points of matters and their intricacies. It is said: 'so-and-so is latif al-yad (lit. subtle of hand)' if he is skillful in his craft, guided to what is difficult for others. On this basis, His being latif is an expression of His knowledge. Third: al-Latif is the One who is kind (barr) to His servants, who is gentle (yaltuf) with them in ways they do not know, and arranges their interests in ways they do not expect; from this is His saying: 'Allah is kind to His servants' (42:19). Fourth: al-Ghazali – may God have mercy on him – said: 'And this name...'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name 'al-Latif'. The author gives three distinct meanings, plus a fourth from al-Ghazali that is cut off. The first meaning is the literal sense of physical smallness, which is impossible for God; instead it is interpreted as 'the senses cannot perceive Him' – a negative attribute (tanzih). The second meaning is 'the one who knows the finest details of things', using the analogy of a craftsman with a 'subtle hand' – this makes al-Latif an attribute of knowledge. The third meaning is 'the One who is kind and gentle to His servants', providing for them in unexpected ways, supported by Qur'an 42:19. The fourth meaning from al-Ghazali begins but is incomplete in the text.

Key Terms
اللطيف
التنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'al-Latif' means that Allah is physically small or subtle in a material sense.
Correction: The passage explicitly rejects that and explains that it means Allah is beyond sensory perception, an attribute of transcendence.

Checkpoint
What does the author do with the first meaning of 'al-Latif'?
A) Accepts it literally for Allah
B) Rejects it and reinterprets it as transcendence
C) Ignores it entirely
D) Claims it applies only to prophets

Key Takeaway
The first meaning of al-Latif is not about size but about Allah's transcendence beyond sensory perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as 'subtle' in a way that reduces His majesty—like a gentle breeze or a fine mist? How does this lesson challenge that mental image and call you to a higher tanzih?

If your senses cannot perceive Allah, then what does it mean to 'feel' His presence? In what concrete ways can you train yourself to seek Him beyond sensory experience, starting today?
?
This passage shows that a word (latif) can be misunderstood and must be corrected to protect Allah's transcendence. Is there an attribute of Allah you have been describing in a human-like way without realizing it? Write down one and commit to studying its correct meaning from the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Al-Latif as Knowledge and Kindness
Explains second and third meanings of al-Latif as knowledge and kindness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third meanings of al-Latif, which affirm Allah's knowledge of subtle details and His gentle kindness to creation.

Core Idea
Al-Latif reveals Allah's perfect knowledge and gentle care.

The second and third meanings of 'al-Latif' shift from negation to affirmation. The author presents two positive attributes: comprehensive knowledge of all subtleties, and boundless kindness toward His servants. We'll explore these meanings with Quranic support and see how they complement the first meaning.

3

The third meaning emphasizes Allah's benevolence.

The second meaning is 'the one who knows the finest details of things', using the analogy of a craftsman with a 'subtle hand' – this makes al-Latif an attribute of knowledge. The third meaning is 'the One who is kind and gentle to His servants', providing for them in unexpected ways, supported by Qur'an 42:19. The fourth meaning from al-Ghazali begins but is incomplete in the text.

Four Divine Names of Knowledge: Permissible and Impermissible
Enumeration of four knowledge-related divine names with permissibility rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which four knowledge-related divine names are discussed by the author and why two of them are not permissible for Allah.

Core Idea
Not all Arabic intensives for knowledge can be applied to Allah.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents an enumeration of four divine names related to knowledge. The author cites Quranic verses for each and clarifies that two of these words are not used for Allah due to implications of imperfection. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the four names and the reasoning given for each.
2
Identify the permissible and impermissible names
From the passage, identify which names are permissible for Allah and which are not. Note the specific reasons: for al-`Ilm it suggests baseness; for al-`Allāmah it implies effort and acquisition.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Ask yourself: Why must divine names be free from any implication of deficiency? The passage illustrates that even a word like 'allāmah, though seemingly intensive, is unsuitable because it implies change from deficiency to perfection.
This connects to the broader principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : الأعلم . قال تعالى : ( ربكم أعلم بكم )(1).
وخامسها : العلم . قال تعالى : ( الرحمن علم القرآن ) (۲) و ( علمك ما لم تكن تعلم ) (۳) إلا أن هذا اللفظ لا يذكر في حق الله تعالى ، لأنه لفظ يوهم نوعاً من الدناءة .
وسادسها : العليم . قال تعالى : ( وأسروا قولكم أو اجهروا به . إنه عليم بذات الصدور ) (٤)
وسابعها : العلامة . يقال : رجل علامة إذا وصف بكثرة العلم ، ولا يجوز استعماله في حق الله تعالى . لأن هذه اللفظة وضعت لمن خرج عن القلة والنقصان إلى الكثرة والكمال ، بسبب التكلف والارتباض . وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال .
The fourth: al-A`lam (the Most Knowing). He said (the Most High): (Your Lord knows you best) (1). And the fifth: al-`Ilm (Knowledge). He said (the Most High): (The Most Merciful taught the Quran) (2) and (He taught you what you did not know) (3) except that this word is not mentioned regarding Allah the Most High, because it suggests a kind of baseness. And the sixth: al-`Alīm (the All-Knowing). He said (the Most High): (Conceal your words or proclaim them; indeed He is All-Knowing of that within the breasts) (4) And the seventh: al-`Allāmah (the very knowledgeable). It is said: a man is `allāmah when he is described as having abundant knowledge, but it is not permissible to use it regarding Allah the Most High. Because this word was coined for one who has gone from paucity and deficiency to abundance and perfection due to effort and practice. That is impossible regarding Allah the Most High.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and ruling regarding four divine names related to knowledge: al-A`lam, al-`Ilm, al-`Alīm, and al-`Allāmah. The author cites Quranic verses for each. For al-`Ilm (knowledge), he notes that the word itself is not used as a name for Allah because it implies a type of baseness (dana'a) when used in reference to a person. For al-`Allāmah, he explains that it is used for humans who acquire much knowledge through effort and practice, implying a transition from deficiency to perfection, which is impossible for Allah. The lesson guards against using derived forms that imply imperfection for Allah.

Misconception: One might think that any Arabic intensive form like 'allāmah can be used as a divine name because it emphasizes knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'allāmah is not permissible because it implies acquisition through effort and change from deficiency to perfection, which cannot be attributed to Allah.

Key Takeaway

The Divine Name al-Latif: Meanings and Implications
Author enumerates four meanings of al-Latif and illustrates with a morsel example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four meanings of the divine name al-Latif and why it is uniquely attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
Al-Latif means the One whose knowledge and gentleness encompass all subtleties.

This passage explores the divine name al-Latif, beginning with the Quranic verse. The author explains four meanings, dismissing physical smallness as impossible for Allah, and instead emphasizing His transcendent knowledge, benevolence, and perfect gentleness. The lesson includes a powerful example of a simple morsel of food to illustrate Allah's subtle providence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the Quranic citation and the four numbered meanings.
Pay attention to the structure: each meaning is introduced with 'first', 'second', etc.
2
Examine the first meaning and its negation
The first meaning (physical smallness) is deemed impossible for Allah, so it is reinterpreted as transcendence: the senses cannot perceive Him. Understand how this becomes an attribute of tanzih.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
3
Understand the second and third meanings
The second meaning is deep knowledge of subtle matters; the third is extreme kindness to servants. Note how both are expressions of Allah's perfection.
These are positive attributes, in contrast to the first.
4
Study al-Ghazali's fourth meaning
Al-Ghazali combines perfect knowledge of benefits with gentle action. This meaning is only fully realized in Allah. Reflect on why this combination is unique to Him.
This integrates knowledge and action.
5
Reflect on the morsel example and conclusion
Read the example of the morsel of food. It shows how countless creatures cooperate, demonstrating Allah's subtlety. Note how the author ties the specific actions to divine names.
The example makes the abstract concept tangible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثاني : اللطيف . قال تعالى : ( ألا يعلم من خلق ؟ وهو اللطيف الخبير ) (٥) واعلم أن « اللطيف ، له معاني . فالأول : أن الشيء الصغير جداً ، الذي لا يحس به لغاية صغره يسعى لطيفاً . وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال . فوجب حمله على لازمة ، وهو أن الحواس لا تقف عليه لكــونــه لطيفاً ، وبهذا التفسير يكون من صفات التنزيه . الثاني : اللطيف هو العالم بدقائق الأمور وغوامضها ، يقال فلان لطيف اليد. إذا كان حاذقاً في صنعته ، مهتدياً إلى ما يشكل على غيره ، وعلى هذا التقدير فكونه لطيفاً عبارة عن علمه . والثالث : اللطيف هو البر بعباده الذي يلطف بهم من حيث لا يعلمون ، وهييء مصالحهم من حيث إنهم لا يحتسبون ، ومنه قوله تعالى : الله لطيف بعباده ) () والرابع : قال الغزالي - رحمه الله - : وهذا الاسم إنما يستحقه من يعلم حقائق المصالح وغوامضها ، ثم يسلك في اتصالها إلى المستحقين سبيل الرفق دون العنف . فإذا اجتمع هذا العلم وهذا الفعل فقد تم معنى اللطيف ) .
ثم إنه لا يتصور كمال هذا العلم، وكمال هذا الفعل إلا لله - سبحانه - فإما علمه ـ سبحانه ـ بالدقائق فلا إشكال فيه لأن الخفي والجلي بالنسبة إلى علمه سيان . وأما رفقه في الأفعال ، ولطفه فيها ، فلا يدخل تحت الحصر . وههنا ( لا ) (١) يجب شرح أنواع حكم الله في خلق السموات ، والكواكب والعناصر الأربعة ، والمواليد الثلاثة ، بل يجب أن تكتفي ههنا بشرح لطفه في تيسير لقمة يتناولها العبد من غير كلفة ، فقد تعاون على إصلاحها خلق لا يحصى عددهم ، من مصلح الأرض وزارعها وساقيها وحاصد حبها ومنقيها وطاحتها وخابزها ، إلى غير ذلك ، فإن شرحها لا يتم في مجلدات ، فهو سبحانه من حيث دبر الأمور حكيم ، ومن حيث أوجدها : جواد ، ومن حيث رتبها : مصور ، ومن حيث وضع كل شيء في موضعه عدل ، ومن حيث لم يترك فيها دقائق وجوه الرفق لطيف ، ولن يعرف حقيقة لهذه الأسماء من لم يعرف حقيقة هذه الأفعال .
Second name: al-Laṭīf (the Subtle). He said (the Most High): (Does He who created not know? And He is the Subtle, the Aware) (5). Know that `al-Laṭīf' has meanings. The first: an extremely small thing that is not sensed due to its smallness is called laṭīf. This is impossible regarding Allah the Most High. So it must be taken in its necessary sense, which is that the senses do not perceive Him because He is Laṭīf (Subtle). With this interpretation, it is one of the attributes of transcendence. The second: al-Laṭīf is the one who knows the subtle points and intricacies of matters. It is said: so-and-so is laṭīf al-yad (skilled in hand) if he is dexterous in his craft, guided to what is difficult for others. On this estimation, His being Laṭīf is an expression of His knowledge. The third: al-Laṭīf is the Beneficent to His servants, who is kind to them in ways they do not know, and arranges their benefits in ways they do not anticipate. From it is His statement: (Allah is Laṭīf with His servants) (6). The fourth: al-Ghazālī (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This name is only deserved by one who knows the true benefits and their subtleties, then proceeds in delivering them to those who deserve them through gentleness rather than violence. When this knowledge and this action are combined, the meaning of al-Laṭīf is perfected. Then, the perfection of this knowledge and this action cannot be conceived except for Allah (glorified is He). As for His knowledge (glorified) of subtleties, there is no doubt because the hidden and the apparent are equal with respect to His knowledge. As for His gentleness in actions and subtlety in them, it is beyond enumeration. And here (it is not) necessary to explain the types of Allah's wisdom in creating the heavens, the planets, the four elements, and the three generated beings. Rather, one must suffice here with an explanation of His subtlety in facilitating a morsel that the servant consumes without effort. For creatures beyond count have cooperated in its preparation: from the cultivator of the earth, the sower, the irrigator, the reaper of its grain, the winnower, the miller, the baker, and so on. Their full explanation would not be completed in volumes. He (glorified) in terms of how He arranged matters is Ḥakīm (Wise); in terms of how He brought them into existence is Jawād (Generous); in terms of how He formed them is Muṣawwir (Fashioner); in terms of how He placed everything in its place is `Adl (Just); in terms of how He did not leave out the subtle aspects of gentleness is Laṭīf (Subtle). The reality of these names will not be known by one who does not know the reality of these actions.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the divine name al-Laṭīf. It begins by citing the Quranic verse from Surah al-Mulk. The author then explains that the term has multiple meanings. The first meaning (physical smallness) is impossible for Allah, so it must be interpreted as transcendence—that He is not perceived by the senses. The second meaning is deep knowledge, like a skilled artisan. The third meaning is extreme benevolence and kindness to servants in ways they do not perceive. The fourth meaning, from al-Ghazālī, combines perfect knowledge of benefits and delivering them gently. The author then argues that only Allah possesses this perfection of knowledge and action. He gives an extended example of a single morsel of food, showing how countless creatures cooperate in its production, illustrating Allah's subtle providence. He concludes each divine action necessitates a corresponding name, and true knowledge of these names depends on understanding the actions.

صفات التنزيه

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some mistakenly think al-Laṭīf means physically small or that Allah is small.
Correction: The first meaning is rejected for Allah; instead, it signifies that He is not perceived by the senses (transcendence) and that He has perfect knowledge and gentle action.

Two Interpretations of al-Khabir
Author gives two interpretations of al-Khabir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two valid interpretations of the divine name al-Khabir and the theological nuance that distinguishes them from human knowledge.

Core Idea
Al-Khabir means either Allah knows all inner realities or He informs — never through testing.

In this lesson, we explore the divine name al-Khabir (the Aware). The author presents two interpretations that safeguard Allah's transcendence while affirming His perfect knowledge. Both interpretations are valid but require careful understanding to avoid attributing human limitations to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's wording.
Notice the two interpretations are introduced with 'الأول' and 'التفسير الثاني'.
2
Identify the first interpretation and its caution
The first interpretation links al-Khabir to deep knowledge of inner reality. Note the caution that human 'khibrah' is gained through testing, which does not apply to Allah.
The author explicitly says 'والله منزه عنه' (Allah is exalted above that).
3
Identify the second interpretation and its analogies
The second interpretation treats al-Khabir as 'the one who informs' (al-mukhbiṛ). Compare with the examples of al-Sami' and al-Badi' to understand the pattern.
This interpretation avoids any implication of acquired knowledge.
4
Reflect on why both interpretations are necessary
Consider how each interpretation protects Allah's transcendence while affirming His omniscience. Ask yourself how they complement each other.
Both interpretations are rooted in Arabic linguistic usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثالث : الخبير . وله تفسيران: الأول : أن الخبير هو العالم يكنه الشيء المطلع على حقيقته ، يقال فلان بهذا الأمر خبير ، وله به خبرة ، إلا أن الخبرة في صفة المخلوقين إنما تستعمل في العلم الذي يتوصل إليه بالاختبار والامتحان . والله منزه عنه ، والتفسير الثاني : الخبير بمعنى المخبر كالسميع بمعنى المسمع ، والبديع بمعنى المبدع .
Third name: al-Khabīr (the Aware). It has two interpretations: The first: al-Khabīr is the one who knows the inner reality of a thing, acquainted with its true nature. It is said: so-and-so is khabīr in this matter, and he has khibrah (expertise) in it. However, khibrah in the attribute of created beings is only used for knowledge obtained through testing and examination. Allah is exalted above that. The second interpretation: al-Khabīr means 'the one who informs' (al-mukhbiṛ), like al-Samī` means 'the one who hears' (al-musmi`) and al-Badī` means 'the originator' (al-mubdi`).
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name al-Khabīr with two interpretations. The first interprets al-Khabīr as the one who has deep knowledge of the inner reality (kinn) of things, similar to human expertise (khibrah). However, human expertise is attained through testing and examination, which does not apply to Allah. Thus this interpretation is valid but must be stripped of any implication of acquired knowledge. The second interpretation treats al-Khabīr as an active participle meaning 'the one who informs' (al-mukhbiṛ), analogous to al-Samī` (the All-Hearing) meaning 'the one who hears' or 'the one who causes to hear' (al-musmi`) and al-Badī` (the Incomparable) meaning 'the originator' (al-mubdi`).

Key Terms

الخبرة
الاختبار
المخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Khabir implies that Allah acquires knowledge through trial and experience, like humans.
Correction: The author clarifies that while human 'khibrah' is attained through testing, Allah is exalted above that. The first interpretation only affirms that Allah knows inner realities without any process of learning, and the second interpretation completely avoids this by meaning 'the one who informs'.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The name al-Khabir is interpreted in two ways: as Allah's perfect inner knowledge (without testing) or as Allah's act of informing, both affirming His omniscience and transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Meaning of al-Latif: Knowledge and Gentle Action
The author defines al-Latif and illustrates it with the food provision example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine name al-Latif encompasses both perfect knowledge of interests and gentle action, and why only Allah fully deserves this attribute.

Core Idea
Al-Latif means more than subtlety—it requires perfect knowledge and gentle kindness.

This passage from al-Matalib al-'Aliyah unpacks the rich meaning of the divine name al-Latif (the Gentle, the Subtle). The author sets a high bar for deserving this attribute: it requires both perfect insight into hidden benefits and the ability to deliver them with gentleness. Through a vivid example of how a simple morsel of food involves countless unseen acts of kindness, the author shows that only Allah can fully embody this name.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of al-Latif
Read the first few lines to see that al-Latif is not just subtlety but a combination of knowledge and gentle action. Note the condition: knowing realities of interests and connecting them gently.
The term 'masalih' (interests) refers to benefits that bring good to creation.
2
Identify why only Allah qualifies
The passage argues that perfection in both knowledge and action is impossible for others because only Allah knows all subtleties and His gentle acts are beyond enumeration.
3
Meditate on the example of food
Consider the detailed chain of helpers preparing a single morsel—how it illustrates 'lutf' (gentle kindness) in provision without human effort.
The author uses this to show that even a simple act involves countless divine arrangements.
4
Connect the five names to actions
The author closes by linking wisdom, generosity, fashioning, justice, and gentleness to specific aspects of divine action, emphasizing that knowing names requires knowing these actions.
This shows that divine names are not abstract labels but reflect real actions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما يستحقه من يعلم حقائق المصالح وغوامضها ، ثم يسلك في اتصالها إلى المستحقين سبيل الرفق دون العنف . فإذا اجتمع هذا العلم وهذا الفعل فقد تم معنى اللطيف ) . ثم إنه لا يتصور كمال هذا العلم، وكمال هذا الفعل إلا لله - سبحانه - فإما علمه ـ سبحانه ـ بالدقائق فلا إشكال فيه لأن الخفي والجلي بالنسبة إلى علمه سيان . وأما رفقه في الأفعال ، ولطفه فيها ، فلا يدخل تحت الحصر . وههنا ( لا ) (١) يجب شرح أنواع حكم الله في خلق السموات ، والكواكب والعناصر الأربعة ، والمواليد الثلاثة ، بل يجب أن تكتفي ههنا بشرح لطفه في تيسير لقمة يتناولها العبد من غير كلفة ، فقد تعاون على إصلاحها خلق لا يحصى عددهم ، من مصلح الأرض وزارعها وساقيها وحاصد حبها ومنقيها وطاحتها وخابزها ، إلى غير ذلك ، فإن شرحها لا يتم في مجلدات ، فهو سبحانه من حيث دبر الأمور حكيم ، ومن حيث أوجدها : جواد ، ومن حيث رتبها : مصور ، ومن حيث وضع كل شيء في موضعه عدل ، ومن حيث لم يترك فيها دقائق وجوه الرفق لطيف ، ولن يعرف حقيقة لهذه الأسماء من لم يعرف حقيقة هذه الأفعال .
He deserves [the attribute al-Latif] only the one who knows the realities of interests and their hidden depths, and then proceeds in connecting them to those deserving to the way of gentleness without harshness. So when this knowledge and this action are combined, the meaning of al-Latif is fulfilled. Then it is not conceivable that the perfection of this knowledge and the perfection of this action belong except to Allah - exalted is He. As for His knowledge - exalted is He - of the subtleties, there is no problem in it because the hidden and the manifest are equal in relation to His knowledge. And as for His gentleness in actions and His subtlety in them, it cannot be encompassed. And here it is not necessary to explain the types of Allah's wisdom in creating the heavens, the planets, the four elements, and the three generated beings, but rather one should be content here with an explanation of His gentleness in facilitating a morsel that the servant consumes without effort, for countless creatures have cooperated in its preparation: from the one who tills the earth, sows it, waters it, harvests its grain, cleans it, grinds it, and bakes it, and so on. For its explanation cannot be completed in volumes. So He - exalted is He - is wise in how He disposes affairs, generous in how He brings them into existence, fashioner in how He arranges them, just in how He places each thing in its place, and gentle in that He does not leave out the subtlest aspects of kindness. And one will not know the reality of these names unless one knows the reality of these actions.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the divine name al-Latif (the Gentle/Subtle). The author begins by stating the condition for truly deserving this attribute: only one who perfectly knows the true interests and hidden benefits of things, and who then connects those benefits to their recipients with gentleness rather than harshness. When both perfect knowledge and gentle action are present, the full meaning of al-Latif is realized. Then the author argues that such perfection can only belong to Allah – His knowledge of subtleties is absolute (the hidden and apparent are equal to Him), and His gentle actions are beyond enumeration. Instead of listing all divine wisdom in creation, the author focuses on a simple example: the provision of a single morsel of food. Countless beings cooperate (tiller, sower, waterer, harvester, cleaner, grinder, baker) to prepare it without human effort. The author concludes by linking five divine names to corresponding actions: wisdom (tadbīr), generosity (ījād), fashioning (tartīb), justice (waḍ‘ kull shay’ fī mawḍi‘ihī), and gentleness (not leaving out any subtle kindness). One cannot truly know these names without understanding these actions.

Misconception: Al-Latif only means 'subtle' in a non-attribute sense, without implying perfect knowledge or gentle action.
Correction: The author shows that al-Latif requires both perfect knowledge of hidden interests and delivering them with gentleness.

B) Knowledge of interests and gentle action

?

The Divine Name al-Khabir and Its Two Interpretations
The author defines al-Khabir with two interpretations: knower of inner secrets and active informer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of the divine name al-Khabir and how they relate to Allah's exclusive knowledge.

Core Idea
Allah knows all hidden realities directly, not through testing.

In this lesson, we explore the divine name al-Khabir (the All-Aware). The author presents two complementary interpretations: one emphasizing Allah's perfect knowledge of inner secrets, the other highlighting His active role in informing creation. This dual perspective safeguards Allah's transcendence while affirming His communication with the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to hear the flow, then read the literal translation.
Notice the two interpretations are clearly separated by 'التفسير الثاني'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
List them: first, al-Khabir as knower of inner reality; second, as one who informs. Compare with human expertise.
The author contrasts human knowledge through testing with Allah's direct knowledge.
3
Understand the analogy with other names
The author links the second interpretation to al-Sami' and al-Badi', which have active meanings. This shows a pattern.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this name impacts your understanding of Allah's knowledge and communication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثالث : الخبير . وله تفسيران: الأول : أن الخبير هو العالم يكنه الشيء المطلع على حقيقته ، يقال فلان بهذا الأمر خبير ، وله به خبرة ، إلا أن الخبرة في صفة المخلوقين إنما تستعمل في العلم الذي يتوصل إليه بالاختبار والامتحان . والله منزه عنه ، والتفسير الثاني : الخبير بمعنى المخبر كالسميع بمعنى المسمع ، والبديع بمعنى المبدع .
The third name: al-Khabir. It has two interpretations: The first: that al-Khabir is the one who knows the inner nature of a thing, the one who is informed of its reality. It is said: So-and-so is khabir (expert) in this matter, and he has experience with it. However, experience when used as an attribute of creatures is only used for knowledge attained through testing and examination. Allah is exalted above that. The second interpretation: al-Khabir in the sense of 'one who informs' (mukhbi), just as al-Sami' means 'one who makes hear' (musmi') and al-Badi' means 'one who originates' (mubdi').
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name al-Khabir (the All-Aware/Expert). The author gives two interpretations. The first: al-Khabir means the one who knows the inner reality and hidden core of a thing. Linguistically, 'khabīr' is used for a human expert who has knowledge through experience and testing. However, the author emphasizes that such a method is inapplicable to Allah – He knows directly without trial. The second interpretation treats al-Khabir as active participle meaning 'the one who informs' (mukhbi), analogous to al-Sami' (the Hearing) also meaning 'the one who makes hear' and al-Badi' (the Originator) meaning 'the one who originates'. So Allah informs creatures about hidden realities.

Key Terms

الخبرة
المخبر
السميع
البديع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah acquires knowledge through testing and learning like human experts.
Correction: Allah's knowledge is direct and innate; He does not need to test or examine to know. The term 'khabir' when used for humans implies experience, but for Allah it means perfect, immediate knowledge of hidden realities.

Checkpoint

B) It means 'the one who informs' like al-Sami' means 'the one who makes hear'
C) It means 'the witnessed' like al-Shahid
D) It means 'the preserver' like al-Hafiz

Key Takeaway
Al-Khabir means both the knower of all hidden realities (without testing) and the one who informs creation, as shown by analogies with other divine names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Allah, as al-Mukhbi, has informed you of unseen realities through the Qur'an. What is one hidden truth He has revealed that you have not yet allowed to transform your daily life? What step will you take this week to act on that knowledge?

The Four Interpretations of al-Shahid
The author enumerates four interpretations of al-Shahid with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four interpretations of the divine name al-Shahid and how each relates to knowledge, presence, proof, and being testified to.

Core Idea
Al-Shahid means far more than just 'watching'—it encompasses knowledge, presence, evidence, and witness.

The name al-Shahid is one of Allah's beautiful names, but its meaning is deeper than simple observation. In this passage, the author unpacks four distinct interpretations, each drawing on Quranic usage. These range from knowledge of the apparent to the idea that Allah Himself is the One testified to by creation. Understanding these layers enriches our appreciation of divine witnessing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four interpretations
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author introduces each interpretation with a Quranic verse. Try to list the four interpretations in your own words.
The author uses 'the first', 'the second', etc., to structure the passage.
2
Compare each interpretation to its Quranic proof
For each interpretation, find the Quranic verse the author cites. Ask yourself how that verse supports the meaning being explained.
The first interpretation uses 'Knower of the unseen and the witnessed' (6:73). The second uses 'whoever witnesses the month' (2:185). The third uses 'Allah witnesses that there is no deity except Him' (3:18). The fourth is not directly cited with a new verse but builds on the concept.
3
Notice the shift in the fourth interpretation
The fourth interpretation reverses the active sense: al-Shahid means 'the One testified to' rather than 'the One who testifies'. Reflect on how this passive meaning fits with the name.
This is similar to how some names like al-Mu'min can be understood actively or passively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع : الشهيد . قال تعالى : ( أولم يكف بربك أنه على كل شيء شهيد ) ؟(٢) وقال تعالى : شهد الله ) (٣) واعلم أن الشهيد مبالغة من الشاهد ، كالعليم من العالم، والقدير من القادر ، وفي تفسيره وجوه : الأول : إنه العالم . قال تعالى : ( عالم الغيب والشهادة ) (١) و ( الغيب : عبارة عما يظن و و الشهادة ، عما ظهر . فإذا اعتبر العلم مطلقاً فهو العليم ، وإذا أضيف إلى الغيب والأمور الباطنية فهو الخبير . وإذا أضيف إلى الأمور الظاهرة فهو الشهيد . الثاني : إن الشاهد والشهيد هو الحاضر المشاهد . قال تعالى : فمن شهد منكم الشهر فليصمه ) (٣) أي فمن حضر . وهذا الحضور إن كان بالعلم فهو الوجه الأول. وإن كان بالرؤية والإبصار كان وجهاً ثانياً . الثالث : الشهيد والشاهد ، هو الذي يثبت به الأمر المتنازع فيه بين الخصمين ، فكونه شهيداً معناه : أنه تعالى بين حقائق الأشياء ، وأظهرها ، ومن جملتها أنه بين توحيده وعدله وصفات جلاله بنصب الدلائل، ووضع البينات ، ومنه قوله : شهد الله أنه لا إله إلا هو ) ) والرابع : إنه تعالى شهيد بمعنى المشهود له وذلك لأن العباد يشهدون له بالوحدانية ، ويقرون له بالعبودية .
The fourth name: al-Shahid. He said, exalted: 'Is it not sufficient that your Lord is over all things a Witness?' (41:53) And He said, exalted: 'Allah witnesses' (3:18). And know that al-Shahid is an intensification of al-Shahid, like al-'Alim from al-'Alim and al-Qadir from al-Qadir. There are several interpretations: The first: that He is the All-Knowing. He said, exalted: 'Knower of the unseen and the witnessed' (6:73). Unseen refers to what is hidden, and the witnessed refers to what is apparent. So when knowledge is considered absolutely, it is al-'Alim; when added to the unseen and inner matters, it is al-Khabir; and when added to apparent matters, it is al-Shahid. The second: that al-Shahid is the present observer. He said: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' (2:185), i.e., whoever is present. And this presence, if it is by knowledge, that is the first interpretation; and if it is by sight and vision, that is a second aspect. The third: that al-Shahid is the one through whom a disputed matter is established between two opponents. So His being a Witness means that He - exalted - makes the realities of things clear and manifests them, and among that is that He manifests His oneness, His justice, and the attributes of His majesty by setting up proofs and establishing evidences. And from this is His saying: 'Allah witnesses that there is no deity except Him' (3:18). The fourth: that He - exalted - is a Witness in the sense of 'the One testified to', because the servants testify to His oneness and acknowledge His servitude.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage lists and explains four interpretations of the divine name al-Shahid (the Witness). The author first notes that al-Shahid is an intensive form (mubālagha) of shāhid, similar to 'alīm from 'ālim and qadīr from qādir. Then he presents four aspects: (1) Al-Shahid means 'the All-Knowing' specifically regarding apparent things. The author distinguishes between al-'Alim (absolute knowledge), al-Khabir (knowledge of the hidden), and al-Shahid (knowledge of the apparent). (2) Al-Shahid means 'the present observer' – either by knowledge (which reverts to the first interpretation) or by physical sight. (3) Al-Shahid is the one through whom a dispute is settled – by making realities clear and manifesting proofs, such as the evidence for tawḥīd. (4) Al-Shahid means 'the One testified to' – because servants testify to His oneness and servitude. This final interpretation flips the active meaning to a passive one, similar to some views of other names.

الشاهد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Shahid only means 'watching' or 'present'.
Correction: It also means knowledge of the apparent, establishing proof, and being testified to.

Checkpoint

A) For knowledge of hidden things
B) For knowledge of apparent things
C) For absolute knowledge
D) For knowledge of the future

Key Takeaway
The divine name al-Shahid encompasses multiple meanings: knowledge of the apparent, presence, establishing proof, and being testified to by creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe Allah is al-Shahid, witnessing every hidden thought and action. But do you live today as if that is true? What would change in your private moments—your internet browsing, your intentions, your speech—if you truly felt His presence as a present observer? Identify one area and commit to a specific change before tomorrow.
?
The fourth interpretation says you testify to Allah's oneness. When you say 'La ilaha illa Allah,' does your heart affirm it in times of hardship, or does it waver? Think of a recent situation where you felt alone or afraid. Did your tongue bear witness with full conviction? If not, what is holding you back from that reality? Write down one step to strengthen that testimony in your daily life.
?
Allah is the One who clarifies disputed matters by setting up proofs. Is there an attribute of Allah that you find intellectually or spiritually unsettling—maybe His justice, His decree, or His infinite knowledge? Have you sincerely sought His proofs in the Quran and Sunnah to remove that doubt? If not, when will you?

The Divine Name al-Hafiz: Two Meanings
Author explains al-Hafiz as an intensive form with two meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of the divine name al-Hafiz: as the One who never forgets (knowledge) and the One who preserves from loss (action).

Core Idea
al-Hafiz combines perfect knowledge and active preservation.

The name al-Hafiz appears in multiple Qur'anic verses, each highlighting a different facet of preservation. This lesson unpacks two key meanings: first, that Allah never forgets anything, which relates to His perfect knowledge; second, that He actively preserves creation from loss or waste. Understanding both enriches our comprehension of this divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two meanings
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author explicitly states 'al-Hafiz has two meanings'. Underline or note each meaning as you read.
The first meaning is related to forgetfulness, the second to loss.
2
Understand the first meaning: knowledge and forgetfulness
The first meaning is 'contrary to forgetfulness and inadvertence'. The author explains this returns to the attribute of knowledge. Allah knows everything, completely and in detail, never subject to forgetting.
This connects al-Hafiz to the attribute of knowledge.
3
Understand the second meaning: active preservation from loss
The second meaning is 'whose opposite is loss/waste'. Allah preserves the heavens and the earths, maintaining their existence and order. This is an active, continuous preservation.
The verse from Surat al-Baqarah (2:255) is cited for this meaning.
4
Reflect on the combination of the two meanings
Consider how al-Hafiz encompasses both knowledge and action. Allah knows all things perfectly (never forgets) and preserves them perpetually (never loses or wastes). This dual aspect enriches our understanding of divine care.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الخامس : الحفيظ . قال تعالى : ( ولا يؤوده حفظهما ) (٤) وقال تعالى : ( فالله خير حافظاً ) () وقال : ( إنا نحن نزلنا الذكر ، وإنـا لـه لحافظون ) (1) واعلم أن الحفيظ مبالغة من الحافظ كالعليم من العالم . وللحفيظ معنيان: أحدهما: ضد النسيان والسهو، ويرجع معناه إلى العلم فهو تعالى حفيظ للأشياء بمعنى كونه تعالى عالماً بجملها وتفاصيلها ، لا يتبدل بالسهو والنسيان . والثاني : الحفيظ . هو الذي ضده التضييع ، فهو سبحانه حافظ السموات والأرضين . قال تعالى : ( ولا يؤوده حفظهما )
The fifth name: al-Hafiz. He said, exalted: 'He is not wearied by their preservation' (2:255) and He said: 'Allah is the best preserver' (12:64) and He said: 'Indeed We have sent down the Reminder, and indeed We will preserve it' (15:9). And know that al-Hafiz is an intensification of al-Hafiz, like al-'Alim from al-'Alim. Al-Hafiz has two meanings: One: contrary to forgetfulness and inadvertence, and its meaning returns to knowledge. So He - exalted - is Hafiz of things in the sense of being Knower of their wholes and their details, not subject to forgetfulness and inadvertence. The second: al-Hafiz is the one whose opposite is loss/waste. So He - exalted - is the Preserver of the heavens and the earths. He said: 'He is not wearied by their preservation' (2:255).
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name al-Hafiz (the Preserver). The author first notes that al-Hafiz is an intensive form, like 'alīm. Then he presents two meanings. The first meaning relates to knowledge: al-Hafiz means 'the One who never forgets'. It is opposite to forgetfulness and inadvertence. This meaning ultimately returns to the attribute of knowledge: Allah knows all things in their entirety and detail, never subject to forgetfulness. The second meaning relates to action: al-Hafiz means 'the One who preserves from loss'. Its opposite is 'tadī‘' (waste/loss). Allah preserves the heavens and the earths from destruction, as stated in the verse. The author cites the verse 'He is not wearied by their preservation' (2:255) which applies to both meanings.

الحافظ
النسيان
السهو
التضييع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Hafiz only means 'protector' from harm, without the aspect of knowledge.
Correction: The author shows that the first meaning of al-Hafiz is 'contrary to forgetfulness', which directly relates to Allah's perfect knowledge, not just active protection.

Checkpoint

A) Preserving from physical harm
B) Preserving from forgetfulness (i.e., knowing all details perfectly)
C) Preserving from punishment
D) Preserving the Quran

Key Takeaway
The divine name al-Hafiz encompasses both perfect knowledge (never forgetting) and active preservation (never losing or wasting).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a mistake or sin you committed that you hope no one ever finds out. Now realize that Allah (al-Ḥafīẓ) has preserved every detail of it in His knowledge—nothing is forgotten. How does that make you feel right now? Will you turn to Him in sincere repentance, or continue hiding?
?
You often worry about losing your health, wealth, or loved ones. But Allah is the One who preserves the heavens and earths without weariness. Is your anxiety rooted in a lack of trust that He can preserve what matters most to you? What would change today if you truly believed that His preservation never fails?
?
The Prophet ﷺ said, 'Whoever preserves forty hadith...' (a well-known tradition). Consider: You have just learned that al-Ḥafīẓ means Allah never forgets. But do you actively strive to preserve the knowledge you have learned from this lesson? Will you review it, teach it, or let it slip away like something forgotten?

The Divine Name al-Raqīb and the Key of Watchfulness
Sanusi explains the divine name al-Raqīb and the servant's duty of watchfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Raqīb and how the servant's constant awareness of Allah's watchfulness is the key to all goodness.

Core Idea
Allah's watchfulness inspires the servant's awareness, unlocking every good deed.

Sanusi now explains the divine name al-Raqīb (the Watchful). He anchors it in the Qur'an and then draws a profound distinction: the servant's murāqaba is not God's attribute but the servant's own knowledge that God is always aware. This awareness, he teaches, unlocks every good deed. Let us see how he builds this concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi first defines al-Raqīb using Quranic verses and al-Khaṭṭābī's explanation, then shifts to the servant's murāqaba.
The passage has two clear sections: definition of the divine name and application for the servant.
2
Distinguish divine attribute from servant action
Identify that al-Raqīb is God's attribute (the Preserver, the Watchful), while murāqaba is the servant's action—his knowledge that God is aware of him. This distinction is crucial.
The word murāqaba comes from the same root but refers to the servant's state.
3
Reflect on why murāqaba is the key to all good
Sanusi says that when the servant is certain of God's watchfulness over his states, thoughts, and words, he fears God and stands in awe. This state leads to all good deeds.
Consider how this awareness transforms one's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السادس : الرقيب ، قال تعالى حكاية عن عيسى عليه السلام : ( فلما توفيتني كنت أنت الرقيب عليهم ) والرقيب الحافظ الذي لا يغيب عنه شيء. ومنه قوله تعالى : ( ما يلفظ من قول إلا لديه رقيب عتيد ) (4) قال الخطابي : وهو في نعوت الآدميين : الموكل بحفظ الشيء المترصد له ، المحترز عن الغفلة فيه . وأما مراقبة العبد ربه فهي علم العبد باطلاع الحق عليه ، فاستدامته لهذا العلم هي المسماة بمراقبة الله تعالى . وهذه المراقبة مفتاح كل الخيرات ، فإن العبد إذا تيقن أن الله مراقب لأحواله ، مطلع على ضمائره ، سامع لأقواله ، لا يغيب عن علمه النقير والقطمير والصغير والكبير ، خاف سطوات عقابه في كل حال، وهابه في كل مقال ، عالما بأنه الحكيم الرقيب ، والشاهد الذي لا يغيب
The sixth name: al-Raqīb (the Watchful). He said, exalted, in the story of Jesus, peace be upon him: (When You took me up, You were the Watchful over them) (5:117). And al-Raqīb is the Preserver from whom nothing is hidden. And from it is His saying, exalted: (Not a word does he utter but that there is beside him a Raqīb (watcher) ready) (50:18). Al-Khaṭṭābī said: And it (al-Raqīb) among the descriptions of humans is the one entrusted with preserving something, observing it, cautious about heedlessness regarding it. As for the servant’s murāqaba (watchfulness) of his Lord, it is the servant’s knowledge of the Truth’s awareness of him, so his continuous maintenance of this knowledge is called murāqabah of God, exalted. And this murāqabah is the key to all good things, for if the servant is certain that God is Watchful over his states, Aware of his innermost thoughts, Hearing his words, that not a small thing nor a large thing is absent from His knowledge, he fears the onslaught of His punishment in every condition, and stands in awe of Him in every speech, knowing that He is the Wise Watchful, and the Witness from whom nothing is hidden.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Raqīb (the Watchful). The author begins with a Quranic verse from the story of Jesus where Jesus says to God, 'You were the Raqīb over them.' He then defines al-Raqīb as the Preserver from whom nothing is hidden, citing another verse about a watcher (raqīb) beside every person. He quotes al-Khaṭṭābī's explanation that for humans, raqīb means one entrusted with preserving something, observing it carefully. Then the author distinguishes the servant’s murāqaba (watchfulness) of God, which is the servant’s knowledge that God is aware of him. Maintaining this knowledge is called murāqabah, and it is the key to all good. When a servant is certain that God watches his states, knows his innermost thoughts, hears his words, and nothing is absent from His knowledge, he fears God’s punishment in every situation and stands in awe of Him, knowing that God is al-Ḥakīm al-Raqīb (the Wise Watchful) and al-Shāhid (the Witness) who never forgets.

الحافظ
الخطابي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might confuse the servant's murāqaba with divine murāqaba, thinking it is God's watchfulness rather than the servant's awareness.
Correction: The passage clarifies that murāqaba for the servant is his knowledge that God is aware of him, not God's attribute itself. It is the servant's continuous state of awareness.

The Divine Name al-Muhaymin and Its Three Combined Attributes
Author explains the divine name al-Muhaymin as combining perfect knowledge, power, and continuous care.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three combined attributes that constitute the divine name al-Muhaymin and why only Allah can possess them perfectly.

Core Idea
Al-Muhaymin uniquely combines perfect knowledge, power, and constant care.

Sanusi now turns to the seventh divine name, al-Muhaymin. He draws from the Quranic verse where Allah is described as 'al-Salām, al-Mu'min, al-Muhaymin'. In this passage, he unpacks the meaning of al-Muhaymin by showing it is not a simple attribute but a composite of three distinct qualities. Understanding this name deepens our appreciation of Allah's comprehensive guardianship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the Quranic citation at the beginning.
The verse is from Surah al-Hashr (59:23).
2
Identify the three attributes
The passage lists three attributes that combine to form the meaning of al-Muhaymin. Find each one and note what it refers to.
The attributes are: knowledge of the state, complete power, and constant perseverance.
3
Reflect on the exclusivity to Allah
The author concludes that these attributes can only be perfectly combined in Allah. Think about why this is logically necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السابع : المهيمن . قال تعالى : ( السلام المؤمن المهيمن ) (1) وهو اسم لمن كان موصوفاً بمجموع صفات ثلاثة : أحدها : العلم بأحوال الشيء . والثاني : القدرة التامة على تحصيل مصالح ذلك الشيء . والثالث : المواظبة على تحصيل تلك المصالح، فالجامع لهذه المعاني : اسم المهيمن . ولن تجتمع هذه الصفات على الكمال إلا الله - سبحانه .
The seventh name: al-Muhaymin (the Guardian, the Preserver). He said, exalted: (The Peace, the Faithful, the Guardian) (59:23). And it is a name for the one who is described by a combination of three attributes: one of them: knowledge of the state of a thing. The second: complete power to obtain the interests (maṣāliḥ) of that thing. And the third: constant perseverance in obtaining those interests. So the one who combines these meanings is the name al-Muhaymin. And these attributes cannot come together perfectly except for God, glorified.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Muhaymin (the Guardian). The author cites the Quranic verse from Surah al-Hashr where God is described as 'al-Salām, al-Mu'min, al-Muhaymin'. He then explains that al-Muhaymin is a name for one who is described by three combined attributes: First, knowledge of the state of a thing (knowing what is good or harmful). Second, complete power to achieve the interests (maṣāliḥ) of that thing (ability to bring about benefit). Third, constant perseverance in achieving those interests (continuous care). The name al-Muhaymin is the one that encompasses these three meanings. The author concludes that these attributes can only be found perfectly together in God, the Exalted. Thus al-Muhaymin uniquely denotes God’s perfect knowledge, power, and perpetual care.

Misconception: One might think al-Muhaymin simply means 'protector' or 'guardian' in a general sense, without recognizing the specific combination of attributes.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Muhaymin specifically means one who has perfect knowledge, complete power, and constant care, and these are exclusive to Allah.

Key Takeaway
The name al-Muhaymin encapsulates Allah's perfect knowledge, power, and continuous care for creation.

What concrete action can you take this week to rely on His muhaymin nature instead of your own efforts?
?
The name al-Muhaymin implies that Allah's care is not passive but active and constant. In what area of your life have you been demanding that a created being (a spouse, parent, friend, or leader) provide you with the constant care that only Allah can give? How will you repent from that misplaced trust and realign your heart to seek from Him alone?

The Divine Name ar-Raqeeb and the Key of Muraqabah
Explains the divine name ar-Raqeeb with Quranic evidence and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name ar-Raqeeb, its Quranic evidence, and how the servant's watchfulness (muraqabah) is the key to all goodness.

Core Idea
ar-Raqeeb is the ever-watchful Guardian; muraqabah is the key to all good.

This lesson explores the divine name ar-Raqeeb, which combines vigilant observation with active guardianship. We will see how the Qur'an presents this attribute and how the early scholar al-Khattabi distinguished its human and divine senses. Most importantly, we learn that the servant's constant awareness of Allah's watchfulness—called muraqabah—is the foundation of all piety and goodness.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and Quranic proof
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first defines ar-Raqeeb as 'the Guardian from whom nothing is absent' and then supports it with two Quranic verses.
The first verse is about Prophet Jesus (peace be upon him).
2
Understand al-Khattabi's distinction
Al-Khattabi explains that when the term 'raqeeb' is used for humans, it means one entrusted with guarding. Contrast this with the divine sense, which implies perfect and constant awareness without negligence.
This helps avoid anthropomorphism.
3
Grasp the concept of muraqabah
The passage defines the servant's watchfulness (muraqabah) as the knowledge that Allah is fully aware of him, and continuing in that awareness. This is called muraqabah of Allah.
Muraqabah is a state of heart, not just an action.
4
Reflect on the key to all good
The passage declares that this muraqabah is the key to all good. Because when a servant is certain that Allah watches everything—inner thoughts, words, small and big—he fears Allah and is awed, leading to righteousness.
List the areas of Allah's watchfulness mentioned: states, innermost thoughts, words.
5
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint question to confirm you can identify the main point from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السادس : الرقيب ، قال تعالى حكاية عن عيسى عليه السلام : ( فلما توفيتني كنت أنت الرقيب عليهم ) والرقيب الحافظ الذي لا يغيب عنه شيء. ومنه قوله تعالى : ( ما يلفظ من قول إلا لديه رقيب عتيد ) قال الخطابي : وهو في نعوت الآدميين : الموكل بحفظ الشيء المترصد له ، المحترز عن الغفلة فيه . وأما مراقبة العبد ربه فهي علم العبد باطلاع الحق عليه ، فاستدامته لهذا العلم هي المسماة بمراقبة الله تعالى . وهذه المراقبة مفتاح كل الخيرات ، فإن العبد إذا تيقن أن الله مراقب لأحواله ، مطلع على ضمائره ، سامع لأقواله ، لا يغيب عن علمه النقير والقطمير والصغير والكبير ، خاف سطوات عقابه في كل حال، وهابه في كل مقال ، عالما بأنه الحكيم الرقيب ، والشاهد الذي لا يغيب
The sixth name: ar-Raqeeb (the Watcher). He, exalted, said, narrating from Jesus, peace be upon him: 'When You took me up, You were the Watcher over them.' And ar-Raqeeb is the Guardian from whom nothing is absent. And from it is His saying, exalted: 'Not a word does he utter but there is a Watcher by him ready.' Al-Khattabi said: It, in the attributes of humans, is the one entrusted with guarding something, watching for it, guarding against negligence concerning it. As for the servant's watchfulness (muraqabah) of his Lord, it is the servant's knowledge of the Truth's awareness of him, and his continuance in this knowledge is what is called watchfulness (muraqabah) of Allah, exalted. And this watchfulness is the key to all good things. For when the servant is certain that Allah is watching over his states, aware of his innermost thoughts, hearing his words, and that nothing, be it a speck or a tiny thing, small or great, is absent from His knowledge, he fears the blows of His punishment in every state, and holds Him in awe in every speech, knowing that He is the Wise, the Watcher, the Witness who does not disappear.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the divine name ar-Raqeeb (the Watcher). It begins by giving the definition: ar-Raqeeb is the Guardian from whom nothing is absent, supported by Quranic verses. Then it quotes Al-Khattabi who explains the human attribute of being a watcher (raqeeb) as one entrusted with guarding and watching for negligence. The passage then distinguishes this from the servant's watchfulness (muraqabah) of Allah, which is the servant's awareness that Allah is fully aware of him. This awareness, when constantly maintained, is called muraqabah and is described as the key to all good. The reason is that certainty of Allah's comprehensive watchfulness—over states, innermost thoughts, and words, and that nothing is hidden from Him—instills fear and awe in the servant, leading to piety.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That ar-Raqeeb merely means a passive observer.
Correction: The passage clarifies that ar-Raqeeb is the active Guardian who is never negligent and from whom nothing is absent, implying both watchfulness and guardianship.

The Name al-Muhaymin and Its Three Attributes
Defines the divine name al-Muhaymin as a combination of three attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine name al-Muhaymin combines three specific attributes: knowledge, complete power, and continuous care.

Core Idea
Al-Muhaymin means perfect knowledge, power, and relentless care.

Divine names often encapsulate deep meanings. In this lesson, we explore the name al-Muhaymin (the Dominant) as defined by Imam al-Razi. The passage breaks down this name into three essential attributes, showing that it is not a simple title but a composite of perfect qualities. Understanding this helps us appreciate the comprehensiveness of Allah's care.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the definition.
2
Identify the three attributes
List the three attributes mentioned: knowledge of states, complete power to obtain benefits, and perseverance in obtaining those benefits.
Notice how each attribute builds on the previous one.
3
Understand the combination
See that al-Muhaymin is not just one attribute but the combination of all three. The name applies only when all are present perfectly.
4
Reflect on perfection
Consider the concluding statement: these attributes are found perfectly only in Allah. Why must that be the case?
Think about how any creature falls short in one or more of these.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السابع : المهيمن . قال تعالى : ( السلام المؤمن المهيمن ) وهو اسم لمن كان موصوفاً بمجموع صفات ثلاثة : أحدها : العلم بأحوال الشيء . والثاني : القدرة التامة على تحصيل مصالح ذلك الشيء . والثالث : المواظبة على تحصيل تلك المصالح، فالجامع لهذه المعاني : اسم المهيمن . ولن تجتمع هذه الصفات على الكمال إلا الله - سبحانه .
The seventh name: al-Muhaymin (the Dominant). He, exalted, said: 'The Peace, the Faithful, the Dominant.' And it is a name for one who is described by a combination of three attributes: one of them: knowledge of the states of a thing. The second: complete power to obtain the benefits of that thing. The third: perseverance in obtaining those benefits. So the one who combines these meanings is the name al-Muhaymin. And these attributes are not found in perfection except in Allah, glorified be He.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Muhaymin (the Dominant) by breaking it down into three necessary attributes. First, it cites the Quranic verse from Surah al-Hashr where the name appears. Then it explains that al-Muhaymin applies to one who possesses a combination of three qualities: (1) knowledge of the states of a thing, (2) complete power to obtain the benefits or well-being of that thing, and (3) continuous perseverance in obtaining those benefits. The passage emphasizes that these three attributes are found in perfection only in Allah. Thus, al-Muhaymin is not merely a synonym for 'guardian' but a composite name implying comprehensive knowledge, power, and sustained action for the welfare of creation.

The Infinite Effects of Allah's Power and the Multiplicity of His Names
Explains the first type: infinite effects of divine power lead to many names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah has many names corresponding to the infinite varieties of His creation.

Core Idea
Allah's names reflect the boundless manifestations of His power and wisdom.

This passage, a concluding discussion of the chapter, reveals the profound connection between the diversity of creation and the multitude of divine names. The author explains that the almost infinite effects of Allah's power necessitate a name for each type, and deeper knowledge of creation enhances knowledge of Allah's names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the first type of research once to grasp its flow.
Notice the phrase 'تكاد تكون غير متناهية' — this is the core idea.
2
Identify the cause and effect
The passage states that Allah's power creates an almost infinite variety of beings, and for each type He appointed a specific name. This is why He has many names.
3
Connect knowledge of creation to knowledge of names
The more one knows the details of creation and the wisdom behind them, the greater one's knowledge of Allah's names. Reflect on this connection.
This is the 'secret' and 'mainstay' of the chapter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا لما انتهينا إلى هذا الموضع عرض ما أوجب قطع هذا النوع من الكلام . ونختم هذا الباب بنوعين من البحث : أما النوع الأول : فهو أن تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في خلق الأجناس والأنواع والأصناف والأشخاص بحسب الذوات وبحسب الصفات ، ويحسب آثار الحكمة في تخليق تلك الذوات الموصوفة بتلك الصفات المخصوصة تكاد تكون غير متناهية ، ولهذا السبب جعل الله تعالى بحسب كل نوع منها اسم خاص . وكل من كان أكمل عرفاناً بأقسام مخلوقات الله تعالى ، وأكثر وقوفاً على آثار حكمته في تخليقها كان علمه بأسماء الله أكبر . فهذا هو السر ، والعمدة في هذا الباب . والنوع الثاني من البحث : أن هذه الرقى العزائم المذكورة بالألفاظ المجهولة ، إما أن تكون من أسماء الله أو من أسماء الملائكة أو من أسماء الشياطين . ولا شك أن أشرفها وأقواها في التأثير هو أسماء الله . ولما بينا أنها محصورة في هذه الأقسام التي لخصناها ، علمنا أن منتهى الأمر في تلك الألفاظ المجهولة أن تكون دالة على هذه الصفات ، فلعل السبب في تأثيرات تلك الألفاظ : أن قارئها إذا لم يكن عالماً بها فإنه يستولي على قلبه نوع خوف وفزع ، فيحصل في ذلك الوقت اتصال بجوهر النفس الناطقة بعالم المفارقات والمقدسات ، فتقوى التأثيرات . لهذا السبب ( والله أعلم بالصواب )
And know that when we reached this point, something appeared that necessitated cutting this type of discourse. And we conclude this chapter with two types of research: As for the first type: it is that the effect of the power of Allah, exalted, in the creation of genera, species, kinds, and individuals according to essences and according to attributes, and according to the traces of wisdom in the creation of those essences described with those particular attributes, is almost infinite. For this reason Allah, exalted, appointed for each type a specific name. And whoever is more perfect in knowledge of the divisions of Allah's creation, and more acquainted with the traces of His wisdom in their creation, has greater knowledge of Allah's names. This is the secret and the mainstay in this chapter. And the second type of research: that these incantations (ruqa) and spells mentioned with unknown words, are either from the names of Allah, or from the names of angels, or from the names of devils. There is no doubt that the most noble and most powerful in effect are the names of Allah. And since we have shown that they are limited to these divisions which we have summarized, we know that the ultimate matter in those unknown words is that they indicate these attributes. Perhaps the reason for the effects of those words is that when the reciter is not knowledgeable about them, a kind of fear and terror overcomes his heart, and at that time a connection occurs between the essence of the rational soul and the world of separate substances and holy things, so the effects become stronger. For this reason (and Allah knows best what is correct).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage provides two concluding discussions for the chapter. The first discussion explains why Allah has many names: because His power creates an almost infinite variety of beings, each with specific attributes and wisdom, and He appoints a name for each type. Thus, deeper knowledge of creation leads to greater knowledge of Allah's names. The second discussion addresses the use of unknown words in incantations (ruqa). These words must be names of Allah, angels, or devils. The most powerful are Allah's names. The author suggests that even if the words are unknown, they still denote divine attributes, and their effectiveness may stem from the fear and awe they induce in the reciter, causing a spiritual connection to the transcendent realm, thereby strengthening the intended effect. The passage ends with a disclaimer that Allah knows best.

Key Terms
قدرة الله
الأسماء
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah has only 99 names mentioned in hadith.
Correction: The passage indicates that Allah has names corresponding to every type of creation, which are numerous; the 99 are the most well-known.

Checkpoint

B) Because His power creates almost infinite types of beings
C) Because angels have many names
D) Because humans need many names

Key Takeaway
Allah's names are not arbitrary; they arise from the boundless manifestations of His power and wisdom in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The more you study creation, the greater your knowledge of Allah's names. What specific aspect of creation have you ignored or neglected to study, and what does that reveal about your desire to know Him? Will you commit to learning one new thing about creation this week to increase your knowledge of His names?
?
If Allah's power is almost infinite in its effects, then your current understanding of His power is painfully limited. Does that thought humble you or frighten you? And what will you do today to step out of your comfort zone and reflect on a part of creation you have never considered before?

The Effectiveness of Unknown Words in Incantations
Explains the second type: the nature and effectiveness of unknown words in ruqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale behind the effectiveness of unknown words in ruqa and the author's explanation.

Core Idea
Unknown words in ruqa work through the divine attributes they signify and the spiritual state they induce.

In this second research type, the author addresses a practical question: how can unknown words in incantations be effective? He argues that such words must belong to one of three categories, and their power ultimately derives from Allah's names. Moreover, the reciter's lack of knowledge can produce a fear that facilitates a spiritual connection, enhancing the effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second type
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the classification and the proposed mechanism.

2
Identify the three categories
The passage lists three possible origins for unknown words in ruqa: names of Allah, names of angels, or names of devils.
3

The author suggests that the reciter's lack of knowledge causes fear and terror, which leads to a spiritual connection with the transcendent realm, making the effects stronger.
This is a psychological and spiritual explanation.
4
Reflect on the ultimate source
Even though the words are unknown, they ultimately indicate divine attributes, which is why they have power.

And know that when we reached this point, something appeared that necessitated cutting this type of discourse. And we conclude this chapter with two types of research: As for the first type: it is that the effect of the power of Allah, exalted, in the creation of genera, species, kinds, and individuals according to essences and according to attributes, and according to the traces of wisdom in the creation of those essences described with those particular attributes, is almost infinite. Perhaps the reason for the effects of those words is that when the reciter is not knowledgeable about them, a kind of fear and terror overcomes his heart, and at that time a connection occurs between the essence of the rational soul and the world of separate substances and holy things, so the effects become stronger. For this reason (and Allah knows best what is correct).

Commentary
This passage provides two concluding discussions for the chapter. The first discussion explains why Allah has many names: because His power creates an almost infinite variety of beings, each with specific attributes and wisdom, and He appoints a name for each type. Thus, deeper knowledge of creation leads to greater knowledge of Allah's names. The second discussion addresses the use of unknown words in incantations (ruqa). These words must be names of Allah, angels, or devils. The most powerful are Allah's names. The author suggests that even if the words are unknown, they still denote divine attributes, and their effectiveness may stem from the fear and awe they induce in the reciter, causing a spiritual connection to the transcendent realm, thereby strengthening the intended effect. The passage ends with a disclaimer that Allah knows best.
