The Mu'tazilite Proof That God Does No Evil and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazilite proof that God does no evil and then refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God cannot do evil and the Ash'ari critique that it leads to necessitarianism.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite proof for divine justice actually undermines God's free will.

The Mu'tazilites argued that because God knows all things and is independent of all needs, He must avoid evil. Their opponents, the Ash'aris, responded that this logic would make God a compelled agent rather than a free chooser. In this lesson, we trace the argument and its refutation, examining how the Mu'tazilites tried to establish God's moral perfection but ended up compromising His voluntary agency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three premises
Read the passage carefully to identify the three principles the Mu'tazilites claim are established. Notice how each is defined (goodness/evil based on benefit/harm, God's independence, God's omniscience).
These are foundational assumptions that the Mu'tazilites take as given.
2
See how the conclusion follows
Trace how the three principles combine to yield the premise that God knows the ugliness of evil and knows He is independent, leading to the conclusion that God does not do evil.
3
Examine the two supporting arguments
The Mu'tazilites provide two ways to justify the link between knowledge of evil and avoidance. The first uses an analogy with human choice; the second claims intuitive certainty. Compare both.
Pay attention to the assumption that no opposing motive exists in God.
4
Analyze the Ash'ari objection
The objection presents a dilemma: if knowledge necessitates avoidance, God becomes a necessitated agent; if not, certainty of avoidance is lost. The objection also notes that the same reasoning would make divine acts obligatory, undermining voluntary agency.
This critique aligns with Ash'ari emphasis on divine will and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما طريق المعتزلة : قالوا : ثبت بالدليل : أن الحسن إنما يحسن لوجوه فائدة إليه وأن القبح يقبح لوجوه عائدة إليه. وثبت أيضاً بالدليل أنه تعالى غني عن كل الحاجات، وثبت أيضاً بالدليل أنه تعالى عالم بكل المعلومات، وإذا ثبتت هذه الأصول الثلاثة فإنه يتولد من مجموعها مقدمة واحدة، وهي أنه تعالى عالم بقيح القبيح وعالم بكونه غنياً عنه. ثم نقول : وكل من كان كذلك فإنه لا يفعل القبيح ينتج أنه تعالى لا يفعل القبيح. أما الأصول الثلاثة فقد تقدم الكلام فيها. وأما المقدمة الثانية فقد احتجوا على صحتها من وجهين : الأول : إن الإنسان إذا قيل له : إن صدقت أعطيناك ديناراً، وإن كذبت أعطيناك ديناراً، واستوى الصدق والكذب بالنسبة إليه في جميع المصالح العاجلة والأجلة، إلا في كونه صدقاً وكذباً. فإنه لا بد وأن يترك الكذب ويفعل الصدق. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الوجه المقتضي للصرف عن الكذب مجرد علمه بكونه كذباً، لأن عند العلم بكونه كذباً يحصل هذا الصرف، وعند عدم هذا العلم لا يحصل هذا الصرف، فثبت أن الموجب لهذا الصرف هو العلم بكونه كذباً فثبت أن العلم بالقبح يوجب الصرف عن فعل القبيح، ولما حصل هذا العلم في حق الله تعالى، وجب أن يؤثر في صرفه عن القبيح. والوجه الثاني : هو أن نترك هذا القياس، وندعي العلم الضروري بأن العلم بكون الفعل قبيحاً يوجب الانصراف عنه، اللهم إلا إذا صار هذا الصارف معارضاً بداعية الشهوة والحاجة. لكنا بينا أن هذه الداعية في حق الله تعالى ممتنعة، فكان علمه تعالى بقبح الفعل صارفاً له عن هذا الفعل، ولم يوجد في حقه ما يعارضه، وهو داعية الشهوة، فوجب أن يمتنع الله من فعل القبيح، فهذا تقرير دليل المعتزلة. وللاعتراض عليه. نقول : أتدعون أن عند حصول العلم يكون الفعل قبيحاً، يمتنع صدور الفعل عنه أو لا تدعون الامتناع ؟ فإذا ادعيتم الامتناع لزمكم كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار، لأن ذاته موجبة لذلك العلم. وذلك العلم موجب، فوجب الامتناع من ذلك الفعل، وموجب الموجب موجب، فكانت ذاته موجبة للامتناع من ذلك الفعل، فيكون موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار. وأما إن قلتم : إن عند حصول هذا الصارف لا يجب هذا الامتناع، كان معناه أن مع حصول هذا الصارف يجوز، ومع هذا التجويز كيف يمكنكم الجزم بأنه لا يقع ؟ وبأنه بتقدير الوقوع يفضي إلى المحال. وأيضاً : عند العلم بأن ترك الثواب وترك العوض قبيح، وجب ترك ذلك، فيلزم أن يكون فعله واجباً عقلاً، فيعود ما ذكرنا إلى أنه يكون موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار. وقد ذكرنا مع الفلاسفة أن القول يكون الإله تعالى موجباً بالذات، فإن هذا البحث ساقط بالكلية. ثم نقول : إن دل هذا الدليل على أنه تعالى لا يفعل القبيح ولا يريده ولا
As for the way of the Mu'tazilites: They said: It is established by proof that the good is good only for some benefit accruing to it, and that the evil is evil for some harm returning to it. And it is also established by proof that He, exalted, is free of need from all needs, and it is also established by proof that He, exalted, knows all known things. And when these three principles are established, there arises from their totality one premise, which is that He, exalted, knows the ugliness of the ugly and knows that He is independent of it. Then we say: And everyone who is thus does not do the ugly; it follows that He, exalted, does not do the ugly. As for the three principles, discussion of them has preceded. As for the second premise, they have argued for its validity in two ways: First: [Consider] a man told: 'If you tell the truth we will give you a dinar, and if you lie we will give you a dinar,' and truth and lying are equal for him in all immediate and ultimate benefits, except in being truth and lying. Then he must necessarily leave lying and do truth. If this is established, we say: The factor necessitating turning away from lying is merely his knowledge that it is a lie, because when knowledge that it is a lie exists, this turning away occurs, and when this knowledge is absent, this turning away does not occur. So it is established that the cause of this turning away is the knowledge that it is a lie. Thus it is established that knowledge of evil necessitates turning away from doing the evil. And since this knowledge exists in the case of God, exalted, it must produce an effect in turning Him away from evil. The second way: We abandon this analogy and claim necessary knowledge that knowledge that an act is evil necessitates turning away from it, unless this deterrent is opposed by a motive of lust or need. But we have shown that such a motive is impossible in the case of God, exalted. So His knowledge, exalted, of the evil of an act is a deterrent from this act, and there is nothing opposing it in His case, i.e., a motive of lust. Thus it is necessary that God refrain from doing evil. This is the exposition of the Mu'tazilites' proof. And in objection to it we say: Do you claim that when knowledge exists that an act is evil, the act's occurrence is impossible from Him, or do you not claim impossibility? If you claim impossibility, then you are forced to say that He, exalted, is necessitated by essence, not a voluntary agent, because His essence necessitates that knowledge, and that knowledge necessitates, so the impossibility of that act is necessitated, and the necessitator of the necessitator is a necessitator; thus His essence necessitates the impossibility of that act, so He becomes necessitated by essence, not a voluntary agent. And if you say: When this deterrent exists, this impossibility is not necessitated, this means that with the existence of this deterrent it is possible, and with this possibility, how can you be certain that it does not occur? And that supposing it occurs, it leads to absurdity? Also, when knowledge that abandoning reward and abandoning compensation is evil necessitates abandoning that, then His act would be rationally obligatory, and it returns to what we mentioned: that He becomes necessitated by essence, not a voluntary agent. And we have mentioned with the philosophers that the view that God is necessitated by essence is completely invalid. Then we say: If this proof indicates that He, exalted, does not do evil, nor wills it, nor ...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that God cannot do evil. The author first expounds the Mu'tazilite proof, then raises an Ash'ari objection. The Mu'tazilites begin with three premises: (1) good and evil are determined by benefit and harm, (2) God is independent of all needs, and (3) God knows all things. From these they derive that God knows the ugliness of evil and His independence from it, and whoever knows evil in this way must avoid it, so God does not do evil. They support the link between knowledge and avoidance with two arguments. The first is an empirical example: a man offered equal reward for truth and lying will choose truth solely because he knows it is truth, showing that knowledge of moral quality motivates action. The second abandons analogy and claims intuitive certainty that knowledge of evil deters, unless opposed by passion or need; since God has no such passions, His knowledge suffices to prevent evil. The Ash'ari objection then challenges this: if the Mu'tazilites claim that knowledge of evil necessitates refraining, then God's essence necessitates that knowledge, making Him a necessitated agent rather than a voluntary one. If they claim only that it makes refraining possible but not necessary, then they cannot assert certainty that He never does evil. Furthermore, applying the same logic to matters like abandoning reward would make acts rationally obligatory, again implying necessitation. The author concludes that such reasoning undermines divine free will and is ultimately futile, akin to the philosophers' view of God as a necessary cause.

Key Terms
العلم بالقبح
الصارف
الموجب بالذات
الفاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazilite proof successfully establishes God's justice, showing that He is morally perfect and cannot do evil.
Correction: The Ash'ari objection shows that the proof actually compromises God's free will by making Him a necessitated agent, which is contrary to the Islamic conception of God as a voluntary agent.

Checkpoint
What is the main Ash'ari objection to the Mu'tazilite proof that God cannot do evil?
A) The proof relies on a false analogy with human action.
B) The proof implies that God is necessitated by His nature rather than a free agent.
C) The proof assumes that God knows all things, which is false.
D) The proof ignores the possibility of divine mercy outweighing justice.

Key Takeaway
Attributing moral necessity to God based on His knowledge and independence undermines His status as a free, voluntary agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that God must act in a certain way because of His attributes (like justice or mercy)? When you sin, do you expect punishment because you think God is compelled to be just, or do you hope for His free forgiveness? The lesson shows that making God's actions necessary (even just ones) undermines His free will. How does this challenge your default attitude toward God's dealings with you? Will you consciously submit to God's absolute freedom, even when it contradicts your expectations?
?
Imagine you are in a situation of deep suffering, and you find yourself thinking, 'God cannot be just if He lets this happen.' The Mu'tazilites tried to prove God cannot do evil, but the refutation shows that such a proof actually makes God a 'necessitated agent.' Have you ever tried to box God into your own understanding of justice? Are you willing to accept that God's actions are not bound by human moral categories, and that He remains good even when His actions seem inexplicable? How can you trust a God whose will is perfectly free, beyond your comprehension?
?
The Mu'tazilites' proof led to the conclusion that God's knowledge of evil compels Him to avoid it. Sanusi objects that this strips God of voluntary agency. Examine your own faith: Is your relationship with God based on a set of guarantees (e.g., that He must be fair) or on a personal relationship with a free, sovereign Lord? If you discovered that God's justice does not operate according to a rational necessity you can grasp, would your trust in Him waver? Commit to studying God's attributes as described in revelation, rather than imposing your own logic upon Him.

The Dilemma of the Mu'tazilite Proof: Necessity or Possibility?
The objector presents a dilemma against the Mu'tazilite proof: either God is a necessitated being or the proof fails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against the Mu'tazilite proof forces a dilemma: either God becomes a necessitated being or the proof fails to establish impossibility.

Core Idea
If knowledge necessitates prevention, God is no longer a voluntary agent.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God's knowledge of the ugliness of evil prevents Him from doing it. But the objection here shows that this argument leads to an unwelcome consequence: it makes God a necessitated being, acting without choice. Alternatively, if the knowledge does not necessitate prevention, the argument cannot guarantee that evil never occurs. This lesson explores that dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's opening dilemma
The objector asks whether the Mu'tazila claim impossibility or not. Notice the two options presented.
2
Trace the first horn: necessity leads to necessitarianism
If they claim impossibility, then God's essence necessitates knowledge which necessitates prevention, making Him mūjib bi-al-dhāt, not a free agent.
This chain of necessitation is key.
3
Trace the second horn: possibility fails to prove prevention
If they say the deterrent does not necessitate, then the act is merely possible, and certainty that it never occurs is lost.
4
Note the additional argument about reward and compensation
The objector adds that if refraining from evil is necessary, then acts of rewarding become obligatory, again implying necessitarianism.
This reinforces the first horn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللاعتراض عليه. نقول: أتدعون أن عند حصول العلم يكون الفعل قبيحاً، يمتنع صدور الفعل عنه أو لا تدعون الامتناع؟ فإذا ادعيتم الامتناع لزمكم كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار، لأن ذاته موجبة لذلك العلم، وذلك العلم موجب، فوجب الامتناع من ذلك الفعل، وموجب الموجب موجب، فكانت ذاته موجبة للامتناع من ذلك الفعل، فيكون موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار. وأما إن قلتم: إن عند حصول هذا الصارف لا يجب هذا الامتناع، كان معناه أن مع حصول هذا الصارف يجوز، ومع هذا التجويز كيف يمكنكم الجزم بأنه لا يقع؟ وبأنه بتقدير الوقوع يفضي إلى المحال. وأيضاً: عند العلم بأن ترك الثواب وترك العوض قبيح، وجب ترك ذلك، فيلزم أن يكون فعله واجباً عقلاً، فيعود ما ذكرنا إلى أنه يكون موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار. وقد ذكرنا مع الفلاسفة أن القول يكون الإله تعالى موجباً بالذات، فإن هذا البحث ساقط بالكلية. ثم نقول: إن دل هذا الدليل على أنه تعالى لا يفعل القبيح ولا يريده ولا يأمر به. فههنا دلائل تدل على أنه تعالى يفعله ويريده، وهي الدلائل الدالة على أن أفعال العباد كلها واقعة بقضاء الله وقدره. وسيأتي شرح تلك الدلائل على الاستقصاء. والله أعلم.
And to object to it. We say: Do you claim that when knowledge is obtained, the act is ugly, and it is impossible for the act to issue from him, or do you not claim impossibility? If you claim impossibility, then it follows for you that He Most High is a necessitated being (mūjib bi-al-dhāt), not a voluntary agent (fāʿil bi-al-ikhtiyār), because His essence necessitates that knowledge, and that knowledge necessitates, so the impossibility of that act is necessitated, and the necessitator of the necessitator is necessitator, thus His essence is necessitating the impossibility of that act, so He is necessitated by essence, not a voluntary agent. But if you say that when this deterrent is present this impossibility is not necessary, then its meaning is that with the presence of this deterrent it is possible, and with this possibility how can you be certain that it does not occur? And that if it were to occur, it would lead to absurdity. Also: with knowledge that abandoning reward and abandoning compensation (ʿiwaḍ) is ugly, its abandonment is necessary, so it follows that His act is rationally obligatory, and we return to what we mentioned that He would be a necessitated being, not a voluntary agent. We have already mentioned with the philosophers that the statement that the deity is a necessitated being by essence, this discussion is completely invalid. Then we say: If this proof indicates that He Most High does not do evil, nor wills it, nor commands it, then here are proofs indicating that He Most High does it and wills it, and they are the proofs that all actions of servants occur by God's decree and predestination (qaḍāʾ Allāh wa-qadarih). Their detailed explanation will come later. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the Mu'tazilite proof and its counter-argument. The objection first poses a dilemma: either the Mu'tazila claim that God's knowledge makes it impossible for Him to do evil, or they do not. If they claim impossibility, then God becomes a necessitated being (mūjib bi-al-dhāt) because His essence necessitates knowledge, which in turn necessitates the prevention of the act. This leads to the philosophical position that God acts by necessity, not by choice—a view the author finds unacceptable. If they instead say that the deterrent does not necessitate impossibility but merely makes it likely, then they cannot be certain that God never commits evil, and the proof fails to establish absolute prevention. A further point is added: if refraining from evil is necessary due to knowledge, then acts like rewarding and compensating become obligatory, again implying necessitarianism. The author then dismisses the whole discussion as invalid, referencing discussions with philosophers. Finally, the author asserts that there are proofs from Islamic doctrine that God does do evil and wills it, specifically the proofs of predestination (qadar), which will be detailed later. The passage thus defends the Ash'ari position against Mu'tazilite rationalism.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار
صارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite proof is straightforward and irrefutable.
Correction: The objection shows it leads to an undesirable conclusion or fails to prove prevention.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Mu'tazila claim that God's knowledge makes evil impossible?
A) God is a voluntary agent
B) God is a necessitated being
C) Evil does not exist
D) The proof is valid

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof for God's prevention of evil leads to a dilemma: either God is a necessitated being or the proof is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you know something is wrong but do it anyway, what does that say about the relationship between your knowledge and your actions? How does this lesson's dilemma challenge you to take responsibility for your choices, knowing that God's knowledge is perfect and yours is not?
?
If God's knowledge necessitated prevention, your every sin would be impossible. Yet you sin. Does this make you doubt the Mu'tazilite position or lead you to a deeper trust in God's wisdom in allowing evil? How will you respond when you next face the temptation to do wrong?
?
The objector argues that if knowledge necessitates, God becomes a necessitated being—no longer truly free. Are you comfortable with a God whose actions are bound by His own attributes? Or does this drive you to affirm His absolute freedom, even when it means He permits what He hates? How does this reflection change your understanding of His mercy and justice in your life?

Dismissing the Necessitarian View and Affirming Predestination
The author dismisses the necessitarian argument and introduces the proofs of predestination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author dismisses the necessitarian position and asserts that Islamic proofs from predestination show God does will and do evil.

Core Idea
The claim that God is a necessitated being is invalid; predestination proves His will includes all actions.

Having raised the objection, the author now dismisses the entire discussion of necessitarianism as invalid, referencing earlier debates with philosophers. He then asserts that there are positive proofs from Islamic doctrine—namely, the proofs of divine decree and predestination (qaḍāʾ and qadar)—that show God does do evil and wills it. This lesson presents that dismissal and the shift to scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's dismissal of the necessitarian view
The author states that the discussion of God being mūjib bi-al-dhāt is completely invalid, referencing prior refutations.
2
Understand the conditional statement
The author says: if the Mu'tazilite proof indicates God does not do evil, then there are proofs that He does.
This sets up the alternative.
3
Identify the alternative proofs
The proofs are those of predestination: all actions of servants occur by God's decree.
This is the Ash'ari position.
4
Note the promise of detailed explanation
The author says the detailed explanation will come later.
This indicates the argument is part of a larger discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللاعتراض عليه. نقول: أتدعون أن عند حصول العلم يكون الفعل قبيحاً، يمتنع صدور الفعل عنه أو لا تدعون الامتناع؟ فإذا ادعيتم الامتناع لزمكم كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار، لأن ذاته موجبة لذلك العلم، وذلك العلم موجب، فوجب الامتناع من ذلك الفعل، وموجب الموجب موجب، فكانت ذاته موجبة للامتناع من ذلك الفعل، فيكون موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار. وأما إن قلتم: إن عند حصول هذا الصارف لا يجب هذا الامتناع، كان معناه أن مع حصول هذا الصارف يجوز، ومع هذا التجويز كيف يمكنكم الجزم بأنه لا يقع؟ وبأنه بتقدير الوقوع يفضي إلى المحال. وأيضاً: عند العلم بأن ترك الثواب وترك العوض قبيح، وجب ترك ذلك، فيلزم أن يكون فعله واجباً عقلاً، فيعود ما ذكرنا إلى أنه يكون موجباً بالذات، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار. وقد ذكرنا مع الفلاسفة أن القول يكون الإله تعالى موجباً بالذات، فإن هذا البحث ساقط بالكلية. ثم نقول: إن دل هذا الدليل على أنه تعالى لا يفعل القبيح ولا يريده ولا يأمر به. فههنا دلائل تدل على أنه تعالى يفعله ويريده، وهي الدلائل الدالة على أن أفعال العباد كلها واقعة بقضاء الله وقدره. وسيأتي شرح تلك الدلائل على الاستقصاء. والله أعلم.
And to object to it. We say: Do you claim that when knowledge is obtained, the act is ugly, and it is impossible for the act to issue from him, or do you not claim impossibility? If you claim impossibility, then it follows for you that He Most High is a necessitated being (mūjib bi-al-dhāt), not a voluntary agent (fāʿil bi-al-ikhtiyār), because His essence necessitates that knowledge, and that knowledge necessitates, so the impossibility of that act is necessitated, and the necessitator of the necessitator is necessitator, thus His essence is necessitating the impossibility of that act, so He is necessitated by essence, not a voluntary agent. But if you say that when this deterrent is present this impossibility is not necessary, then its meaning is that with the presence of this deterrent it is possible, and with this possibility how can you be certain that it does not occur? And that if it were to occur, it would lead to absurdity. Also: with knowledge that abandoning reward and abandoning compensation (ʿiwaḍ) is ugly, its abandonment is necessary, so it follows that His act is rationally obligatory, and we return to what we mentioned that He would be a necessitated being, not a voluntary agent. We have already mentioned with the philosophers that the statement that the deity is a necessitated being by essence, this discussion is completely invalid. Then we say: If this proof indicates that He Most High does not do evil, nor wills it, nor commands it, then here are proofs indicating that He Most High does it and wills it, and they are the proofs that all actions of servants occur by God's decree and predestination (qaḍāʾ Allāh wa-qadarih). Their detailed explanation will come later. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the Mu'tazilite proof and its counter-argument. The objection first poses a dilemma: either the Mu'tazila claim that God's knowledge makes it impossible for Him to do evil, or they do not. If they claim impossibility, then God becomes a necessitated being (mūjib bi-al-dhāt) because His essence necessitates knowledge, which in turn necessitates the prevention of the act. This leads to the philosophical position that God acts by necessity, not by choice—a view the author finds unacceptable. If they instead say that the deterrent does not necessitate impossibility but merely makes it likely, then they cannot be certain that God never commits evil, and the proof fails to establish absolute prevention. A further point is added: if refraining from evil is necessary due to knowledge, then acts like rewarding and compensating become obligatory, again implying necessitarianism. The author then dismisses the whole discussion as invalid, referencing discussions with philosophers. Finally, the author asserts that there are proofs from Islamic doctrine that God does do evil and wills it, specifically the proofs of predestination (qadar), which will be detailed later. The passage thus defends the Ash'ari position against Mu'tazilite rationalism.

قضاء الله وقدره

Razi's Second Proof Against Rational Good and Evil in God
Razi presents the second proof that rational good and evil cannot be attributed to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that rational concepts of good and evil cannot be applied to God, and why this leads to a contradiction with God's grace.

Core Idea
If God were judged by rational good and evil, He could not be truly gracious.

Razi continues his refutation of rational good and evil with a second, more philosophical argument. He shows that the very concept of grace (niʿma) collapses if we impose rational categories on God. This proof forces us to reconsider what it means for God to be 'gracious' and 'wise'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let Razi's own argument flow before interpreting.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the logical implication (mulazama)
Razi argues that true grace requires the recipient to be in need. But need itself is harmful. So grace would only offset prior harm, making it not genuine beneficence.
Notice the analogy of wounding and healing.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
If rational evil applied, God would be frivolous. But all religions agree He is wise and gracious. Therefore, rational judgment of God is false.
This proof relies on consensus (ijma) of religious traditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : في بيان أن الحسن والقبح بحسب العقل في أفعال الله : إنه لو صح القول بالقبح العقلي ، لم يكن الله منعياً على أحد من عبيده وهذا باطل . بيان الملازمة : أن النعمة إنما تكون نعمة في حق من يكون محتاجاً إليها متهيئاً لها . لأن من لا يكون كذلك لم يكن إيصال ذلك الشيء إليه . نعمة في حقه . إلا أن الحاجة إليه والشهوة به مضرة ، فيثبت أن إيصال النعمة إليه لا يمكن إلا إذا كان مسبوقاً بإيصال ضرر يساويه إليه ، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك صارت هذه النعمة الحاصلة دافعة لذلك الضرر السالف ، فتصير هذه الواقعة كمن يجرح إنساناً ثم يعالج جراحته ، وكمن يغصب من إنسان ديناراً ثم يعطيه ديناراً آخر . وإنه على هذا التقدير لا يكون ذلك إنعاماً ، بل يكون محض العبث ، فيثبت أن القبح العقلي لو كان معتبراً في حق الله تعالى ، لوجب القطع بأنه تعالى غير منعم على أحد من عبيده ، بل كان يلزم أن يكون عابئاً في كل ما فعل وخلق ، ولما أجمع أهل الملل على أنه تعالى حكيم في فعله ، منعم على عبيده ، علمنا أن حكم العقل على الله تعالى بالحسن والقبح باطل . والله أعلم
The second proof: explaining that good and evil according to reason in God's actions: If rational evil were valid, then God would not be gracious to any of His servants, and this is false. Explanation of the implication: That grace is only grace for one who is in need of it and disposed for it. Because one who is not so, conveying that thing to him is not a grace for him. But need for it and desire for it are harmful. So it is established that conveying grace to him is not possible unless it is preceded by conveying an equal harm to him. And when the matter is like this, this attained grace becomes a repeller of that past harm, so this situation becomes like one who wounds a person and then treats his wound, and like one who usurps a dinar from a person and then gives him another dinar. And on this estimation, that is not beneficence, but pure futility. So it is established that if rational evil were considered with respect to God, it would necessitate the certainty that He is not gracious to any of His servants, rather it would necessitate that He is frivolous in everything He does and creates. And since the people of all religions have agreed that He is wise in His action, gracious to His servants, we know that the judgment of reason upon God regarding good and evil is false. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second proof) demonstrating that the concepts of rational good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ al-ʿaqliyyān) cannot be applied to God. The author, Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī, begins by stating the thesis: if rational evil were valid, then God would not be gracious (munʿiman) to any of His servants, which is false (bāṭil). He then explains the logical connection (bayān al-mulāzama): grace (niʿma) is only truly grace for one who is in need of it and disposed for it. If someone does not need or desire something, giving it to them is not a grace. However, need and desire themselves are harmful (ḍārr). Therefore, conveying grace to someone is only possible if it is preceded by conveying an equal harm to them. In that case, the grace merely repels the prior harm, making the situation like wounding someone and then treating the wound, or taking a dinar and then giving another. This is not genuine beneficence (inʿām) but pure futility (ʿabath). Thus, if rational evil applied to God, He would not be gracious at all; rather, He would be frivolous in all His acts. Since all religions agree that God is wise (ḥakīm) and gracious, the judgment of reason upon God based on good and evil must be false.

القبح العقلي
النعمة
الملازمة
العبث
حكيم
منعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that rational good and evil apply universally, including to God.
Correction: Razi shows that applying rational categories to God would negate His grace and wisdom.

Checkpoint

A) God would be unjust.
B) God would not be gracious to any of His servants.
C) God would be forced to create evil.
D) God's actions would be meaningless.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof demonstrates that rational good and evil cannot apply to God because they would undermine the reality of His grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you truly believed that God's actions are beyond your moral categories, would you stop complaining about His decrees? If not, what is holding you back from complete submission?
?
Consider a specific blessing you enjoy. Can you trace it to a prior harm from God that it 'repays'? If not, then your gratitude should be unbounded. Are you that grateful? If not, what will you do about it?

Objection: Are Need and Desire Harmful Creations?
Objection to the second proof with three sub-challenges.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector challenges the proof's premise that grace requires prior need and that need is harmful, and how each sub-objection pushes the argument deeper.

Core Idea
The objection questions whether need and desire are necessarily harmful and divinely created.

In this passage, an objector (fā'il qīla) raises three sequential challenges to the second proof's claim that grace requires prior need and that need is harmful. Each challenge forces a re-examination of the relationship between divine action, human attributes, and the meaning of grace. Understanding these objections is crucial for grasping the full kalam argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the repeated pattern: the objector concedes a point then asks a question.
The passage ends mid-example; focus on the argument's structure.
2
Identify the three sub-objections
The passage contains three distinct challenges: (1) why is need harmful? (2) even if harmful, why is it created by God? (3) even if created, why does that imply futility?
Each challenge begins with 'سلمنا' (we concede) followed by 'لم قلتم' (why did you say).
3
Analyze the first objection
The objector denies the conditional that grace requires need and desire, then asks: why is need harmful? What evidence is there?
This challenges the assumption that need is a harm.
4
Analyze the second objection
The objector concedes harm but questions whether it is created by God. He suggests need might be a necessary attribute of essence, like the evenness of four.
This pushes the discussion into the nature of attributes (lāzim al-dhāt).
5
Analyze the third objection and example
The objector concedes creation but argues that futility only follows if God gives exactly what is needed. If He gives more, it is not futile. The incomplete example illustrates this.
The example is cut off; consider how it would complete the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الشرطية ممنوعة وهي قوله : أن لا تكون النعمة نعمة ، إلا إذا كان المنعم عليه محتاجاً إليها مشتهياً لها ، والحاجة والشهوة ضررهما من فعل الله - سبحانه - فتقول : لا نزاع في أنه ما لم يكن الحيوان محتاجاً إلى الشيء ومشتهياً له ، لم يكن إيصال ذلك الشيء إليه نعمة في حقه . لكن لم قلت : إن الحاجة إلى الشيء والشهوة ضرر ؟ وما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ سلمنا أنه ضرر ، ولكن لم قلتم : إن ذلك الضرر حاصل بتخليق الله تعالى ؟ وما الدليل عليه ؟ وتقريره : أن كون الإنان محتاجاً إلى الشيء وصف لازم لذاته ولماهيته ، ولما كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن حصوله بسبب جعل الجاهل وفعل الفاعل ، كما أن كون الأربعة زوجاً ، وكون الثلاثة فرداً ، ليس بسبب الجمل والخلق والتكوين . سلمنا أن الحاجة إلى الشيء والشهوة له ، إنما حصل بتخليق الله تعالى وإيجاده . فلم قلتم : إن على هذا التقدير يكون إيصال النعمة إليه عبثاً ؟ وتقريره : أن هذا العبث إنما يلزم إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى لا يوصل إليـه النعمة إلا بقدر حاجته وشهوته. أما إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى خلق الحاجة والشهوة فيه . ثم إنه بعد ذلك أعطاه من المنافع ما يزيد على قدر حاجته أضعافـاً مضاعفة ، فهذا يمنع من كونه عبثاً . ومثاله : أن من أوصل ديناراً من الغير إلى
If it is said: The conditional is denied, which is his statement: That grace is not grace unless the one graced is in need of it and desires it, and need and desire—their harm is from God—so we say: There is no dispute that as long as the animal is not in need of something and desiring it, conveying that thing to it is not a grace for him. But why did you say that need for something and desire are harmful? And what is the evidence that the matter is so? We concede that it is harmful, but why did you say that this harm comes about by God's creation? And what is the evidence for it? Its clarification: That a person's being in need of something is a necessary attribute of his essence and quiddity, and since the matter is so, its occurrence is not due to the act of an agent, just as four being even and three being odd is not due to making and creating. We concede that need for something and desire for it only come about by God's creation and bringing into existence. Then why did you say that on this estimation, conveying grace to him is futility? Its clarification: That this futility only follows if we say that He does not convey grace to him except to the extent of his need and desire. But if we say that He created need and desire in him, then afterwards He gave him benefits that exceed his need many times over, this prevents it from being futility. Its example: That one who conveys a dinar from another to...
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an objection and the beginning of an answer to the second proof. The objector (fāʾil qīla) challenges a premise of the proof: the claim that grace requires prior need and that need is harmful. The objector first denies the conditional (al-sharṭiyya mamnūʿa) that grace only applies when the recipient is in need and desires the thing, and that need and desire are harmful actions of God. He then makes several points: (1) while it is agreed that without need and desire the gift is not a grace, why is need harmful? (2) Even if it is harmful, why is that harm created by God? Perhaps need is a necessary attribute of the essence (lāzim li-dhātihi) and not caused, like evenness of four. (3) Even conceding that God creates the need and desire, why does that imply futility? The futility only follows if God gives only enough to satisfy the need; but if He gives many times over what is needed, it is not futile. The objection ends with an incomplete example of a person giving a dinar.

Proportional Compensation for Prior Need
Razi argues that the amount of blessing equals the prior harm or need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the amount of divine blessing exactly equals the prior harm or need, and why this matters for divine justice.

Core Idea
Divine blessing perfectly compensates prior harm.

Razi presents a logical argument that the degree of pleasure or blessing we receive corresponds directly to the intensity of our prior desire or need. This is not merely psychological but theological: it shows that divine rewards are proportionate to prior hardship, ensuring perfect justice. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure of the argument.
Notice that Razi uses a mathematical equality principle in his conclusion.
2
Identify the premises about causation
Find where Razi argues that disparities in desires are caused by God, not nature, and why this is essential for the discussion of good and evil.
He dismisses the necessitarian view as irrelevant to this inquiry.
3
Trace the correlation between desire and pleasure
Razi states that the stronger the desire, the more complete the pleasure upon fulfillment. Mark this step as the link between prior need and present blessing.
This is a psychological principle he treats as evident.
4
Understand the equality chain
Razi concludes that the amount of benefaction equals the amount of need/desire, which is harm. By transitivity, present blessing equals prior harm. See how he uses 'equals of equals' to finish the argument.
The argument is complete when this equality is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا التفاوت أمر معلوم بالضرورة، فاختصاص أحد ذينك الشخصين بتلك الرغبة العظيمة دون الثاني، إما أن يكون بالطبع أو بالخالق المختار. والأول بيناه. لأنا بينا في أول هذا الباب: أن الكلام في الحسن والقبح والحكمة والسفه فرع على القول بإثبات الفاعل المختار. وإما بتقدير أن يكون المؤثر في وجود العالم موجباً بالذات، يسقط هذا البحث بالكلية فيثبت: أن ذلك التفاوت لم يحصل إلا لأجل أن الله سبحانه خص أحدهما بتلك الرغبة والشهوة، وخص الثاني بتلك النفرة والكراهية. وكذا القول في الرغبة في أكل الأطعمة الشهية. فرب إنسان كانت رغبته في طعام معين، وكانت نفرته عن غير ذلك الطعام متأكدة، ورب إنسان بالضد من ذلك، فيثبت: أن هذه الشهوات المختلفة والرغبات المتضادة، إنما حصلت بإيجاد الله تعالى. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: كلما كان الميل إلى الشيء والرغبة فيه والحرص عليه أشد، كان الالتذاذ بوجدانه أتم وأكمل، وكلما كان هذا المعنى أقل، كان الالتذاذ بوجدانه أضعف. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن مقدار الإنعام مساوي لمقدار اللغة، ومقدار اللذة مساوي لمقدار الحاجة السابقة ومقدار الشهوة السابقة. وقد دللنا على أن الحاجة والشهوة ضرر. والمساوي للمساوي للشيء مساوي لذلك الشيء. فيلزم أن يكون مقدار الإنعام الحاضر، مساوياً لمقدار الضرر السابق. وحينئذ يتم الكلام.
This disparity is a matter known by necessity. So the specification of one of those two persons with that great desire without the other is either by nature or by the free Creator. The first we have explained, for we explained at the beginning of this chapter that discourse on good and evil, wisdom and foolishness, is subsidiary to affirming the free agent. As for the assumption that the effecting influence in the existence of the world is necessary by essence, this entire inquiry falls away. Thus it is established that that disparity did not occur except because God—glorified be He—specified one of them with that desire and appetite, and specified the second with that aversion and repulsion. The same holds for the desire to eat palatable foods. A person may have a strong desire for a specific food and a strong aversion to other food, and another person may be the opposite. Thus it is established that these varied appetites and conflicting desires only occurred by God's bringing them into existence. When this is established, we say: the stronger the inclination to a thing, the desire for it, and the eagerness for it, the more perfect and complete is the pleasure upon finding it; and the weaker this meaning, the weaker the pleasure upon finding it. And when this is established, we say: the amount of benefaction equals the amount of [the prior] need; the amount of pleasure equals the amount of the prior need and the amount of the prior desire. And we have demonstrated that need and desire are harm. And what equals what equals a thing equals that thing. So it follows necessarily that the amount of present benefaction equals the amount of the prior harm. And at this point the discourse is complete.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing that the amount of divine blessing equals the amount of prior harm or need. The author first argues that differences in desires and aversions among people are caused by God, the free Creator, not by nature, because the discussion of good and evil presupposes a free agent. If the world's cause were necessary, the whole inquiry would be irrelevant. He then notes that the strength of desire determines the strength of pleasure upon fulfillment. Since desire/need is a harm (something undesirable), and pleasure equals the measure of prior need, it follows that the blessing exactly compensates that prior harm. The argument uses a mathematical principle: if A = B and B = C, then A = C.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
لغة
حاجة
شهوة
لذة
ضرر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that needs are harmful.
Correction: It affirms that the blessing compensates exactly, so no injustice; needs are indeed harmful, but the compensation is proportional.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what determines the amount of pleasure upon finding a desired object?
A) The object's intrinsic worth
B) The strength of the prior desire
C) The giver's generosity
D) The person's moral state

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that divine blessing is exactly proportionate to prior hardship, ensuring perfect justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent intense desire you had that was fulfilled. Can you see now that the pleasure you experienced was exactly proportional to the prior need? How does this realization change the way you thank Allah for that blessing? Are you willing to acknowledge that your craving itself was a form of harm that He compensated?
?
Consider a current struggle or unmet desire. If Razi's argument is true, then the relief or attainment you seek will be exactly equal to your present hardship. Does that help you trust Allah's justice and timing? What practical step can you take today to surrender that desire to Him with full confidence in His compensation?
?
You have desires and aversions that you did not choose—they were assigned by God. Is there any desire or aversion you resent having? Can you accept it as part of Allah's plan for you, knowing that the pleasure of fulfilling it (or the relief from avoiding it) will perfectly match the prior difficulty? What would it look like to embrace your unique set of inclinations as a sign of His personal care?

Razi's Refutation of the Excess Objection
Razi refutes the objection that God could give more than needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that God could give more than needed by distinguishing cases based on whether the recipient desires the excess.

Core Idea
An undesired excess cannot bring pleasure; a desired excess is just another need.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if God causes need, could He not compensate by giving even more than needed? Razi's response is a tight logical dissection that distinguishes between cases where the recipient desires the extra and where they do not. The argument reveals an important principle: pleasure and benefit depend on desire, not just quantity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: why might someone think giving beyond need could be a net favor? The objector argues that despite God causing need, He can give extra to outweigh it.
2
Note the two-case analysis
Razi splits the problem into two possibilities: does the recipient have a desire (shahwa) for the excess, or not?
This is a classic logical division.
3
Follow the first case (desire present)
If the recipient desires the excess, then that excess is also a need. So the total favor equals the total harm (including the harm of the original need plus the new need for the excess). Thus the favor is not actually extra.
The 'favor' is exactly equal to the harm.
4
Follow the second case (no desire)
If there is no desire for the excess, then it cannot produce pleasure or benefit. Therefore, the excess is useless.
Pleasure comes from fulfilling desire.
5
Grasp the conclusion
In either case, giving beyond need does not result in a net favor greater than the harm. The objection fails.
The argument is complete and tight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى، وإن كان هو الذي أحوج، إلا أنه أعطى فوق قدر الحاجة؟ فالجواب: أن هذا باطل. لأن ذلك القدر الزائد، هل إليه شهوة وهل له في حصوله رغبة أو ليس كذلك؟ فإن كان الأول فحينئذ قد كان المنعم عليه محتاجاً إلى حصول تلك الزيادة كما كان محتاجاً إلى حصول ذلك الأصل، ولما كان محتاجاً إلى الأصل وإلى الزيادة معاً، فحينئذ يعود الأمر إلى أن النعمة إنما حصلت بقدر المضرة السالفة. وإن كان الثاني وهو أن لا يكون للمنعم عليه حاجة إلى وجدان تلك الزيادة، ولا يكون له شهوة إلى حصول تلك الزيادة، فنقول: الشيء الذي لا يكون محتاجاً إليه، ولا يكون بتعلق الشهوة والرغبة، امتنع أن يكون وجدانه سبباً لحصول اللذة والمنفعة.
As for his statement: 'Why is it not permissible to say that He—exalted is He—even though He is the One Who made [them] needy, yet He gave beyond the measure of need?' The answer: this is false. Because that excess amount—is there an appetite for it and a desire for its attainment, or not? If it is the first, then the one upon whom the favor is bestowed was in need of the attainment of that excess just as he was in need of the attainment of that original [need], and since he was in need of the original and of the excess together, then the matter returns to the fact that the favor only occurred in the measure of the prior harm. And if it is the second, i.e., that the one upon whom the favor is bestowed has no need for finding that excess, and has no appetite for the attainment of that excess, then we say: a thing that is not needed, and with which no appetite or desire is connected, it is impossible that its finding be a cause for the attainment of pleasure and benefit.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The objector suggests that God, though causing need, could give more than needed, making it a net blessing. The author refutes by dividing into two possibilities. First, if the recipient has a desire for the extra amount, then that extra is also a need, and the total favor equals the total previous harm (including the extra need). Second, if there is no desire for the extra, then it cannot produce pleasure or benefit, because benefit comes from fulfilling desire. Thus, giving beyond need is either not really extra (because it corresponds to an additional need) or it is useless. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms
أحوج
فوق قدر الحاجة
شهوة
رغبة
لذة
منفعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author denies that God has the power to give more than needed.
Correction: The author does not deny God's power; he argues that such excess would either be needed (thus not truly excess) or produce no pleasure.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response if the recipient has no desire for the extra amount?
A) It still gives pleasure because it is from God
B) It cannot cause pleasure or benefit
C) The recipient will develop desire later
D) It is a trial for the recipient

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that a favor beyond need is either not truly beyond need (if desired) or does not produce pleasure (if not desired), so the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often ask God for more—more wealth, more health, more comfort. But do you truly desire those things, or have you just assumed they are blessings? What if some of what you receive is actually unwanted excess that yields no spiritual benefit? How will you examine your own desires to distinguish genuine needs from mere accumulation?
?
Razi argues that benefit comes only from fulfilling a real desire. Think of a time you received something you thought you wanted, but it brought no lasting satisfaction. What does that reveal about your own understanding of what is truly beneficial? Will you dare to ask Allah to give you only what is best for you, even if it means not getting what you impulsively crave?
?
The objection assumes that giving more is always good, but Razi shows it can be pointless. How often do you measure God's favor by quantity rather than by genuine need? If you were to honestly assess your life, would you say that what you have is exactly what you need—or are you clinging to excesses that do not bring you closer to Him? Commit to listing three things you possess that you do not truly need, and reflect on whether they have increased your gratitude or your heedlessness.

The First Argument: Faith Would Change God's Knowledge
Al-Razi's first reductio argument about foreknowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's first argument that if faith occurred despite God's foreknowledge of disbelief, it would imply God's knowledge becoming ignorance, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If faith occurs against God's knowledge, God's knowledge would become ignorance.

Al-Razi begins his demolition of the Mu'tazilite position on taklif with a simple logical argument. If God knows that someone will never believe, yet commands them to believe, then if they do believe, God's knowledge would no longer match reality—it would become ignorance. This is impossible, so the command is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if he believes... then...'.
The passage is very concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Razi argues that the occurrence of faith would necessitate a change in God's knowledge from true to false, which is impossible. Understand why this follows from the nature of knowledge and command.
God's knowledge is eternal and immutable; any change would be impossible.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
If the consequence (changing God's knowledge) is impossible, then the command that would lead to it is also impossible. This is a straightforward reductio ad absurdum.
This argument sets the foundation for the more complex faces that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يؤمن ، كان صدور الإيمان منه : يستلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلا ، ومستلزم المحال محال ، فالتكليف تكليف بالمحال وقد تذكر هذا على وجه ثالث وهو أن جود الإيمان يستحيل أن يحصل مع العلم يعدم الإيمان ، لأنه إنما يكون علماً لو كان مطابقاً للمعلوم ، والعلم بعدم الإيمان إنما يكون مطابقاً ، لو حصل عدم الإيمان . فلو وجد الإيمان مع العلم بعدم الإيمان لزم الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال . فالأمر بتحصيل الإيمان مع حصول العلم بعدم الإيمان : أمر بالجمع بين الضدين ، بل أمر بالجمع بين العدم والوجود ، وكل ذلك محال ، ونذكر هذا على وجه رابع وهو : أنه تعالى كلف هؤلاء الذين أخبر عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة بالإيمان، والإيمان معتبر فيه تصديق الله تعالى في كل ما أخبر عنه ومما أخبر به أنهم لا يؤمنون قط . فقد صاروا مكلفين بأن يؤمنوا ، وبأنهم لا يؤمنون قط، وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين النفي والإثبات . ونذكر هذا على وجه خامس وهو أنه تعالى عاب الكفار على أنهم حاولوا فعل شيء على خلاف ما أخبر الله عنه فقال : ( يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله قل : لن تتبعونا . كذلكم قال الله من قبل ) فيثبت : أن القصد إلى تكوين ما أخبر الله عن عدم تكوينه : قصد لتبديل كلام الله ، وذلك مما قد نهى الله عنه في هذه الآية . ثم إن في سائر الآيات أخير الله تعالى عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة ، فالقصد إلى تحصيل الإيمان ، يكون قصداً إلى تبديل كلام الله ، وذلك منهي عنه . ثم إنه أمر الكل بتحصيل الإيمان فكان هذا جمعاً بين الأمر والنهي بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد ، وذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق . واعلم أن هذا الكلام هو الكلام الهادم لأصول الاعتزال. ولقد تكلفت لهم كلمات كثيرة ، وأنا أذكرها مهنا على سبيل الاستقصاء.
If he believes, then the occurrence of faith from him would necessitate the change of God's knowledge into ignorance, and what necessitates the impossible is itself impossible. So the obligation is an obligation of the impossible. This has been mentioned in a third way: that the existence of faith is impossible to occur together with the knowledge of the absence of faith, because it would only be knowledge if it corresponded to the known, and the knowledge of the absence of faith would only correspond if the absence of faith occurred. So if faith existed together with the knowledge of the absence of faith, the conjunction of two contradictories would follow, which is impossible. So commanding the acquisition of faith together with the existence of knowledge of the absence of faith is a command to combine two opposites, indeed a command to combine non-existence and existence, and all that is impossible. We mention this in a fourth way: that He, exalted, obligated those about whom He informed that they will never believe to believe, and faith requires believing God in all that He informed about, and from what He informed is that they will never believe. So they become obligated to believe and obligated to never believe, and this is an obligation combining negation and affirmation. We mention this in a fifth way: that He, exalted, blamed the disbelievers for attempting to do something contrary to what God informed about, saying: 'They wish to change the words of God. Say: You will not follow us. Thus God has said before.' So it is established that intending to bring about what God has informed about its non-occurrence is an intention to change God's words, and that is what God has forbidden in this verse. Then in other verses God informed about them that they will never believe, so intending to attain faith becomes an intention to change God's words, and that is prohibited. Then He commanded everyone to attain faith, so this is a combination of command and prohibition regarding one thing, and that is an impossible obligation. Know that this discourse is the discourse that demolishes the foundations of Mu'tazilism. I have exerted myself to provide many words for them, and I mention them here exhaustively.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'arite (al-Razi's) argument that the Mu'tazilite position on taklif (obligation) is impossible if God has foreknowledge that certain people will never believe. It gives five formulations (wujuh) of the same essential argument. First, it argues that if faith occurs despite God's knowledge of its non-occurrence, that would turn God's knowledge into ignorance, which is impossible. Second (implied by the flow), it notes that knowledge must match reality; if God knows non-faith, then faith cannot occur without contradiction. Third, it explicitly states that the occurrence of faith with knowledge of non-faith would unite two contradictories. Fourth, it points out that God informed that some will never believe, so commanding them to believe requires them to both believe and not believe simultaneously—a contradiction. Fifth, it uses the Quranic verse about those who wish to change God's words: trying to achieve faith when God has said they won't is attempting to change God's words, which is forbidden. Furthermore, God commands all to believe, but has also informed that some will not, so this imposes a command and a prohibition on the same act—an impossible obligation. Al-Razi concludes that this argument destroys the foundations of Mu'tazilism, and he has prepared extensive responses to their objections.

Key Terms
التكليف بالمحال
علم الله
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what would the occurrence of faith (despite God's knowledge of non-faith) necessitate?
A) The increase of God's knowledge
B) The change of God's knowledge into ignorance
C) The affirmation of human free will
D) The nullification of the command

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that if faith occurs despite God's foreknowledge, God's knowledge would become ignorance, which is impossible, so the command is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your own spiritual laziness by telling yourself that if God had wanted you to be more faithful, He would have created you that way? How does al-Razi’s argument strip away that excuse? Write down one specific area where you will stop hiding behind divine decree and take responsibility tonight.
?
Imagine you are standing before God and He says: 'You knew My knowledge was perfect, yet you acted as if My knowledge could be overruled.' Does your daily life treat God’s knowledge as if it were negotiable? What would it look like to live with the awe that God already knows—and still holds you accountable? Commit to one action tomorrow that reflects this truth.
?
Al-Razi argues that commanding the impossible is absurd. Are there any areas in your life where you claim to obey God but secretly believe His commands are too difficult or unreasonable? If so, what does that say about your view of God's wisdom? Repent of one such thought right now and replace it with trust.

The Third and Fourth Arguments: Combining Contradictories
Third and fourth faces showing contradictions in taklif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's third and fourth arguments showing that commanding belief in light of foreknowledge entails combining contradictories (existence and non-existence, affirmation and negation).

Core Idea
Commanding belief with foreknowledge of disbelief forces a contradiction.

Al-Razi presents two more refined formulations of the same problem. The third argument shows that the existence of faith cannot coexist with God's knowledge of its non-existence, as that would require a logical contradiction. The fourth argument highlights that God's own information about future disbelief makes the command to believe a command to both affirm and negate that information—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two arguments
The passage is divided into two clear parts: one introduced as 'third way' and one as 'fourth way'. Read each separately and note their distinct formulations.
The third argument is about the impossibility of faith existing with God's knowledge of its absence.
2
Analyze the third argument's logic
The third argument uses the correspondence theory of knowledge: knowledge is only knowledge if it matches reality. If God knows non-faith, then for that knowledge to be true, non-faith must occur. If faith occurs, God's knowledge would be false, and that would be a contradiction (al-jam' bayn al-naqiḍayn).
This is more subtle than the first argument; it focuses on the definition of knowledge.
3
Understand the fourth argument's novelty
The fourth argument adds the element of God's own information (khabar). Since God has informed that some will never believe, faith requires believing that information. But if one believes, they must also believe God's word that they will never believe, which is a contradiction. They are commanded to both affirm and negate the same thing.
This argument ties the logical impossibility to the nature of faith as tasdiq (affirmation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يؤمن ، كان صدور الإيمان منه : يستلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلا ، ومستلزم المحال محال ، فالتكليف تكليف بالمحال وقد تذكر هذا على وجه ثالث وهو أن جود الإيمان يستحيل أن يحصل مع العلم يعدم الإيمان ، لأنه إنما يكون علماً لو كان مطابقاً للمعلوم ، والعلم بعدم الإيمان إنما يكون مطابقاً ، لو حصل عدم الإيمان . فلو وجد الإيمان مع العلم بعدم الإيمان لزم الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال . فالأمر بتحصيل الإيمان مع حصول العلم بعدم الإيمان : أمر بالجمع بين الضدين ، بل أمر بالجمع بين العدم والوجود ، وكل ذلك محال ، ونذكر هذا على وجه رابع وهو : أنه تعالى كلف هؤلاء الذين أخبر عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة بالإيمان، والإيمان معتبر فيه تصديق الله تعالى في كل ما أخبر عنه ومما أخبر به أنهم لا يؤمنون قط . فقد صاروا مكلفين بأن يؤمنوا ، وبأنهم لا يؤمنون قط، وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين النفي والإثبات . ونذكر هذا على وجه خامس وهو أنه تعالى عاب الكفار على أنهم حاولوا فعل شيء على خلاف ما أخبر الله عنه فقال : ( يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله قل : لن تتبعونا . كذلكم قال الله من قبل ) فيثبت : أن القصد إلى تكوين ما أخبر الله عن عدم تكوينه : قصد لتبديل كلام الله ، وذلك مما قد نهى الله عنه في هذه الآية . ثم إن في سائر الآيات أخير الله تعالى عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة ، فالقصد إلى تحصيل الإيمان ، يكون قصداً إلى تبديل كلام الله ، وذلك منهي عنه . ثم إنه أمر الكل بتحصيل الإيمان فكان هذا جمعاً بين الأمر والنهي بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد ، وذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق . واعلم أن هذا الكلام هو الكلام الهادم لأصول الاعتزال. ولقد تكلفت لهم كلمات كثيرة ، وأنا أذكرها مهنا على سبيل الاستقصاء.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This lesson exposes a logical impossibility in combining divine foreknowledge and human obligation. But what about your own inner contradictions? Are there commands you know you should obey, yet you live as if you have 'knowledge' that you will fail? How does the impossibility in the lesson mirror the impossibility of living in denial of Allah's commands?

The Fifth Argument: Quranic Evidence and Conclusion
Fifth face: Quranic argument and final demolition of Mu'tazilism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's fifth argument from Quranic verses about changing God's words, and his conclusion that this demolishes Mu'tazilism.

Core Idea
Trying to achieve what God said will not happen is trying to change God's words.

Al-Razi strengthens his case by invoking a Quranic verse where disbelievers are blamed for trying to change God's words. He argues that commanding faith in those whom God has declared will never believe would be commanding an attempt to change God's words, which is forbidden. Thus, this is an impossible obligation. He concludes that this argument destroys the foundations of Mu'tazilism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and locate the Quranic verse
The passage quotes part of a verse from the Quran (Surah al-Fath? Actually it says 'يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله قل: لن تتبعونا. كذلكم قال الله من قبل'). Identify the verse and understand its original context.
The verse is about the Bedouins who wished to follow the Prophet but were told they would not.
2
Understand the argument from 'changing God's words'
Al-Razi argues that if God has informed that someone will never believe, then trying to make them believe is an attempt to change what God has said. Since changing God's words is forbidden, the command to achieve faith is a command to do something forbidden, which is impossible.
This adds a moral dimension: the command conflicts with another divine prohibition.
3
Grasp the final point about combining command and prohibition
Al-Razi notes that God has both commanded all to believe and informed that some will not believe. Thus, for those individuals, the command to believe coexists with the prohibition against changing God's words, resulting in a command and prohibition on the same act—an impossible obligation.
This final point ties together the logical and moral contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يؤمن ، كان صدور الإيمان منه : يستلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلا ، ومستلزم المحال محال ، فالتكليف تكليف بالمحال وقد تذكر هذا على وجه ثالث وهو أن جود الإيمان يستحيل أن يحصل مع العلم يعدم الإيمان ، لأنه إنما يكون علماً لو كان مطابقاً للمعلوم ، والعلم بعدم الإيمان إنما يكون مطابقاً ، لو حصل عدم الإيمان . فلو وجد الإيمان مع العلم بعدم الإيمان لزم الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال . فالأمر بتحصيل الإيمان مع حصول العلم بعدم الإيمان : أمر بالجمع بين الضدين ، بل أمر بالجمع بين العدم والوجود ، وكل ذلك محال ، ونذكر هذا على وجه رابع وهو : أنه تعالى كلف هؤلاء الذين أخبر عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة بالإيمان، والإيمان معتبر فيه تصديق الله تعالى في كل ما أخبر عنه ومما أخبر به أنهم لا يؤمنون قط . فقد صاروا مكلفين بأن يؤمنوا ، وبأنهم لا يؤمنون قط، وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين النفي والإثبات . ونذكر هذا على وجه خامس وهو أنه تعالى عاب الكفار على أنهم حاولوا فعل شيء على خلاف ما أخبر الله عنه فقال : ( يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله قل : لن تتبعونا . كذلكم قال الله من قبل ) فيثبت : أن القصد إلى تكوين ما أخبر الله عن عدم تكوينه : قصد لتبديل كلام الله ، وذلك مما قد نهى الله عنه في هذه الآية . ثم إن في سائر الآيات أخير الله تعالى عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة ، فالقصد إلى تحصيل الإيمان ، يكون قصداً إلى تبديل كلام الله ، وذلك منهي عنه . ثم إنه أمر الكل بتحصيل الإيمان فكان هذا جمعاً بين الأمر والنهي بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد ، وذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق . واعلم أن هذا الكلام هو الكلام الهادم لأصول الاعتزال. ولقد تكلفت لهم كلمات كثيرة ، وأنا أذكرها مهنا على سبيل الاستقصاء.
If he believes, then the occurrence of faith from him would necessitate the change of God's knowledge into ignorance, and what necessitates the impossible is itself impossible. So the obligation is an obligation of the impossible. This has been mentioned in a third way: that the existence of faith is impossible to occur together with the knowledge of the absence of faith, because it would only be knowledge if it corresponded to the known, and the knowledge of the absence of faith would only correspond if the absence of faith occurred. So if faith existed together with the knowledge of the absence of faith, the conjunction of two contradictories would follow, which is impossible. So commanding the acquisition of faith together with the existence of knowledge of the absence of faith is a command to combine two opposites, indeed a command to combine non-existence and existence, and all that is impossible. We mention this in a fourth way: that He, exalted, obligated those about whom He informed that they will never believe to believe, and faith requires believing God in all that He informed about, and from what He informed is that they will never believe. So they become obligated to believe and obligated to never believe, and this is an obligation combining negation and affirmation. We mention this in a fifth way: that He, exalted, blamed the disbelievers for attempting to do something contrary to what God informed about, saying: 'They wish to change the words of God. Say: You will not follow us. Thus God has said before.' So it is established that intending to bring about what God has informed about its non-occurrence is an intention to change God's words, and that is what God has forbidden in this verse. Then in other verses God informed about them that they will never believe, so intending to attain faith becomes an intention to change God's words, and that is prohibited. Then He commanded everyone to attain faith, so this is a combination of command and prohibition regarding one thing, and that is an impossible obligation. Know that this discourse is the discourse that demolishes the foundations of Mu'tazilism. I have exerted myself to provide many words for them, and I mention them here exhaustively.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'arite (al-Razi's) argument that the Mu'tazilite position on taklif (obligation) is impossible if God has foreknowledge that certain people will never believe. It gives five formulations (wujuh) of the same essential argument. First, it argues that if faith occurs despite God's knowledge of its non-occurrence, that would turn God's knowledge into ignorance, which is impossible. Second (implied by the flow), it notes that knowledge must match reality; if God knows non-faith, then faith cannot occur without contradiction. Third, it explicitly states that the occurrence of faith with knowledge of non-faith would unite two contradictories. Fourth, it points out that God informed that some will never believe, so commanding them to believe requires them to both believe and not believe simultaneously—a contradiction. Fifth, it uses the Quranic verse about those who wish to change God's words: trying to achieve faith when God has said they won't is attempting to change God's words, which is forbidden. Furthermore, God commands all to believe, but has also informed that some will not, so this imposes a command and a prohibition on the same act—an impossible obligation. Al-Razi concludes that this argument destroys the foundations of Mu'tazilism, and he has prepared extensive responses to their objections.

Key Terms
تبديل كلام الله
الجمع بين الأمر والنهي
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that al-Razi's argument denies human free will and makes God unjust
Correction: Al-Razi's argument is not about free will or justice; it is about logical coherence. He does not deny that God can do whatever He wills; rather, he shows that the Mu'tazilite position leads to contradictions.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth argument conclude about the combination of the command to believe and the prohibition against changing God's words?
A) It is a test of faith
B) It is an impossible obligation (taklif ma la yutaq)
C) It shows God's mercy
D) It is a recommendation, not a command

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument uses the Quranic prohibition against changing God's words to show that commanding belief in those who will never believe combines command and prohibition, making it impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever wished that God's decree would be different for someone you love—that they would be guided despite evidence otherwise? How does this passage challenge the root of that desire? Will you submit to God's words even when your heart aches?
?
What does this argument reveal about your own assumption that everyone can be guided if they just try hard enough? Do you genuinely trust God's sovereignty over faith, or do you secretly believe human effort can override His decree?
?
If God's words about some people never believing are unchangeable, what does that mean for your own accountability? Are you presuming that you control your faith, or are you relying entirely on His mercy? How will this passage change the way you pray for guidance?
